Proofs Against Adding the Attribute of Perception
Commentator refutes perception as an extra attribute with three rational proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational arguments used to reject perception as an additional attribute of Allah.

Core Idea
Perception entails physical connection; since that is impossible for Allah, perception cannot be His attribute.

The commentator presents a concise case against considering perception (al-idrāk) as an attribute distinct from knowledge, hearing, and sight. The argument rests on three independent but complementary rational proofs: the entailment of physical connection, the sufficiency of divine knowledge, and the invalidity of the opposite claim. This passage sharpens our understanding of how kalam reasoning applies logical entailment to divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow. Then read the translation to ensure you understand the literal meaning.
The passage uses technical terms like al-lāzim and al-malzūm.
2
Identify the three arguments
The passage contains three distinct proofs against adding perception. Find each one and note how they are connected.
Look for the keywords: 'li-mā anna' (first), 'wa-li-anna' (second), and the refutation beginning with 'wa-daʿwā' (third).
3
Analyze the entailment argument
Focus on the first argument: perception and connection are rationally inseparable. Since connection is impossible for Allah, perception must also be impossible.
This uses the principle that impossibility of the necessary (lāzim) implies impossibility of the necessitating (malzūm).
4
Understand the sufficiency argument
The second argument: Allah's knowledge already encompasses all objects of perception, so no separate attribute is needed, especially without revelatory or evidentiary support.
This is a 'principle of parsimony' argument within kalam.
5
Evaluate the refutation of opposites
The third argument refutes the opponent's claim that without perception Allah would have its opposites. Knowledge itself precludes those opposites.
The opponent's false dichotomy is dismantled by appealing to the attribute of knowledge that Allah necessarily possesses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلاف في زيادة صفة الإدراك جمع ؛ لِمَا أنَّ بينهما وبين الاتصال بمتعلقاتها تلازما عقليًّا(۱)؛ فلا يُتصوّر انفكاكها عنه (۲) ، والاتصال مستحيل عليه تعالى، واستحالة اللازم توجب استحالة الملزوم (۳) ، ولأنَّ إحاطة العِلم بمتعلقاتها(٤) كافية عن إثباتها حيثُ لم يَرِدْ بها سَمْعُ (٥) ولا دلَّ عليها فعله تعالى ، ودعوى أنه تعالى لو لم يتصف بها اتّصفَ بأضدادها فاسدة؛ لمُنافاة العِلم لتلك الأضداد(٦)، وقد وجب اتصافه تعالى به .
The disagreement concerning the addition of the attribute of perception. He combined [the arguments] because between them and connection to their objects there is a rational entailment; so its separation from it is inconceivable, and connection is impossible for Him, Most High, and the impossibility of the necessary (al-lāzim) entails the impossibility of the necessitating (al-malzūm). And because the encompassing of knowledge (al-ʿilm) of its objects is sufficient for proving it, since no revealed text (samʿ) has come concerning it nor does His act indicate it. And the claim that if He, Most High, were not described with them, He would be described with their opposites is false, because knowledge precludes those opposites, and He is necessarily described with it.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage presents three rational arguments against establishing perception (al-idrāk) as an extra attribute beyond knowledge, hearing, and sight. First, there is a rational entailment (talāzum ʿaqliyy) between perception and physical connection to its objects (e.g. touch, smell). Since connection is impossible for God, the attribute which necessitates it must also be impossible. Second, God's knowledge already encompasses all objects of perception, so no additional attribute is needed—especially since no revelation or divine act indicates one. Third, the opponent's claim that without perception God would have its opposites (like ignorance) is invalid, because God's knowledge already precludes those opposites. The passage concludes that God is necessarily described with knowledge (or with the attributes known from revelation), not with a separate perception attribute.

Key Terms
الإدراك (perception)
الاتصال (connection)
اللازم (the necessary)
الملزوم (the necessitating)
العلم (knowledge)
السمع (revealed text/hearing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of connection directly disproves perception as an attribute.
Correction: The argument relies on the rational entailment between perception and connection; if the entailment is denied, the argument fails. The commentator assumes the entailment is self-evident.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument given against adding perception as an attribute?
A) Knowledge suffices for all objects of perception.
B) There is a rational entailment between perception and physical connection, which is impossible for God.
C) No scriptural evidence supports perception as an attribute.
D) The claim of opposites is false.

Key Takeaway
Perception cannot be an attribute of Allah because it entails physical connection, is redundant given His all-encompassing knowledge, and the claim of opposites is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself imagining Allah in a physical or sensory way, as if He 'sees' or 'hears' like a human? How does this lesson challenge that tendency, and what will you do to purify your conception of Him starting today?
?
Is your trust in Allah's knowledge truly complete, or do you sometimes feel that He needs to 'perceive' your struggles directly for them to be real? How can you strengthen your reliance on His all-encompassing knowledge instead of demanding a more tangible sense of His awareness?
?
When you read arguments like this that deny an attribute, do you feel a resistance because it seems to diminish Allah? Examine your heart: are you attached to a comforting anthropomorphic image rather than the transcendent reality? What concrete step will you take to surrender your limited imagination to the teachings of the salaf?

The Suspension Position on the Attribute of Perception
The commentator explains the suspension position due to conflicting evidence and differing proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some theologians adopt suspension (waqf) regarding the attribute of perception, and how conflicting evidence leads to this reasoned neutrality.

Core Idea
When rational and scriptural proofs conflict, suspension is a reasoned theological stance.

This passage discusses the attribute of perception (al-idrāk) and why some theologians refrain from affirming or denying it. It traces the disagreement back to a methodological difference in proving attributes. By reading this, you'll grasp the reasoning behind the suspension position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation without analyzing too deeply. Let the author's structure come through.
Note the key terms: الوقف, الخلف, الدليل العقلي, الدليل السمعي.
2
Identify the two opposing positions
The passage describes two views: those who affirm perception (based on rational proof) and those who deny it (based on scriptural proof). Identify the reasoning behind each.
Also notice that the disagreement stems from the earlier three attributes.
3
Understand why suspension is valid
The commentator explains that suspension is valid because the evidence for and against is conflicting. Neither side can be decisively proven.
Consider why both extremes are avoided: no scriptural proof for affirmation, and the denial relies on a weak Literalist view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات
[ مذهبُ الوَقْف في صفة الإدْرَاكَ واختيارُ الشَّارِح لَه ]
في جوابَ ذلك (خُلْفٌ) أي: اختِلافٌ مَبني على الاختلاف في دليل إثبات الصفات الثلاث السَّابقة ، فمَن أثبتها بالدليل العقلي أثبته (۱) ، ومَن أثبتها بالدليل السمعي نفاه .
(وَعِنْدَ قَوْمٍ (۲) صَحَّ فِيه الوَقْفُ) فاعلُ «صح»، و«عند»: متعلق بصح، وضمير «فيه» يعود على الإدراك ، وتقدير المتن: وصح الوقف ـ أي التَّوقف عن ترجيح إثباتِ الإدراك ونفيه ، وعدم الجزم (۳) بأحدهما (٤) عند قوم من المتكلمين ؛ لتعارض الأدلَّة ، فلا يجزم (٥) بثبوت الإدراك له تعالى زيادة على العلم كأهل القول الأوَّل ؛ لأنَّ المُعتمد في إثبات الصفات التي لا يتوقف عليها الفعل إنَّما هو الدليل السمعي (٦) ، ولم يرد بإثبات صفة الإدراك له تعالى سَمْع ، ولا يجزم بنفيها كأهل القول الثاني ؛ لأنَّه إِنَّما يتمشى على قول بعض الظاهريَّة أنَّه تعالى لا صِفةَ له وراء الصَّفات السَّبع المذكورة (٧) ، وهذا القول
The Section on Divine Matters (Ilāhiyyāt) — The position of suspension (al-waqf) regarding the attribute of perception and the commentator's choice thereof. In response to that [argument], there is a difference (khulf) i.e., a disagreement based on the disagreement concerning the proof for establishing the three previous attributes. Whoever established them by rational proof affirms it [perception], and whoever established them by scriptural proof (dalīl samʿī) denies it. And according to some people, suspension (al-waqf) concerning it is valid. The doer of 'ṣaḥḥa' is 'al-waqf', and 'ʿinda' is related to it, and the pronoun 'fīhi' refers to perception. The meaning of the text: Suspension is valid — i.e., refraining from preferring affirmation or negation of perception, and not being certain (jazm) of either, according to some theologians, due to the conflict of evidence. So one is not certain of affirming perception for Him as an addition to knowledge, like the people of the first opinion, because what is relied upon in establishing attributes that do not depend on action is only scriptural proof, and no revealed text has come to affirm the attribute of perception for Him; nor is one certain of denying it like the people of the second opinion, because it [denial] only proceeds on the saying of some Literalists (al-Ẓāhiriyya) that He, Most High, has no attribute beyond the seven mentioned attributes — and this saying [is not accepted].
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage discusses the suspension (al-waqf) position regarding the attribute of perception. It begins by noting that the disagreement (khulf) about perception stems from a prior disagreement about how the three previous attributes (speech, hearing, sight) were proven. Those who proved them by rational proof (e.g., analogy from human perfections) affirm perception as well; those who proved them solely by scriptural proof deny it. The commentator then explains that some theologians hold that suspension is valid: one should neither affirm nor deny perception definitively, because the evidence is conflicting. On one hand, one cannot affirm it as an addition to knowledge because the reliable method for attributes not entailed by action is scriptural evidence, and no such evidence exists. On the other hand, one cannot deny it because denying it relies on a weak opinion of some Literalists who restrict God's attributes to just seven, a view the mainstream does not accept. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is that suspension is the safest position given the conflicting indications.

Key Terms
الوقف (suspension)
الخلف (disagreement)
الدليل العقلي (rational proof)
الدليل السمعي (scriptural proof)
الظاهرية (Literalists)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Suspension (waqf) implies ignorance or indecisiveness.
Correction: Suspension is a reasoned theological stance based on the conflict of evidence. It is not a failure to decide but a recognition that neither side has decisive proof.

Checkpoint
Why does the suspension position refrain from affirming perception as an additional attribute of God?
A) Because rational proof definitively proves it is not an attribute.
B) Because scriptural proof for perception is absent, and attributes not dependent on action are only established by scriptural evidence.
C) Because all theologians agree perception is not an attribute.
D) Because perception is included in the attribute of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Suspension is a legitimate theological position when rational and scriptural proofs are inconclusive, avoiding unwarranted affirmation or denial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a matter of faith where evidence seems conflicting, do you rush to a conclusion to feel certain, or do you patiently suspend judgment out of reverence for Allah's transcendence? How can adopting a stance of 'waqf' protect you from speaking about Allah without knowledge?
?
Consider the attribute of perception. Do you find it easier to affirm something about Allah based on rational speculation or to remain silent where revelation is silent? Which path demonstrates greater taqwa? What would it look like for you to practice 'waqf' in your own beliefs?

The Doctrine of Waqf on Perception
Sanusi discusses the dispute over perception and the waqf position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the doctrine of waqf (suspension) regarding the attribute of perception and why some theologians adopt it over outright affirmation or negation.

Core Idea
Suspending judgment on perception is safer than affirming or denying without clear evidence.

Sanusi presents a theological dispute about whether God possesses an attribute of perception beyond knowledge. He explains that a group of theologians adopt a position of suspending judgment (waqf) due to conflicting proofs. This lesson examines that position and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: dispute, two opposing views, then the waqf position.
The passage ends with the commentator's endorsement of waqf as safer.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Identify the view that affirms perception based on rational proof and the view that negates it based on scriptural proof. Understand their foundations.
3
Understand the waqf position
See why some theologians suspend judgment: conflicting proofs and lack of clear scriptural evidence for perception. Note that waqf means refraining from certainty either way.
The passage emphasizes that scriptural proof is the basis for attributes not tied to action.
4
Note the commentator's preference
Sanusi states that the waqf position is 'safer and more correct' than the other two. Reflect on why he considers this the best stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ مذهبُ الوَقْف في صفة الإدْرَاكَ واختيارُ الشَّارِح لَه ] في جوابَ ذلك (خُلْفٌ) أي: اختِلافٌ مَبني على الاختلاف في دليل إثبات الصفات الثلاث السَّابقة ، فمَن أثبتها بالدليل العقلي أثبته (١) ، ومَن أثبتها بالدليل السمعي نفاه . (وَعِنْدَ قَوْمٍ صَحَّ فِيه الوَقْفُ) فاعلُ «صح»، و«عند»: متعلق بصح، وضمير «فيه» يعود على الإدراك ، وتقدير المتن: وصح الوقف ـ أي التَّوقف عن ترجيح إثباتِ الإدراك ونفيه ، وعدم الجزم بأحدهما عند قوم من المتكلمين ؛ لتعارض الأدلَّة ، فلا يجزم بثبوت الإدراك له تعالى زيادة على العلم كأهل القول الأوَّل ؛ لأنَّ المُعتمد في إثبات الصفات التي لا يتوقف عليها الفعل إنَّما هو الدليل السمعي ، ولم يرد بإثبات صفة الإدراك له تعالى سَمْع ، ولا يجزم بنفيها كأهل القول الثاني ؛ لأنَّه إِنَّما يتمشى على قول بعض الظاهريَّة أنَّه تعالى لا صِفةَ له وراء الصَّفات السَّبع المذكورة ، وهذا القول أسلم وأصحُ مِن الأَوَّلَيْنِ
[The doctrine of suspension (waqf) regarding the attribute of perception and the commentator's choice for it] In response to that, (there is a dispute) i.e., a difference based on the difference in the proof for establishing the three previous attributes. So whoever established them by intellectual proof established it (perception), and whoever established them by scriptural proof negated it. (And with some people the suspension is valid therein) The subject of 'is valid', and 'with' is related to 'is valid', and the pronoun 'therein' refers to perception. And the estimation of the text: and the suspension—i.e., refraining from preferring the establishment of perception and its negation, and not being certain of either—is valid with some theologians due to the conflicting proofs. So one is not certain of the existence of perception for Him, exalted, in addition to knowledge like the people of the first opinion, because the relied-upon in establishing attributes that do not depend on action is only scriptural proof, and no scriptural evidence has come for establishing the attribute of perception for Him, exalted. And one is not certain of its negation like the people of the second opinion, because it is only consistent with the saying of some literalists (Ẓāhirīs) that He, exalted, has no attribute beyond the seven mentioned attributes. And this opinion is safer and more correct than the first two.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of suspending judgment (waqf) regarding the attribute of perception (idrāk). It begins by stating that the dispute is based on how one proves the three previous attributes (speech, hearing, sight). Those who prove these attributes through rational proof (al-dalīl al-ʿaqlī) also prove perception, while those who rely on scriptural proof (al-dalīl al-samʿī) negate it. The commentator then explains the position of a group who hold that suspension is valid: due to conflicting evidence, one cannot definitively assert or deny that God possesses perception as an attribute distinct from knowledge. The reasoning is that for attributes not essential for action, scriptural proof is the basis, and no clear scriptural text establishes perception; on the other hand, denying it relies on an extreme literalist view that limits God's attributes to seven. The commentator concludes that the suspension position is 'safer and more correct' (aslam wa aṣaḥ) than either outright affirmation or negation.

Key Terms
الوقف (al-waqf)
الإدراك (al-idrāk)
الدليل العقلي (al-dalīl al-ʿaqlī)
الدليل السمعي (al-dalīl al-samʿī)
الصفات الثلاث (al-ṣifāt al-thalāth)
الظاهرية (al-Ẓāhiriyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That waqf (suspension) implies outright denial of the attribute of perception.
Correction: Waqf is a reasoned suspension due to conflicting proofs; it does not deny the attribute but refrains from affirming or negating it without clear evidence.

Checkpoint
Why do the people of waqf suspend judgment on whether perception is an attribute of God?
A) Because they reject all rational proofs.
B) Because the scriptural proofs are contradictory and no clear text establishes it.
C) Because they affirm only the seven known attributes.
D) Because they consider perception identical to knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The waqf position on perception is considered safer and more correct because it neither affirms nor denies without clear scriptural evidence, avoiding the pitfalls of both extremes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological issue where scholars disagree, do you have the patience and humility to suspend judgment (waqf) until you have clear evidence, or do you feel compelled to take a side based on limited reasoning?
?
The waqf position is called 'safer' because it avoids both affirming without proof and denying without proof. In your own life, are you more concerned with being 'right' or with being safe from error before Allah?
?
The dispute here hinges on whether you rely on rational proof or scriptural proof. Which do you naturally trust more? Are you willing to let the source of your trust be examined in light of this lesson's warning about extremes?

Essential Attributes and the Definition of Perception
Sanusi introduces essential attributes and defines perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the essential attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) as the fourth category of divine attributes and the Ashʿarī definition of perception (al-idrāk).

Core Idea
Essential attributes are God's active perfections; perception is a universal representation, not just sense.

Sanusi now enters the fourth and final division of attributes—the essential attributes, which are the direct result of the previous discussions. He lists seven of these attributes in a poetic line, then provides a precise definition of perception (idrāk), a key term that applies to both divine and creaturely knowing. This lesson unpacks both the list and the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic verse and commentary once. Try to identify the seven attributes from the verse before looking at the translation.
The verse is dense; note the line numbers.
2
Understand the placement
Notice that the commentary says this section is the 'result' of what preceded. The essential attributes are derived from the positive and relative attributes discussed earlier.
3
Analyze the definition of idrāk
Study the definition: 'the representation (tamaththul) of the reality of the perceived before the perceiver, beholding it by that with which he perceives.' Break down each component: tamaththul, haqīqat al-mudrak, al-mudrik.
This definition applies to both God and creatures in meaning, but modality differs.
4
Connect to theology
Ask yourself: How does this definition of perception relate to God's knowledge and His attributes of life, will, etc.?
The commentary hints that these attributes are results, i.e., they presuppose the earlier attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الرابع: الصفات المعنوية
١- حَيٌّ عَلِيمٌ قَادِرٌ مُرِيدُ سَمِيعٌ بَصِيرٌ مَا يَشَاءُ يُرِيدُ
ثمَّ شرَعَ فيما هو كالنتيجة لما قبله - وهو الصَّفات المعنوية، رابعُ أقسام الصفات. والإدراك: تمثُل حقيقة المدرك عند المدرك يشاهدها بما به يدرك.
The fourth section: the essential attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya)
1. Living, Knowing, Willing, Hearing, Seeing, Whatever He wills He wills.
Then he began what is like the result of what preceded—namely the essential attributes, the fourth of the divisions of attributes. And perception (al-idrāk): the representation of the reality of the perceived before the perceiver, beholding it by that with which he perceives.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage begins the fourth section on the essential attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya). It first presents a verse listing the attributes: Living, Knowing, Powerful, Willing, Hearing, Seeing, and the general clause 'whatever He wills He wills.' The commentator explains that this section is like the result of the previous discussions, as these attributes are derived from the meaning of the earlier ones. Then he defines perception (al-idrāk): it is the representation (tamaththul) of the reality of the perceived object before the perceiver, who beholds it by means of the faculty by which he perceives. This definition applies to both God and creatures in terms of meaning, though the modality differs.

Key Terms
الصفات المعنوية (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya)
الإدراك (al-idrāk)
تمثُل (tamaththul)
حقيقة المدرك (ḥaqīqat al-mudrak)
المدرك (al-mudrik)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think idrāk is only sense perception (seeing, hearing).
Correction: The passage defines idrāk broadly as representation of reality before the perceiver, not limited to sensory organs.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the definition of perception (al-idrāk)?
A) The simple awareness of an object.
B) The representation of the reality of the perceived before the perceiver, beholding it by that with which he perceives.
C) The acquisition of knowledge about an object.
D) The act of seeing or hearing.

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes are the active perfections of God, and perception is the representation of the perceived reality before the perceiver, a definition that unifies divine and creaturely knowing in meaning but not modality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that perception is a representation of reality before the perceiver. When you think of God's names and attributes, do you merely repeat words, or do you actively picture their reality as if they are present before you? What specific attribute will you try to really represent in your mind today?
?
The essential attributes are 'the result' of what came before. Is your own knowledge of God built step by step, or do you leap to conclusions? What foundational truth about God have you neglected that you need to rebuild today?
?
Sanusi defines perception as beholding the reality through a faculty. What or who are you 'beholding' in your daily life that distracts you from beholding God's perfections? Will you make a deliberate choice to train your heart's perception on God's attributes right now?

Understanding al-Sifaat al-Ma'nawiyya: The Derived Attributes
Introduces the fourth category of divine attributes, their derivation, and their number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the ma'nawiyya attributes and how they are derived as branches and results of the ma'ani attributes.

Core Idea
The ma'nawiyya attributes are the results of the ma'ani attributes, like branches from a root.

After discussing the essential attributes (sifaat al-ma'ani), the author turns to a related set: the ma'nawiyya attributes. These are not separate from the ma'ani but are their consequences. Understanding this relationship is key to grasping the interconnectedness of divine attributes in Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the provided Arabic text once without translation, then read the literal translation. Note the key terms: فرع and نتيجة.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the relationship between attributes
The passage states that the ma'nawiyya are like a branch (far') and a result (natija) of the ma'ani. Ask yourself: what does it mean for an attribute to be derived from another?
Think of a tree: the ma'ani are the trunk, the ma'nawiyya are the branches.
3
Reflect on the significance of derivation
Consider why the author calls the ma'nawiyya a 'result' of what preceded. This highlights that the attributes are not arbitrary but follow logically from the previous categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم شرع فيما هو كالنتيجة لما قبله - وهو الصفات المعنوية، رابع الأقسام وهي سبع، وقيل لها المعنوية: نسبة للسبع المعاني منها، والتي هي فرع منها، وعلى هذا فهي بمنزلة النتيجة لما قبلها.
Then he embarked on what is like a result of what preceded - namely the ma'nawiyya attributes, the fourth of the categories, and they are seven, and they are called al-ma'nawiyya: in relation to the seven al-ma'ani from which they are derived, and they are branches of them, and upon this they are in the position of a result of what preceded.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth division of attributes in the author's classification. It states that these attributes are called ma'nawiyya because they are derived from the seven meanings (sifaat al-ma'ani). They are like branches (far') of the meanings, and like a result (natija) of what came before. The passage emphasizes the derivative nature of these attributes.

Key Takeaway
The ma'nawiyya attributes are seven in number and are derived as branches and results of the seven ma'ani attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The passage teaches that these seven attributes are derived and not self-standing. In what areas of your belief do you treat Allah's attributes as if they were separate powers? Repent of that division and commit to affirming His oneness in all that you say and do.

The Divine Attributes of Life and Knowledge
Defines and proves the divine attributes of Life and Knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and scriptural proofs for Allah being al-Hayy and al-'Alim, and the unique reality of these attributes as essential to His essence.

Core Idea
Allah’s life and knowledge are essential to Himself, not borrowed from creation.

In this passage, the author establishes two of the seven essential attributes of Allah: Life and Knowledge. He argues that these attributes are necessarily proven for Allah through reason, revelation, and consensus. The passage also clarifies the true meaning of al-Hayy and al-'Alim, distinguishing them from created beings who possess life and knowledge derivatively. Let's examine the text to see the evidence and definitions provided.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice how the author moves from proving life to defining it, then to proving knowledge.
The passage uses the term 'ضرورة' (necessity) to indicate rational necessity.
2
Identify the proofs for al-Hayy
Look for the three sources of proof mentioned: necessity (ضرورة), the Book and Sunnah, and consensus. Also note the logical argument that a knowing and powerful being must be living.
3
Understand the definition of al-Hayy
The passage defines Haqiqat al-Hayy as He whose life is essential to His essence (لذاته). Contrast this with creatures whose life is derived from a spirit or soul.
This guards against the misconception that Allah's life is like a created life.
4
Extend to al-'Alim
Similarly, the passage proves that Allah is 'Alim because knowledge is necessary for Him, and defines His knowledge as all-encompassing of everything that can be known.
Notice the phrase 'شامل لكل ما من شأنه أن يعلم' (encompassing everything that is in its nature to be known).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحيث وجبت له الحياة فهو حي كما علم من ضرورة، وثبت بالكتاب والسنة، بحيث لا يمكن إنكاره ولا تأويله، أنه تعالى حي، وسميع، وبصير، وانعقد الإجماع عليه، وما ثبت من كونه تعالى عالما قادرا، إذ العالم القادر لا يكون إلا حيا ضرورة، وحقيقة الحي هو: الذي تكون حياته لذاته، وليس ذلك لأحد من الخلق. وحيث وجب له العلم فهو عليم أي عالم، وهو: الذي علمه شامل لكل ما من شأنه أن يعلم.
And since life is necessarily proven for Him, He is Hayy (Living) as is known from necessity, and established by the Book and the Sunnah, such that it cannot be denied nor interpreted away, that He, exalted, is Living, Hearing, Seeing, and consensus has been concluded upon it. And what is established of His being All-Knowing and All-Powerful, for the knowing and powerful cannot be except living by necessity. And the reality of al-Hayy is: He whose life is essential to Himself, and that is not for any of creation. And since knowledge is necessarily proven for Him, He is 'Alim (All-Knowing), i.e., knowing, and He is: He whose knowledge encompasses everything that is in its nature to be known.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines two of the seven ma'nawiyya attributes: al-Hayy and al-'Alim. It first affirms that Allah is Hayy based on necessity, scripture, and consensus. It argues that since Allah is Knowing and Powerful, He must be Living, as a knowing and powerful being is necessarily living. Then it defines the true meaning of al-Hayy as He whose life is essential (li dhatihi), not derived from an external source like a spirit. This applies only to Allah. Then it defines al-'Alim as He whose knowledge encompasses everything that can be known.

B) He whose life is essential to Himself.
C) He who is powerful.

Key Takeaway
Allah is al-Hayy and al-'Alim with life and knowledge that are essential to His essence and unlike any created being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says Allah's knowledge encompasses everything. Is there a sin you are still hiding from Him in your heart, as if He doesn't see? Will you now confess it and seek His forgiveness, knowing He already knows but waits for you to turn to Him?
?
Your own life and knowledge are gifts given to you, but Allah's are essential to Him. How does this difference affect your humility before Him? What one action can you take tomorrow to remind yourself that He alone is truly independent of all?

The Correct Stance on al-Ahwaal and the Purpose of Listing al-Sifaat al-Ma'nawiyya
Clarifies that there are no states (ahwaal) and the purpose of listing ma'nawiyya attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that there are no states (ahwaal) and why the ma'nawiyya attributes are listed after the ma'ani attributes, including the refutation of deviant sects who deny the subsistence of attributes.

Core Idea
Denying states means denying that ma'nawiyya are separate entities, not denying the attributes themselves.

In this passage, the author states the correct Ash'ari stance on the controversial concept of states (ahwaal). He then explains why he listed the ma'nawiyya attributes after the ma'ani attributes: to emphasize that attributes necessarily subsist with the essence, refuting those who doubted this. Finally, al-Dardir clarifies that denying states does not mean denying the attributes like power; it only denies their being additional existences beyond the meanings.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of states
States (ahwaal) were a theological concept proposed to explain how attributes relate to the essence. Some argued they were real but neither existent nor non-existent.
The Ash'ari position is to reject states as real entities.
2
Read the author's affirmation of the correct view
Read the first sentence: 'The correct view according to us is that there is no state.' This is the stance of verifiers like Ibn al-Subki.
3
Notice the purpose of listing ma'nawiyya attributes
The author says he listed the ma'nawiyya attributes after the ma'ani attributes to show the necessity of the attribute subsisting with the described being.
This is a refutation of deviant sects.
4
Consider the refutation of deviant sects
The deviants allowed that some attributes like speech and will might not subsist with the essence, and they denied that attributes are additional to the essence.
This is a critical theological point.
5
Understand al-Dardir's clarification
Al-Dardir explains that denying the state means denying that the ma'nawiyya attributes are additional existences over the ma'ani attributes, not denying the existence of the attributes themselves like power.
This avoids misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المصنف في شرحه الصغير: والصحيح عندنا أنه لا حال، كما هو مختار المحققين كابن السبكي في جمع الجوامع بل إنما عددت هذا القسم بعد عدي صفات المعاني لبيان وجوب قيام الصفة بالموصوف؛ ردا على بعض فرق الضلال حيث جوزوا في بعضها عدم قيامه بالموصوف، كالكلام والإرادة، وحيث نفوا زيادة صفاته على ذاته، وعلى هذا فهي بمنزلة النتيجة لما قبلها. قال الشيخ الدردير ومعنى إنكار الحال: إنكار زيادتها على المعاني، لا إنكار كونه قادرا مثلا من أصله، إذ هذا لا يقوله مسلم أبدا، إذ كونه قادرا مجمع عليه، وإنما الخلاف في زيادته على المعاني والحق عدم الزيادة.
The author said in his small commentary: 'The correct view according to us is that there is no state (haal), as is the choice of the verifiers like Ibn al-Subki in Jam' al-Jawami'. Rather, I only counted this section after counting the attributes of meanings (sifaat al-ma'ani) to clarify the necessity of the attribute subsisting with the described being; in refutation of some deviant sects who allowed in some of them the non-subsistence of the attribute with the described, such as speech and will, and who denied the increase of His attributes over His essence. And upon this, they are in the position of a result of what preceded.' Al-Shaykh al-Dardir said: 'And the meaning of denying the state (haal) is: denying their increase over the meanings, not denying that He is Powerful, etc., from its root, for no Muslim ever says that, since His being Powerful is agreed upon. The disagreement is only in its increase over the meanings, and the truth is its non-increase.'
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage addresses the theological concept of 'states' (ahwaal), which were proposed by some theologians to explain the relationship between attributes and essence. The author affirms that the correct position is that there are no states, following the verifiers like Ibn al-Subki. Instead, the listing of the ma'nawiyya attributes after the ma'ani attributes serves to demonstrate that the attributes necessarily subsist with the described being (al-mawsuf). This is a refutation of deviant sects who doubted the necessity of attributes like speech and will subsisting with the essence, and who denied that the attributes are additional to the essence. The author then clarifies that this is why the ma'nawiyya attributes are like a result of the ma'ani attributes. Al-Dardir adds that denying the state means denying that the ma'nawiyya attributes are additional existences beyond the ma'ani attributes, not denying the reality of the attributes like power and will.

Key Terms
حال
أحوال
صفات المعاني
زيادة
قيام الصفة بالموصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying states (ahwaal) means denying that attributes like power and will are real.
Correction: Denying states only denies that ma'nawiyya attributes are additional separate entities; it does not deny the reality of the attributes themselves (e.g., that Allah is Powerful).

Checkpoint
According to the author, why did he list the sifaat al-ma'nawiyya after the sifaat al-ma'ani?
A) To increase the number of attributes.
B) To clarify the necessity of the attribute subsisting with the described being.
C) To introduce a new category.
D) To deny the existence of attributes.

Key Takeaway
The correct Ash'ari position is that there are no states; the ma'nawiyya attributes are not separate entities but a result of the ma'ani attributes, and denying states does not deny the reality of the attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that no Muslim ever denies that Allah is Powerful. But do you live as if that is true? In moments of helplessness, do you truly believe His power is completely and eternally subsisting in His essence? Examine your actions: do they reflect certainty in His attributes or are they entangled in doubt? Commit this week to reminding yourself daily that His attributes are real and fully present.
?
The author refutes those who allowed some attributes like speech and will not to subsist. Have you ever, even subconsciously, thought that Allah's will or speech is less than real—perhaps by questioning predestination or the Quran? What would it mean for you to fully submit to the reality of His attributes without reducing them? Pray for steadfastness in affirming all of Allah's attributes as they truly are.
?
The scholars debated the fine point of states to safeguard tawhid. How much do you care about theological precision? Are you willing to delve into these matters to protect your faith, or do you dismiss them as unnecessary? What does your attitude say about your love for knowing Allah? Make a plan to study one attribute of Allah this week with a sound source.

Defining Hayy and 'Alim, and Rejecting al-Hal
Defines Hayy and 'Alim, and rejects the concept of al-Hal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attributes of Life and Knowledge are necessary for God, and why the concept of 'state' (al-hal) is rejected by Sunni theologians.

Core Idea
God's attributes are real and essential; there is no separate 'state' beyond them.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary on Umm al-Barahin defines two key divine attributes: al-Hayy (the Living) and al-'Alim (the Knowing). It also clarifies a critical theological dispute about the nature of attributes, rejecting the idea of a 'state' (al-hal) as an extra entity. Understanding these points is foundational for grasping the Sunni position on God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the definition of Hayy and 'Alim.
The passage uses terms like 'ضرورة' (necessarily) and 'ثبت بالكتاب والسنة'.
2
Identify the two attributes defined
Find where Sanusi defines 'Hayy' and 'Alim'. What is the basis for each? (necessity, scripture, consensus)
3
Understand the meaning of 'al-Hal'
Read the section on al-Hal. The author explains that the correct position is to deny any additional entity beyond the attributes of meanings.
Al-Dardir clarifies that this does not deny the attribute itself.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Why does the author mention this refutation? What deviant views are being corrected? (denying subsistence of attributes, denying increase over essence)

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات التي هي فرعٌ الدين الأقسام وهي سبع ، وقيل لها المعنويَّةُ : نسبة للسَّبع ) المعاني منها (۱) ـ فقال: وحيثُ وجبت له الحياة فهو (حَيَّ) كما عُلِم مِن ضرورةً ، وثبت بالكتاب والسُّنَّة ، بحيث لا يمكن إنكاره ولا تأويله ، أنَّه تعالى حي، وسميع ، وبصير ، وانعقد الإجماع عليه ، وما ثبت من كونه تعالى عالماً قادراً ، إذ العالِمُ القادرُ لا يكونُ إلَّا حيًّا ضرورةً، وحقيقة الحي(٢) هو: الذي تَكُون حياته لذاته (۳) ، وليس ذلك لأحدٍ مِن الخلق . وحيثُ وجبَ له العلم فهو (عَلِيمٌ ) أي : عالم (٤) ، وهو: الذي علمه شامل لكلِّ ما من شأنه أنْ يُعلَم. هنا قال المصنف في شرحه الصغير : والصحيح عندنا أنه لا حالَ ، كما هو مختار المحققين كابن السبكي في جمع الجوامع بل إنما عدَدتُ هذا القسم بعد عدي صفات المعاني لبيان وجوب قيام الصفة بالموصوف ؛ ردا على بعض فرق الضلال حيثُ جوزوا في بعضها عدمَ قيامه بالموصوف ، كالكلام والإرادة ، وحيث نفوا زيادة صفاته على ذاته ، وعلى هذا فهي بمنزلة النتيجة لما قبلها . قال الشيخ الدردير ومعنى إنكار الحال : إنكار زيادتها على المعاني ، لا إنكار كونه قادرا مثلا من أصله، إذ هذا لا يقوله مسلم أبدا ، إذ كونه قادرا مجمع عليه، وإنما الخلاف في زيادته على المعاني والحق عدم الزيادة. اهـ. وانظر: «هداية المريد» ج ١ ص ٤٠٥ .
The section on divine matters, which are a branch of religion; the divisions are seven, and they are called the moral attributes, in relation to the seven meanings. So he said: Where life is necessary for Him, He is 'Living' — as known necessarily, and established by the Book and the Sunnah, such that it cannot be denied nor interpreted away — that He is Living, Hearing, Seeing, and consensus has been reached upon it; and what is established of His being Knowing, Powerful, for the knower who is powerful cannot but be living necessarily. The reality of the Living is the one whose life is of His essence, and that is not for any of creation. And where knowledge is necessary for Him, He is 'Knowing', i.e., Knower, and He is the one whose knowledge encompasses everything that can be known. Here the author said in his small commentary: The correct position according to us is that there is no 'state', as is the choice of the verifiers like Ibn al-Subki in Jam' al-Jawami'; rather I only counted this section after enumerating the attributes of meanings to clarify the necessity of the attribute subsisting in the described, as a refutation of some deviant sects who permitted in some of them the attribute not subsisting in the described, like Speech and Will, and who denied the increase of His attributes over His essence; and on this, it is like the result of what came before. Al-Dardir said: The meaning of denying the 'state' is denying its increase over the meanings, not denying that He is powerful, for example, from its root, for no Muslim ever says that; indeed His being powerful is agreed upon; the disagreement is only on its increase over the meanings, and the truth is the absence of increase. End. And see: Hidayat al-Murid, vol. 1, p. 405.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth section on the moral attributes (al-sifat al-ma'nawiyya) and begins by stating they are branches of religion. It then defines two specific attributes: al-Hayy (the Living) and al-'Alim (the Knowing). For al-Hayy, the argument is that since life is necessary for God, He is Living; this is known by necessity, scripture, and consensus. The reality of life for God is that it is of His essence, not derived from anything else, unlike creatures. For al-'Alim, knowledge is necessary for God, so He is Knowing, and His knowledge encompasses all knowable things. The passage then inserts a discussion on the concept of 'state' (al-hal). The author clarifies that the correct position is that there is no separate 'state' over and above the attributes of meanings. This refutes deviant sects who denied that attributes like Speech and Will subsist in God, or who denied that attributes are additional to the essence. The purpose of mentioning this section after the attributes of meanings is to emphasize the necessity of attributes subsisting in the described. Al-Dardir explains that denying the 'state' does not mean denying that God is powerful, which is agreed upon, but rather denying that the 'state' is something extra beyond the attribute of power. The truth is that there is no such extra entity.

Key Terms

عليم
الحال
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying the concept of 'al-Hal' means denying that God has attributes like power and knowledge.
Correction: Denying al-Hal means denying that there is a separate 'state' beyond the attribute itself; the attribute (e.g., power) is real, but it is not accompanied by a distinct entity called a 'state'.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi and al-Dardir, what does 'denying al-Hal' mean?
A) Denying that God is powerful.
B) Denying that attributes subsist in God.
C) Denying that there is a separate entity beyond the attribute.
D) Denying the necessity of life and knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Hayy and 'Alim are necessary attributes of God, and there is no separate 'state' beyond the attributes themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Hayy and 'Alim', do you truly believe His life and knowledge are nothing like your own? What would change in your daily reliance if you internalized that His life is of His essence, not just a title?
?
The lesson warns against adding extra concepts like 'al-Hal' that distance you from the direct reality of God's attributes. Is there any comfortable belief or practice you hold that actually diminishes the immediacy of God's attributes as taught here? Commit to one step to remove it.
?
If God's knowledge encompasses everything (as stated in the source), why do you still fear the unknown? Does your anxiety reveal a gap between affirming this truth and actually trusting it? What would it look like to live today as if God's 'Alim attribute is your ultimate security?

Definitions of al-Qadir and al-Murid
Defines al-Qadir and al-Murid with their rational conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of al-Qadir as the one who can act or refrain according to will, and al-Murid as the one whose will brings the non-existent into existence.

Core Idea
God's power and will are both defined by His ability to choose and to create.

The passage lays out the fourth category of divine attributes: the moral attributes. It begins by defining two fundamental attributes: power and will. Understanding these definitions is essential for grasping how God interacts with creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the concise style and the use of conditional 'if He wills'.
The passage uses 'where power is necessary for Him' to indicate that these attributes are essential.
2
Identify the definition of al-Qadir
Find the sentence defining al-Qadir. Note that it emphasizes the ability to act or refrain, implying free will.
The term 'motives' is a concession to human understanding.
3
Identify the definition of al-Murid
Find the definition of al-Murid. Notice that will is directed at the non-existent to bring it into existence.
This shows that God's will is creative, not just directive.
4
Reflect on the role of will in both definitions
Both definitions center on will. How does this distinguish God from creation?
Creation acts by compulsion or instinct; God acts by free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الرابع الصفات المعنوية وحيثُ وجبت له القدرة فهو (قَادِرُ) والقادر هو : الذي إنْ شاءَ فعل وإن شاء ترك ؛ فهو المتمكِّن من الفعل والتّرك ، يصدر عنه كل منهما بحسب الدواعي المختلفة (۱) . وحيثُ وجبت له الإرادة فهو (مُريدٌ) وهو : الذي تَتوجه إرادته على المعدوم فتوجده (۲) . وحيثُ وجبَ له السَّمعُ فهو (سمع (۳)) أي: سَمِيعٌ ، لكنَّه حذف الياء منها للضّرورة. وحيثُ وجبَ له البَصر فهو (بَصِيرُ) لأنَّ كلَّ حيٌّ يَصحُ أن يكون سميعاً وبصيرا ، وكلُّ ما يصح للواجب من الكمالات أنْ يَثبت له بالفعل، لبراءته عن أنْ يكونَ له ذلكَ بالقُوَّة والإمكان ، يجب والجميع صفات كمال ،قطعاً، والخُلو عن صفة الكمال في حقٌّ مَن يصح أنَّه لا اتصافه بها نقص، وهو محال عليه تعالى، ومن خصائصه سبحانه: يَشْغله ما يُبْصِره عمَّا يسمعه ولا ما يسمعه عما يبصره، بل يحيط علما بالمسموعات والمُبصرات من غير سَبْقيَّة إدراك بإحدى الصفتين على الأخرى، فلا يَشغله شأن عن شأن. وأشار بقوله : (مَا يَشَا يُرِيدُ) إلى اختيار مذهب الجمهور من المشيئة والإرادة ، وأنَّه يُطلق إحداهما على الأخرى ، والمعنى أنَّ كلَّ ما يشاؤه
The fourth section: the moral attributes. And where power is necessary for Him, He is 'Powerful' — the Powerful is the one who, if He wills, does, and if He wills, refrains; so He is capable of action and refraining; each of them issues from Him according to different motives. And where will is necessary for Him, He is 'Willing' — the one whose will is directed at the non-existent and so He brings it into existence. And where hearing is necessary for Him, He is 'Hearing' — i.e., Hearing, but the ya' was dropped due to poetic necessity. And where sight is necessary for Him, He is 'Seeing' — because every living being can be hearing and seeing; and every perfection that is possible for the Necessary Being must be actual for Him, because He is free from being such by potentiality and possibility. All are perfections, absolutely. Lack of a perfection in one who can be described by it is a deficiency, which is impossible for God. Among His characteristics is that what He sees does not distract Him from what He hears, nor what He hears from what He sees; rather He encompasses knowledge of heard and seen things without priority of perception by one attribute over the other, so no matter distracts Him from another. And He indicated by his saying 'whatever He wills, He wills' the choice of the majority position on volition and will, that one is used interchangeably with the other, meaning that whatever He wills...
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage continues the definition of the moral attributes, covering al-Qadir (Powerful), al-Murid (Willing), al-Sami' (Hearing), and al-Basir (Seeing). For al-Qadir, the definition is the ability to act or refrain according to will, though the term 'motives' is a concession. For al-Murid, will is directed at the non-existent to bring it into existence. For al-Sami' and al-Basir, they are necessary because any living being can have these, and God, being free from potentiality, must have them actual. All these are perfections; lack of them would be deficiency, impossible for God. A special characteristic is that God's hearing and seeing are simultaneous and non-distracting—He perceives all things together without one sense preceding the other. Finally, the passage addresses the relationship between mashiyya (volition) and irada (will), affirming the majority view that they are synonymous and used interchangeably, meaning whatever God wills, He wills. The text ends mid-sentence but the key point is stated.

Key Terms

مريد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's power means He can do anything regardless of His will, as if power were arbitrary.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God's power is exercised 'if He wills,' so power is always in harmony with will.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of al-Qadir (the Powerful) according to this passage?
A) He who creates all things.
B) He who can do anything regardless of will.
C) He who can act or refrain as He wills.
D) He who only acts but never refrains.

Key Takeaway
God's power and will are inseparable; He is powerful because He can choose, and His will brings existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in God's power, but do you truly accept that He can refrain from acting? How does this affect your expectation of His intervention in your life?
?
How does understanding that God's will is directly creative change the way you view your own plans and wishes? Are you willing to submit your will to His?
?
If God's power is always coupled with His will, what does that mean for the things you desire? Do you sometimes wish for something without considering whether it aligns with His will?

Divine Hearing, Sight, and the Unity of Will
Explains necessity of hearing and sight, and equates volition with will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be Hearing and Seeing, how His perception is simultaneous and non-distracting, and that mashiyya (volition) and irada (will) are synonymous.

Core Idea
God's attributes of hearing and sight are perfect and simultaneous, and His will is identical with His volition.

After defining power and will, the passage proceeds to discuss the attributes of hearing and sight, establishing that God must possess them perfectly. Additionally, it addresses a theological nuance: the relationship between the terms 'volition' and 'will,' affirming their interchangeability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the justification for hearing and sight
Read the Arabic and translation. Pay attention to the argument that God must have these attributes because every living being can have them, and God must have all perfections actual.
The phrase 'because every living being can be hearing and seeing' shows that these are perfections.
2
Understand the special characteristic of simultaneous perception
Notice the sentence 'what He sees does not distract Him from what He hears.' This emphasizes that God's senses are not limited like human senses.
This is a distinctive attribute of God: He perceives all things at once without interference.
3
Examine the point about mashiyya and irada
Read the final part where the passage equates mashiyya and irada. Understand that they are used interchangeably.
The majority view is that there is no distinction between volition and will in God.

The Four Essential Attributes of Allah
Defines four essential attributes and argues they are necessary perfections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four essential attributes (al-sifāt al-maʿnawiyya) – Qadir, Murid, Sami', Basir – and why they are necessary perfections of Allah, including His unique characteristic of simultaneous comprehensive perception.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are perfections; lacking any would be a deficiency, which is impossible for Him.

In this passage, al-Laqqani defines the four essential attributes – power, will, hearing, and sight – that are necessarily proven for Allah. These are not mere names but real attributes that indicate absolute perfection. The author also explains why Allah must possess these perfections and how His perception is unlike ours, being simultaneous and all-encompassing.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the translation. Notice the rhythm and technical terms.
The passage uses poetic license (حذف الياء) for one term.
2
Identify each attribute
Find the four definitions: Qadir (ability to act or refrain), Murid (will bringing existence), Sami' (hearing), Basir (seeing). Understand how each is derived from a necessary attribute.
Each definition follows the pattern: 'Wherever [attribute] is necessary for Him, He is [name].'
3
Understand the perfection argument
The passage argues that these are perfections, and any lack would be deficiency. Since Allah is absolutely perfect, He must possess them actually, not potentially. Also note the unique characteristic: His perception is simultaneous without distraction.
This refutes any notion that God could lack these attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الرابع الصفات المعنوية وحيثُ وجبت له القدرة فهو (قَادِرُ) والقادر هو : الذي إنْ شاءَ فعل وإن شاء ترك ، فهو المتمكِّن من الفعل والتّرك ، يصدر عنه كل منهما بحسب الدواعي المختلفة . وحيثُ وجبت له الإرادة فهو (مُريدٌ) وهو : الذي تَتوجه إرادته على المعدوم فتوجده . وحيثُ وجبَ له السَّمعُ فهو (سمع) أي: سَمِيعٌ ، لكنَّه حذف الياء منها للضّرورة. وحيثُ وجبَ له البَصر فهو (بَصِيرُ) لأنَّ كلَّ حيٌّ يَصحُ أن يكون سميعاً وبصيرا ، وكلُّ ما يصح للواجب من الكمالات أنْ يَثبت له بالفعل، لبراءته عن أنْ يكونَ له ذلكَ بالقُوَّة والإمكان ، يجب والجميع صفات كمال ،قطعاً، والخُلو عن صفة الكمال في حقٌّ مَن يصح أنَّه لا اتصافه بها نقص، وهو محال عليه تعالى، ومن خصائصه سبحانه: يَشْغله ما يُبْصِره عمَّا يسمعه ولا ما يسمعه عما يبصره، بل يحيط علما بالمسموعات والمُبصرات من غير سَبْقيَّة إدراك بإحدى الصفتين على الأخرى، فلا يَشغله شأن عن شأن.
The fourth section: the essential attributes (al-sifāt al-maʿnawiyya). Wherever power (qudra) is necessary for Him, He is (Qādir) al-Qādir (the Powerful). Al-Qādir is: the one who, if He wills, does, and if He wills, leaves; so He is able to act and to refrain, each of them issuing from Him according to different motives. And wherever will (irāda) is necessary for Him, He is (Murīd) al-Murīd (the Willer), which is: the one whose will is directed to the non-existent and He brings it into existence. And wherever hearing (samʿ) is necessary for Him, He is (samʿ) meaning Samīʿ (All-Hearing), but the yāʾ is omitted for poetic necessity. And wherever sight (baṣar) is necessary for Him, He is (Baṣīr) All-Seeing, because every living being may properly be hearing and seeing, and every perfection that is possible for the Necessary Being must be actually established for Him, due to His being free from having it potentially or possibly; all are attributes of perfection, absolutely, and being devoid of an attribute of perfection in the case of one to whom it is possible to be described by it is a deficiency, which is impossible for Him, exalted is He. And among His specific characteristics, may He be glorified: He is not occupied by what He sees from what He hears, nor what He hears from what He sees; rather, He encompasses knowledge of audibles and visibles without priority of perception by one attribute over the other; no matter preoccupies Him from another matter.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth section of divine attributes: the essential attributes (al-sifāt al-maʿnawiyya). It begins by defining each attribute in turn. First, al-Qādir (the Powerful): He is the one who can act or refrain according to His will, issuing each from different motives. Second, al-Murīd (the Willer): His will is directed to the non-existent, bringing it into existence. Third, al-Samīʿ (All-Hearing): He hears, and the poem uses 'samʿ' for poetic metre. Fourth, al-Baṣīr (All-Seeing): He sees. The passage then explains that these are attributes of perfection, and any lack of them would be a deficiency, which is impossible for God. Moreover, God has a specific characteristic: He is not preoccupied by one sense over another; He knows all visibles and audibles simultaneously without one perception preceding another.

If you truly believed that, would you be more careful about how you use this existence He granted? What specific action will you take today to align your will with His?

The Synonymous Relationship of Mashiyyah and Iradah
Explains that Mashiyyah and Iradah are synonymous according to the majority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the majority of scholars consider mashiyyah (volition) and iradah (will) to be synonymous terms for the same divine attribute, and the reasoning behind this position.

Core Idea
Mashiyyah and iradah are two names for the same divine will.

In this lesson, we examine a key point of consensus in Sunni theology: the relationship between two of Allah's attributes, mashiyyah and iradah. The passage from the matn asserts that these terms are synonymous, a view held by the majority of scholars. We'll see how the author argues for this unity and note the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice how the author presents the majority position.
Pay attention to the phrase 'mā yashā yurīd'.
2
Identify key terms
Identify the terms 'mashiyyah' and 'iradah' in the passage. Understand that they refer to the same attribute according to the majority.
3
Understand the argument and opposing view
The passage states that everything Allah wills is His object of will and vice versa. Contrast this with the view of those who differentiate between the two terms.
The opposing view is attributed to the Karrāmiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتحاد وأشار بقوله : (مَا يَشَا يُرِيدُ) إلى اختيار مذهب الجمهور من المشيئة والإرادة ، وأنَّه يُطلق إحداهما على الأخرى ، والمعنى أنَّ كلَّ ما يشاؤه الله فهو من حيث إِنَّه مَشي له مُراد له ، وكلُّ ما يريده فهو من حيث أنه مراد له مَشيءٌ له ، خلافًا لمن فرّق بينهما.
Unity: And He indicated by His saying: (mā yashā yurīd) to choosing the position of the majority regarding mashiyyah (volition) and irādah (will), and that one of them is applied to the other. The meaning is that everything that God wills (yashā'uhu) is, inasmuch as it is His volition (mashī'), His object of will (murād), and everything that He wills (yurīduhu) is, inasmuch as it is His object of will, His volition (mashī' lah), contrary to those who differentiate between them.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage explains the relationship between two divine attributes: mashiyyah (volition) and irādah (will). The poet's line 'mā yashā yurīd' is interpreted as affirming that the majority of scholars hold that these two terms are synonymous – one can be used in place of the other. The meaning is that whatever God wills (mā yashā'uhu) is also His object of will (murād), and whatever He wills (yurīduhu) is also His volition (mashī' lah). This is in opposition to those (such as the Karrāmiyya) who differentiate between them.

Key Terms
المشيئة
الإرادة
الجمهور
المريد
المراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some scholars think mashiyyah and iradah are distinct attributes.
Correction: The majority holds that they are synonymous; everything Allah wills is His object of will and vice versa.

Checkpoint
According to the majority position described, what is the relationship between mashiyyah and iradah?
A) They are distinct
B) They are synonymous
C) Mashiyyah is eternal while iradah is temporal
D) Iradah is restricted to commands

Key Takeaway
The majority of Sunni scholars affirm that mashiyyah and iradah are synonymous terms for Allah's eternal will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's will is single and undivided, then your obedience or disobedience cannot be split between what you 'want' and what He 'wills'. Where in your life do you act as if you can separate your desires from His command? Will you today consciously submit your will to His, even in a small matter you've been resisting?
?
The source says the majority affirm synonymy—so why do you sometimes act as if there are two wills: one you obey and one you ignore? What would it mean for your daily choices if you truly believed that everything He wills is His object of will, including the trials and commands you find difficult? Commit now to one specific area where you will stop differentiating and start submitting.

The Seventh Essential Attribute: Allah is the Speaker
Introduces the attribute of speech, stating universal agreement on the attribute but disagreement on its meaning and pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all sects agree Allah is a Speaker, but differ on the meaning and pre-eternity of His speech, and know how the author of al-Jawhara handles this.

Core Idea
All agree Allah speaks; the debate is about the nature of His speech.

In this passage, the author of Jawharat al-Tawhid introduces the seventh essential attribute: that Allah is Mutakallim, the Speaker. He notes that this is a point of universal agreement among all religious groups. However, the real theological debate concerns what exactly 'speech' means when applied to Allah, and whether it is pre-eternal or created. This lesson sets the stage for that discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: first the attribute, then the agreement, then the two points of disagreement.
2
Identify agreement and disagreement
The passage says there is no disagreement that Allah is a Speaker. The disagreement is on two points: the meaning of His speech, and its pre-eternity or temporal origination.
The author says the meaning has already been explained earlier.
3
Note the cross-references
The author references earlier and later parts of the poem. The meaning of speech is already covered; the pre-eternity will be discussed later when the line about declaring the Qur'an free from temporal origination is explained.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why is the nature of God's speech important? It affects how we understand the Qur'an—is it eternal or created? This was a major debate in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسابع الصفات المعنويَّة : أَنَّه تعالى (مُنْكَلَّمْ) لا خلاف لأرباب المَذاهب والمِلَل في ذلك ، إنَّما اختلفوا في معنى كلامه تعالى، وفي قدمه وحدوثه ، وقد علمت معناه ، وأمَّا قِدَمه فيأتي بيانه في قوله: ونَزِّه القُرآن أي: كَلَامَهُ عن الحدوث
And the seventh of the essential attributes: that He, exalted, is (Munkalam) a Speaker. There is no disagreement among the adherents of sects and religions concerning that; they only differed regarding the meaning of His speech, exalted, and regarding its pre-eternity and temporal origination. And you have already known its meaning; as for its pre-eternity, its explanation will come in his saying: 'And declare free the Qur'an, meaning His speech, from temporal origination ...' the line.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage introduces the seventh essential attribute: that God is Mutakallim (the Speaker). The author notes that all religious groups agree that God is a speaker; the disagreement is only about what His speech means and whether it is pre-eternal or created. The meaning of speech (that it is the eternal attribute of speech) has already been explained earlier. The question of its pre-eternity will be taken up later when the poet's line about declaring the Qur'an free from temporal origination is discussed.

Key Terms
المتكلم
الصفات المعنوية
كلام الله
قدم الكلام
حدوث الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all sects agree on the nature of God's speech, i.e., that it is pre-eternal and uncreated.
Correction: The passage clarifies that they only agree on the fact that God speaks; they disagree on the meaning and pre-eternity of His speech.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the point of agreement among all sects regarding God's speech?
A) That it is pre-eternal
B) That it is created
C) That God is a speaker
D) That it has letters and sounds

Key Takeaway
All religious groups agree that Allah is a Speaker, but they differ on what His speech means and whether it is pre-eternal or created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that Allah is the Speaker. But when you read the Qur'an, do you truly feel that you are hearing the words of the One who spoke them? If not, what barrier in your heart prevents this conviction? Take a moment to recite a verse as if Allah is addressing you directly.
?
If the Qur'an is the pre-eternal speech of Allah, then it is not a mere historical document. How does that reality challenge the way you prioritize it over other books or distractions? What will you change today to treat it as the eternal word that demands your full attention and obedience?
?
You know that all sects agree on the fact that Allah speaks, but they differ on its nature. Does this knowledge make you content with merely acknowledging the attribute, or does it drive you to seek a deeper understanding of what it means that Allah has spoken? Commit to one action today that deepens your connection to His speech.

The Seventh Attribute: God is al-Mutakallim (The Speaker)
Consensus on God being a Speaker, with dispute flagged.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the attribute of speech is unanimously affirmed among all schools, and that the real dispute concerns its meaning and whether it is pre-eternal or temporal.

Core Idea
Everyone agrees God speaks; the debate is about what 'speech' means and whether it is eternal.

This lesson introduces the seventh attribute of meaning: that God is al-Mutakallim (the Speaker). The author clarifies that there is complete agreement among all religious groups that God is described as speaking. However, the significant disagreement arises over the interpretation of His speech and its pre-eternality or temporality. Understanding this distinction is crucial before delving into later discussions about the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal English translation. Notice the author's concise style and the two key claims: consensus on the attribute itself, and disagreement on its meaning and temporal nature.
The passage refers to earlier and later explanations, so keep the lesson context in mind.
2
Identify the consensus and the disagreement
The author states there is no disagreement that God is متكلم. Then he pinpoints the actual dispute: the meaning of His speech and whether it is pre-eternal or temporal. Mark these two points in your mind.
This shows that the attribute's existence is not debated, only its nature.
3
Note the author's references to other lessons
The author says 'you have already known its meaning' (referring to earlier lessons) and promises an explanation of pre-eternality later. This teaches that theological topics are interconnected.
This is a typical feature of matn-based learning.
4
Reflect on why this distinction matters
The distinction between affirming the attribute and defining it prevents confusion. Many debates about God's speech (e.g., whether the Qur'an is created) hinge on this very point.
This lesson lays the groundwork for those later discussions.

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Doubt about Multiplicity of Pre-Eternals
Presents Mu'tazilite doubt and gives Ash'ari answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite dilemma about divine attributes and the Ash'ari answer that attributes are neither other than nor identical to the essence.

Core Idea
Attributes are neither other nor identical—avoiding both temporal change and multiple pre-eternals.

The Mu'tazilites posed a powerful dilemma: if God's attributes are real, they must be either temporal or pre-eternal, both leading to unacceptable conclusions. The Ash'ari answer, derived from al-Sanusi's poem, is that the attributes are neither other than the essence nor identical to it. This lesson unpacks the doubt and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
The Mu'tazilites argue that attributes must be either temporal or pre-eternal. Recognize this as a forced choice.
The dilemma is designed to corner those who affirm real attributes.
2
Examine the first horn: temporal attributes
If attributes are temporal, God would have temporal events in His essence and would have lacked perfections in pre-eternity.
3
Examine the second horn: pre-eternal attributes
If attributes are pre-eternal, there would be multiple pre-eternal entities, which is disbelief (kufr) by consensus.
The example of Christians shows the severity.
4
Learn the Ash'ari answer
The attributes are neither other than the essence nor identical to it. This resolves the dilemma because attributes are not separate pre-eternals nor temporal accidents.
This answer preserves both divine simplicity and the reality of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣٢ - مُنْكَلَّمْ ثُمَّ صِفَاتُ الذَّاتِ لَيْسَتْ بِغَيْرِ أو بِعَيْنِ الذَّاتِ
[تقرير شبهة المعتزلة في نفي صفات المعاني، ورَدُّها ] ولمَّا أثبت أهلُ الحقِّ الصفات الحقيقية وردت عليهم شُبهةٌ مِن جانب من نفاها ، تقريرها : إنَّ الصِّفات الوجوديَّة إما أن تكونَ حادثة فيلزم قيام الحَوادِث بذاته، وخلوه تعالى في الأزل عن العلم والقدرة والحياة وغَيرها من الكمالات، وإمَّا أنْ تكون قديمة فيلزم تعدد القدماء، وهو كفر بإجماع المسلمين، وقد كُفِّرَت النَّصارى بزيادة قَدِيمَيْن فكيف بالأكثر ؟ فأجاب عنها بقوله: (ثُمَّ صِفَاتُ الذَّاتِ أي: ثمَّ بعد تقرُّر الواجب لذاته تعالى ، وتقرر قيام صفاته الثبوتية بذاته أخبرك بأنَّه يَدفعُ عنكَ إِسْكَالَ تَعدُّد القدماء أنْ تقولَ: إِنَّ الصفات القائمة بذات الواجب المُتقرّر زيادتها عليه خارجًا لَيْسَتْ بِغَيْرِ الذات الواجب الوجود تعالى أو أي: وليست بِعَيْنِ الذات)
32 - al-Mutakallim; then the attributes of the essence are neither other than nor identical to the essence. [Presentation of the Mu'tazilite doubt regarding the negation of the attributes of meaning, and its refutation] And when the people of truth established the real attributes, a doubt was raised against them from the side of those who deny them. Its presentation: The existent attributes are either temporal, which would necessitate the occurrence of temporal events in His essence and His being devoid in pre-eternity of knowledge, power, life, and other perfections; or they are pre-eternal, which would necessitate a multiplicity of pre-eternals, and that is disbelief by consensus of the Muslims. The Christians were declared disbelievers for adding two pre-eternals, so how about more? So he answered it with his saying: (Then the attributes of the essence — i.e., then after the establishment of the Necessary Being in His essence, and the establishment of the subsistence of His positive attributes in His essence, He informs you that He repels from you the dilemma of multiplicity of pre-eternals by your saying: Indeed, the attributes subsisting in the essence of the Necessary, established as superadded to Him externally, are not other than the essence of the Necessary Existence, exalted, nor are they identical to the essence.)
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the Mu'tazilite doubt about the divine attributes. It begins by quoting the verse from the poem: 'al-Mutakallim; then the attributes of the essence are neither other than nor identical to the essence.' Then the author introduces the doubt: when the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) affirmed the real attributes (attributes of meaning), a doubt was raised by those who deny them. The doubt is presented as a dilemma: the attributes are either temporal or pre-eternal. If temporal, that would imply that temporal events occur in God's essence and that He was devoid of perfections like knowledge, power, and life before the attributes came into existence. If pre-eternal, that would entail multiple pre-eternal entities, which is kufr (disbelief) by consensus. The argument uses the example of Christians who were declared disbelievers for affirming two pre-eternals (the Father and the Son), so affirming more would be even worse. The answer is given by the verse: the attributes of the essence are neither other (ghayr) than the essence nor identical (ʿayn) to it. The commentary explains that this means the attributes are not separate from the essence, nor are they merely the essence itself. This resolves the dilemma because the attributes are not independent pre-eternals that would multiply the number of pre-eternal entities, nor are they temporal occurrences in God. They are aspects of God's essence that are neither fully distinct nor fully identical, thus avoiding both problems.

Key Terms
شبهة المعتزلة (Mu'tazilite doubt)
صفات الذات (attributes of essence)
تعدد القدماء (multiplicity of pre-eternals)
غير (other)
عين (identical)
كفر (disbelief)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer 'neither other nor identical' is contradictory or incoherent.
Correction: It is not contradictory: it means attributes are not separate existences (other) nor are they merely the essence without distinction (identical). They are real aspects of the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazilite dilemma regarding the attributes?
A) Attributes are either created or uncreated, but both lead to problems.
B) Attributes are either temporal or pre-eternal; temporal leads to change in God, pre-eternal leads to multiplicity of pre-eternals.
C) Attributes are either mental or external; mental leads to denial of reality, external leads to polytheism.
D) Attributes are either known or unknown; both are problematic.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari solution to the Mu'tazilite dilemma is that divine attributes are neither other than nor identical to the essence, preserving both divine simplicity and the reality of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's knowledge, do you ever imagine it as something separate from Him, like a stored database in the sky? How does the Ash'ari teaching that attributes are neither other nor identical reshape your mental image of God?
?
The Mu'tazilites were horrified by the idea of multiple pre-eternals, yet many of us unconsciously treat God's attributes as independent entities in our prayers and thoughts. Which of God's attributes do you most struggle to connect directly to His essence?
?
If God's attributes are neither other nor identical, then every act of knowing or trusting in His power or mercy is a direct encounter with God Himself—not a created thing. Does your heart feel the weight of that intimacy right now? How can you turn your next prayer into a real engagement with His essence through His attributes?
