⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقرير شبهة المعتزلة في نفي صفات المعاني، ورَدُّها ; لأنا لو قلنا: «هي هو» لأدَّى إلى أن تكون حادثة - والفرض أنها قائمة بذاته تعالى - لزم قيام الحادث بالقديم، وهو مُحالٌ ; وإِنْ كانت قديمة - والفرض أنها غيرُ ذاته - لزم أن تكون قدماء متعددة بتعدد الصفاتِ زيادة على الذَّاتِ ; والقول بتعدد القدماء هو الكفر بعينه، وقد نصَّ الله تعالى على كفر من قال: إنَّ الله ثالث ثلاثة ، فكيف يكون حالَ مَن أثبت هذا العدد العديد من القدماء ؟ وذهب الكرامية إلى: إثبات الصفات، ولكنّهم زعموا أنَّها حادثةٌ، فَرُّوا مِن القول بتعدد القدماء ، ولكنَّهم التزموا قيام الحادث بالقديم ، وهو باطل ; لما ثبت من قيامه بنفسه ، ومخالفته للحوادث ، ومن استحالة قيام الحادث بالقديم، فأما أبو الحسن فنظر إلى المسألة من جميع وجوهها ; فأثبت صفات الله تعالى للدليل العقلي والسمعي الدال على ثبوتها، وأثبتَ أَنَّها قديمة لاستحالة قيام الحوادث بذات الله تعالى، ثمَّ قال: إنه لا يلزم على ما نقول: تعدد القدماء كما زعم المعتزلة ; لأنَّ هذا إِنَّما يلزم لو قلنا: إِنَّ كلَّ صفة من هذه الصفات غير الذَّات مِن كلِّ وجه ، وبعبارة أخرى إنَّما يُلْزِمُنَا القولُ بتعدد القدماء لو كانت كل صفةٍ قائمة بنفسها ، ونحن لا نقول: إنَّ كلَّ صفةٍ مِن هذه الصفات غير الذات من كل وجه، ولا نقول: إِنَّ كلَّ صفةٍ منها قائمةٌ بنفسها ، وإنْ كنا نقول: إِنَّ كلَّ صفةٍ غير سائر الصفات، وإنما نقول: إن كلَّ صفة من هذه الصفات التي نُثْبِتُها لله تعالى غير الذات من حيث المفهوم، وهذا مِمَّا لا جدال فيه، ولكن كلّ صفة من هذه الصفات لازمةٌ للذات ; فلا تنفكُ الصفة عنها كما لا تنفكُ الذات عن الصفة، فالصفة على هذا ليست عينَ الذَّاتِ مِن كلِّ وجه، وليست غير الذات مِن كُلِّ وجه، وهذا معنى قول المصنف : «ليست بعين أو بغير الذات» جعل الأشعري ليست غير الذات ; لئلا يلزم قيام الحادث بالقديم، أو القولُ بتعددِ القدماء، وليست عين الذات فرارًا ممَّا ذهب إليه الفلاسفة والمعتزلة من نفي الصفات، وقال الشَّمس السَّمَرْقَنْدِيُّ: وهذا خلافٌ لفظي ; لأنَّ القول بأنَّها ليست بغير الذات، محمول على الغير الذي ينفكُّ عن الذات وإن كانت غيرا في المفهوم ، والقول بأنها غيرُ، محمول على الغير في المفهوم وإن كانت تنفكُّ عن الذات اهـ كلامه ، وفي هذا القدر كفاية. وتلخيص ما أشار إليه من الجواب : أنَّ المَحذُورَ إنَّما هو تعدد القدماء المتغايرة، ونحن نمنع تغاير الذات مع الصفات والصِّفاتِ بعضها مع بعض، فينتفي التَّعدُّد ; لأنَّه لا يكون إلا مع التغاير، فلا يلزمُ التَّعدُّد، ولا التَّكثُر ، ولا قدمُ الغير ، ولا تكثر القدماء، فعلِم أَنَّ مذهب أهلِ السُّنَّة أنَّ صفاتِ الذَّات زائدة عليها ، قائمة بها لازمةٌ لها لزوما لا يقبل الانفكاك.
A presentation of the Mu'tazilite objection concerning the negation of attributes of meaning (sifat al-ma'ani), and the response to it: for if we say 'they are He (huwa),' it would entail that they are originated — but the assumption is that they are subsisting in His essence, Exalted is He — then the subsistence of the originated in the eternal would be necessitated, which is impossible. And if they were eternal — and the assumption is that they are other than His essence — then there would be necessarily multiple eternals by the multiplicity of attributes additional to the essence; and the statement of multiple eternals is pure disbelief. Allah, Exalted, has stated the disbelief of whoever says that Allah is the third of three; so what would be the case of one who affirms this numerous number of eternals? The Karramiyya went to affirming the attributes, but they claimed that they are originated; they fled from the statement of multiple eternals, but they adhered to the subsistence of the originated in the eternal, which is false; because of what has been established of His self-subsistence, His dissimilarity to originated things, and the impossibility of the subsistence of the originated in the eternal. As for Abu al-Hasan, he considered the matter from all its aspects; so he affirmed the attributes of Allah, Exalted, by the rational and scriptural evidence indicating their establishment, and he affirmed that they are eternal because of the impossibility of originated things subsisting in the essence of Allah, Exalted. Then he said: It does not follow from what we say multiple eternals as the Mu'tazilites claimed; because this would only follow if we said that every one of these attributes is other than the essence in every respect. In other words, the statement of multiple eternals would only bind us if every attribute were self-subsisting. We do not say that every one of these attributes is other than the essence in every respect, nor do we say that every one of them is self-subsisting, although we do say that each attribute is other than the other attributes. We only say that every one of these attributes that we affirm for Allah, Exalted, is other than the essence in terms of conception (al-mafhum); this is indisputable. But every attribute of these attributes is necessarily concomitant with the essence; so the attribute does not separate from it just as the essence does not separate from the attribute. Thus the attribute on this basis is not identical to the essence in every respect, nor is it other than the essence in every respect. This is the meaning of the author's saying: 'They are neither identical to nor other than the essence.' Al-Ash'ari made them not other than the essence to avoid the subsistence of the originated in the eternal or the statement of multiple eternals, and not identical to the essence to flee from what the philosophers and Mu'tazilites went to of negating the attributes. Al-Shams al-Samarqandi said: 'This is a verbal dispute; because the statement that they are not other than the essence is interpreted as the otherness that separates from the essence, even if they are other in conception; and the statement that they are other interprets otherness in the conception, even if they do not separate from the essence.' His speech ends. In this amount, sufficiency. And a summary of what he indicated of the answer: the danger is only the multiplicity of distinct eternals, and we deny the distinctness (taghayyur) of the essence with the attributes and of the attributes with each other; so multiplicity is negated, because it only occurs with distinctness. Thus neither multiplicity nor plurality nor the eternity of another nor the multiplicity of eternals follows. So it is known that the school of Ahl al-Sunna is that the attributes of the essence are additional to it, subsisting in it, necessarily concomitant with it in a concomitance that does not accept separation.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and refutation of the Mu'tazilite objection against the attributes of meaning (sifat al-ma'ani). It also discusses the Karramiyya position and then fully expounds the Ash'ari solution. The author begins by reciting the Mu'tazilite argument: if the attributes are identical to the essence, they would be originated (since the essence is eternal, but identity would imply something originated?), or if they are other than the essence, they would be eternal and thus multiple eternals. The Mu'tazilites use this dilemma to deny attributes altogether. The Karramiyya attempt to avoid multiple eternals by affirming the attributes but claiming they are originated, but this leads to the impossible consequence of originated things subsisting in the eternal. The author then presents Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari's approach: he affirms the attributes as eternal based on rational and scriptural evidence, but denies the objection of multiple eternals by clarifying that the attributes are neither identical to the essence in every respect nor different in a way that implies separation. They are conceptually distinct but existentially inseparable. Al-Ash'ari's formulation avoids both extremes: making attributes identical (which leads to negation of attributes or origination) and making them fully distinct (which leads to multiple eternals). The passage concludes with a summary stating that the true danger is only multiplicity of distinct entities, and since the attributes are not distinct from the essence in a separating sense, no multiplicity follows. The school of Ahl al-Sunna holds that the attributes are additional to the essence, subsisting in it, and necessarily concomitant without possibility of separation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Ash'ari's statement 'neither identical nor other' is a logical contradiction (tertium non datur).
Correction: Al-Ash'ari distinguishes between conceptual otherness (ghayr bi al-mafhum) and existential otherness (ghayr bi al-wujud). The attributes are conceptually other but existentially inseparable, so they are neither identical nor completely other.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, why does affirming eternal attributes not lead to multiple eternals?
A) Because the attributes are identical to the essence.
B) Because the attributes are originated.
C) Because the attributes are conceptually other but not separable entities.
D) Because the attributes are self-subsisting but not distinct.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari resolved the dilemma by distinguishing conceptual otherness from existential independence, affirming attributes as eternal yet inseparable from the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that Allah has attributes like knowledge and power, do you ever catch yourself thinking of them as separate 'parts' of God, or reducing them to mere names? How does al-Ash'ari's middle path challenge both extremes in your own heart?
?
If someone accused you of believing in multiple gods because you affirm distinct attributes of Allah, would you be able to explain the difference between conceptual distinction and existential separation using this lesson? What would you say?
?
Al-Ash'ari insisted that attributes are 'not other than the essence' to avoid multiplicity, yet 'not identical' to avoid nullifying them. Is your understanding of Allah's attributes marked by this same careful balance, or do you lean toward one extreme? What would it cost you to change?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Denial of Attributes
Refutes Mu'tazilite claim and states Ash'ari doctrine of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theologians refute the Mu'tazilite claim that affirming attributes implies multiple eternal beings and the correct doctrine of essential attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes are not distinct beings, so affirming them does not imply multiplicity.

The Mu'tazilites argued that affirming real attributes would imply multiple eternal beings. This lesson presents the Ash'ari response: distinctness (taghayyur) is denied between essence and attributes, so multiplicity does not arise. You will also learn the positive Ash'ari doctrine that attributes are superadded yet inseparable.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author addresses the objection before stating the positive doctrine.
2
Identify key terms
Identify the Arabic terms for divine attributes, multiplicity of eternals, distinctness, separation, superadded, and necessary being.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Understand the refutation
The author argues that the harmful consequence (multiplicity of eternals) only follows if the attributes are distinct (mutaghayira) from the essence. The Ash'ari deny such distinctness, so multiplicity is negated.
Distinctness here means separate existence.
4
Learn the positive doctrine
The Ash'ari doctrine states that attributes are superadded to the essence, subsisting in it, inseparable, and eternal. Examples: life, knowledge, power.
Superadded does not mean separate; they are necessary concomitants.
5
Avoid the common misconception
The misconception is that 'superadded' implies separate entities. The text clarifies they are inseparable and necessarily exist with the essence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات: إلهين، ولو قلنا غيره لكانت محدثة، فيكون محلا للحوادث. وهو محال. وتلخيص ما أشار إليه من الجواب: أن المحذور إنما هو تعدد القدماء المتغايرة، ونحن نمنع تغاير الذات مع الصفات والصفات بعضها مع بعض، فينتفي التعدد؛ لأنه لا يكون إلا مع التغاير، فلا يلزم التعدد، ولا التكثر، ولا قدم الغير، ولا تكثر القدماء، فعلم أن مذهب أهل السنة أن صفات الذات زائدة عليها، قائمة بها لازمة لها لزوما لا يقبل الانفكاك؛ فهي دائمة الوجود، مستحيلة العدم؛ فهو حي بحياة، عالم بعلم، قادر بقدرة، وهكذا. وما نفى المعتزلة الصفات إلا هروبا من تعدد القدماء، ونحن نقول: القديم لذاته واحد، وهو الذات المقدس، وهذه صفات وجبت للذات، لا بالذات، والتعدد لا يكون في القديم لذاته.
Section of Divine Matters: Two gods, and if we said other than that it would be created, so it would be a locus for accidents. And that is impossible. And a summary of what he indicated from the answer: that the harmful consequence is only the multiplicity of the eternal things that are distinct, and we deny the distinctness of the essence with the attributes and the attributes with each other, so multiplicity is negated; because it does not occur except with distinctness, so multiplicity does not follow, nor multiplicity, nor pre-eternity of another, nor multiplicity of eternals. So it is known that the school of Ahl al-Sunnah is that the attributes of the essence are superadded to it, subsisting in it, necessary for it with a necessity that does not accept separation; so they are perpetual in existence, impossible of nonexistence; so He is living by life, knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, and so on. And the Mu'tazilites did not deny the attributes except fleeing from multiplicity of eternals, and we say: The eternal in Himself is one, and He is the sacred essence, and these are attributes that are necessary for the essence, not by essence, and multiplicity does not occur in the eternal in Himself.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari refutation of the Mu'tazilite argument that affirming real attributes superadded to the divine essence would lead to a multiplicity of eternal beings (ta'addud al-qudama'). The author first clarifies that the real objection concerns only the multiplicity of distinct eternal entities. The Ash'ari response is that the attributes are not distinct from the essence in the sense of being separable or independent; they are distinct only in concept (ma'na) but are necessary concomitants of the essence. Thus, the essence and attributes do not form a plurality of distinct beings. The passage then states the Sunna doctrine: the attributes of the essence are superadded (za'idah) to the essence, subsisting in it, inseparable, eternal, and impossible of non-existence. Examples: life, knowledge, power. Finally, it reasserts that the Mu'tazilites denied attributes only from fear of multiplicity, but the correct view is that the eternal in Himself is one, and the attributes are necessary for the essence (i.e., derived from it) but not self-necessary. This distinction avoids any multiplicity in the eternal essence.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazilite objection is that the attributes are not distinct beings, so affirming them does not imply multiple eternal entities; they are superadded but inseparable.

?

?

Are you willing to re-examine your assumptions based on evidence from revelation and reason?

The Four Types of Divine Attributes
Classifies divine attributes by their relation to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite classification of divine attributes into negative, relational, actual, and essential categories based on their relation to the essence.

Core Idea
Not all divine attributes are identical to the essence; some are negative, relational, or even separable.

In this lesson, we explore a concise but crucial classification from Sanusi's Jawharat al-Tawhid. The passage distinguishes four types of divine attributes based on how they relate to the divine essence. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for grasping later discussions on whether attributes are identical to or distinct from the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and its English translation. Notice the key terms: al-salbiyya, al-idāfiyya, al-fi'liyya, and al-nafsiyya.
The passage is one sentence, so read it multiple times to parse its structure.
2
Identify each attribute type
The passage lists four types: negative, relational, actual, and essential. For each, note the example given and whether it is described as 'ayn' (identical) or 'ghayr' (other).
The actual attributes are said to be 'ghayr' (other) according to the Ash'arites.
3
Understand the significance of 'ayn' vs 'ghayr'
This distinction is the key theological point: some attributes are identical to the essence (like existence), while others (like giving life and causing death) are considered separate acts not subsisting in the essence.
This aligns with the Ash'arite view that God's actions are not eternal attributes.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to see if you can identify which attributes are 'other' according to the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبإضافة الصفات إلى الذات خرجت السلبية: كليس بمركب، والإضافية: كقبل العالم، والفعلية: كالإحياء والإماتة عند الأشاعرة؛ فإنها غير، والنفسية أيضا كالوجود فإنها عين.
And by adding the attributes to the essence, the negative come out: like not being composite, and the relational: like before the world, and the actual: like giving life and causing death according to the Ash'arites; for they are other, and the essential also like existence, for it is identical.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage classifies divine attributes into four categories based on their relation to the essence. First, negative attributes (sifāt salbiyya) such as 'not being composite' – they negate imperfections. Second, relational attributes (sifāt idāfiyya) such as 'before the world' – they denote a relation to creation. Third, actual attributes (sifāt fi'liyya) such as giving life and causing death – according to the Ash'arites, these are considered 'other' (ghayr), meaning they do not subsist in the essence eternally but are created acts. Fourth, the essential attribute (sifāt nafsiyya) such as existence (al-wujūd) – this is identical (ayn) to the essence. The classification shows that not all attributes are identical to the essence; some are negative, some relational, some actual (and thus separable), and only the essential attribute is identical.

Key Terms
السلبية
الإضافية
الفعلية
النفسية
عين
غير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine attributes are identical to the essence.
Correction: Only the essential attribute (like existence) is identical; actual attributes are considered 'other' by the Ash'arites.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which category of attributes is described as 'other' (ghayr) by the Ash'arites?
A) Negative attributes
B) Relational attributes
C) Actual attributes
D) Essential attribute

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are classified into four types, with actual attributes being separable from the essence, unlike the essential attribute which is identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that God's actions like giving life and causing death are distinct from His essence. Do you truly believe that God's actions are separate from His essence, or do you unintentionally mix them? How does this affect your understanding of God's involvement in your daily life? Take a moment to reflect on whether you attribute changes in your life directly to God's essence or to His actions, and commit to seeing them as distinct yet unified in His will.
?
The negative attributes describe what God is not—like 'not composite'. Are there any attributes you mistakenly ascribe to God that are actually imperfections? Examine your own thoughts about God's nature and see if any fall under the category of misattributing composite or changeable characteristics. Repent from any such notions and affirm God's transcendence.
?
The essential attribute is existence itself, identical to His essence. Do you live as if your own existence is dependent on His existence? How often do you acknowledge that your continued existence is a direct manifestation of His essential attribute? Use this lesson to deepen your reliance on Him.

The Mu'tazilite Objection and Its Refutation
Al-Qari presents the Mu'tazilite objection and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite objection that affirming multiple attributes leads to multiplicity of eternals, and the Sunni refutation that attributes are necessary for the essence and not separate entities.

Core Idea
Attributes are necessary for the essence, not additional eternal beings.

Al-Qari presents the Mu'tazilite argument that affirming real attributes of meaning leads to multiplicity of eternal entities, and then refutes it by clarifying that attributes are necessary for the essence, not self-subsisting. This lesson breaks down the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Al-Qari first describes the Sunni position on attributes, then states the Mu'tazilite objection, and finally gives the refutation.
Pay attention to the phrase 'وجبت للذات لا بالذات' (necessary for the essence, not by themselves).
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite fear
The text says the Mu'tazilites denied attributes 'out of flight from multiplicity of eternals.' Ask yourself: why would affirming multiple real attributes imply multiple eternal beings?
This is the core of the objection.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation states that the Eternal in Himself (القديم لذاته) is one, and attributes are 'وجبت للذات لا بالذات' (necessary for the essence, not by themselves). This means attributes are not independent eternal entities; they are necessarily entailed by the essence. So no multiplicity.
Compare this to the Christian doctrine of persons to see the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقرير شبهة المعتزلة في نفي صفات المعاني، ورَدُّها دائمةُ الوجودِ ، مستحيلةُ العدم؛ فهو حي بحياة، عالم الانفكاك [؟]؛ فهي بعلم، قادر بقدرة، وهكذا.. وما نَفَى المعتزلةُ الصِّفاتِ إِلَّا هُروبًا مِن تَعدُّد القدماء، ونحن نقول: القديم لذاته واحدٌ ، وهو الذات المُقدَّس ، وهذه صفات وجبت للذات، لا بالذات، والتَّعدُّد لا يكون في القديم لذاته.
Presentation of the Mu'tazilite doubt in negating the attributes of meaning, and its refutation: They are perpetual in existence, impossible of non-existence; thus He is living by life, knowledgeable by [unclear], powerful by power, and so on. And the Mu'tazilites only denied the attributes out of flight from multiplicity of eternals. And we say: The Eternal in Himself is one, and He is the sanctified Essence, and these attributes are necessary for the essence, not by themselves, and multiplicity does not occur in the Eternal in Himself.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage begins as a presentation of the Mu'tazilite doubt regarding the negation of attributes of meaning (sifat al-ma'ani) and then provides its refutation. The author first states the position of the Ahl al-Sunna: the attributes of meaning (life, knowledge, power) are perpetual in existence and impossible of non-existence, so God is living by a life, knowing by a knowledge, etc. Then he states the Mu'tazilite motive: they denied the attributes only to avoid multiplicity of eternals. The refutation is that the Eternal in Himself is one, and the attributes are necessary for the essence (wajiba li al-dhat) not by themselves (la bi al-dhat), so multiplicity does not occur in the Eternal in Himself. The phrase 'wajiba li al-dhat' means they are necessitated by the essence, and 'la bi al-dhat' means they are not self-subsisting eternals. Therefore, the attributes are not separate eternal entities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming multiple eternal attributes means believing in multiple gods or eternal entities separate from God.
Correction: Attributes are not separate entities; they are necessary for the essence (wajiba li al-dhat) and not independent (la bi al-dhat). Therefore, they do not multiply the number of eternal beings.

Checkpoint

A) Because they thought attributes are created
B) To avoid multiplicity of eternals
C) Because they are not in the Quran
D) Because they lead to anthropomorphism

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites rejected attributes to avoid multiplicity, but the correct view is that attributes are necessary for the essence and do not imply multiple eternals.

The Four Categories of Divine Attributes
Al-Qari classifies attributes into four categories based on relation to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine attributes are classified into four categories based on their relation to Allah's essence.

Core Idea
Attributes relate to essence as either identical, other, or merely negating.

This passage presents a foundational classification in Islamic theology: how divine attributes relate to Allah's essence. Al-Qari lists four categories with clear examples. Understanding this classification helps clarify debates about whether attributes are identical to or distinct from the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting the structure: it introduces the classification, then lists each type with an example and a concluding remark about identity or otherness.
Pay attention to the terms 'عين' (identical) and 'غير' (other).
2
Identify the four categories
Find the four types: negative, relational, active, and essential. Note the example given for each and whether it is said to be 'ayn or ghayr.
3
Understand the basis of classification
The classification is based on the attribute's relation to the essence. Negative attributes negate imperfections, relational attributes express a relationship, active attributes involve action, and essential attributes are intrinsic.
The Ash'ari view on active attributes is mentioned as an example.
4
Distinguish 'ayn vs ghayr
Notice that essential attributes like existence are identical (ayn) with essence, while active attributes are other (ghayr). Negative and relational attributes are neither identical nor additional but are accounted for differently.
This distinction is crucial in later theological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبإضافة الصفات إلى الذات خرجت السلبيَّة: كليس بمُركَّب، والإضافيَّة: كقبل العالم، والفعليَّة: كالإحياء والإماتة عند الأشاعرة؛ فإنَّها غير، والنَّفسيَّة أيضاً كالوجود فإنَّها عين.
And by adding the attributes to the Essence, there result: the negative attribute, like 'not composite'; the relational attribute, like 'before the world'; the active attribute, like 'giving life' and 'causing death' according to the Ash'aris; for they are other than the essence; and also the essential attribute, like existence, for it is identical with the essence.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a classification of attributes based on their relation to the divine essence. The author states that when attributes are considered in relation to the essence, four categories emerge. First, the negative attribute (al-salbiyya) which negates imperfections, e.g., 'not composite'. Second, the relational attribute (al-idafiyya) which expresses a relation, e.g., 'before the world'. Third, the active attribute (al-fi'liyya) which denotes an action, e.g., 'giving life' and 'causing death' according to the Ash'aris; these are other than the essence (ghayr). Fourth, the essential attribute (al-nafsiyya) like existence (al-wujud), which is identical with the essence ('ayn). Thus, negative and relational attributes are not additional to essence, active attributes are distinct (ghayr), and essential attributes are identical.

Key Terms

الإضافيَّة
الفعليَّة
النَّفسيَّة
عين
غير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine attributes are identical with Allah's essence; none are distinct.
Correction: This classification shows that active attributes (like giving life) are considered 'other than the essence' by the Ash'aris, while essential attributes (like existence) are identical. Negative and relational attributes are neither identical nor additional but are modes of description.

Checkpoint

A) Negative attribute
B) Relational attribute
C) Active attribute
D) Essential attribute

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are classified as negative, relational, active, or essential, with different relations to the essence: active attributes are other than the essence, while essential attributes are identical.

This lesson forces you to ask: Are you truly negating all imperfections, or is your mind crafting a composite picture despite the negation? What step will you take today to purify your concept of Allah?

Distinction between attributes of essence and attributes of action
Al-Qari defines and distinguishes eternal attributes of essence from temporal attributes of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key difference between attributes of essence (eternal) and attributes of action (temporal) according to the Ash'aris.

Core Idea
Attributes of essence are in the essence; attributes of action are derived from external acts.

This lesson introduces a fundamental distinction in Ash'ari theology: the difference between attributes that inhere in the divine essence eternally and attributes that are derived from God's temporal acts. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting names like 'Creator' and 'Provider'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: first a general statement, then a breakdown of the two types.
The passage is concise but precise.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage defines 'attributes of essence' and 'attributes of action'. For each, note the criteria: whether the meaning is internal or external.
Attributes of essence are described as 'qāma bihā' (established in it) or derived from that. Attributes of action are derived from a meaning external to the essence.
3
Understand the derivation principle
Notice that attributes of essence are exemplified by 'knowledge' and 'knowing'—both are connected to the essence. Attributes of action like 'Creator' come from 'creation', an act external to the essence.
This shows that names of God can be either essential or derived from actions.
4
Connect to Ash'ari doctrine
The passage states this is according to the Ash'aris. Reflect on why this matters: it preserves God's eternity while affirming His actions in time.
This distinction helps avoid attributing change to God's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفَرقُ بين صفاتِ الذَّات القديمة عند الأشاعرة وصفات الفعل الحادثة: صفات الذات ما قام بها، أو اشْتُقَّ مِن معنى قائم بها، كالعلم وعالم، وصفة الفعل: ما اشتُقَّ مِن معنى خارج عنها، كخالق ورازق؛ فإنَّهما مِن الخلق والرّزق.
And the difference between the eternal attributes of the essence according to the Ash'aris and the temporal attributes of action: attributes of the essence are what is established in it, or derived from a meaning established in it, like knowledge and knowing; and the attribute of action is what is derived from a meaning external to it, like Creator and Provider; for they are from creation and provision.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between two types of attributes: attributes of essence (sifat al-dhat) which are eternal, and attributes of action (sifat al-fi'l) which are temporal (haditha) according to the Ash'aris. The author explains that attributes of essence are those that are established in the essence itself, or derived from a meaning that subsists in the essence, such as knowledge and the attribute 'knowing'. Attributes of action, on the other hand, are derived from a meaning external to the essence, such as 'Creator' derived from creation, and 'Provider' derived from provision. Thus, the attribute of action is not a permanent description of the essence but is temporally acquired.

Key Terms

صفات الفعل
قديمة
حادثة
اشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'Creator' is an eternal attribute of essence like knowledge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'Creator' is derived from the temporal act of creation, so it is an attribute of action, not of essence.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes attributes of essence from attributes of action?
A) Attributes of essence are derived from an external meaning
B) Attributes of action are derived from a meaning in the essence
C) Attributes of essence are derived from a meaning in the essence
D) They are the same

Key Takeaway
Attributes of essence are eternal and derived from within the essence, while attributes of action are temporal and derived from external acts.

Attributes of Essence vs. Attributes of Action
Al-Sharqawi defines and distinguishes two categories of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari distinction between attributes that subsist in Allah's essence (eternal) and those derived from external acts (temporal).

Core Idea
Attributes of essence are inherent; attributes of action are derived from acts.

Al-Sharqawi begins by clarifying a crucial classification in Ash'ari theology: the division of divine attributes into two types. This distinction affects how we understand God's eternal nature and His relationship with creation. The passage gives clear definitions with examples.

Commentary

Classification of Divine Positive Attributes by Relation
Sanusi categorizes positive attributes into related and unrelated, then divides attributes of meanings into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of Allah's positive attributes into related and unrelated, and the four divisions of attributes of meanings based on their relation.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are categorized by relation to creation; only life has no relation.

After establishing that Allah's attributes are eternal and subsist in His essence, Sanusi now classifies them in terms of their relation to created things. This classification helps clarify how attributes like knowledge and power differ in scope. The passage presents a bipartite division first, then a fourfold division of the attributes of meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the bipartite division
Read the first sentence and notice that attributes are divided into related (mutaʿalliq) and unrelated (ghayr mutaʿalliq). Note the definition of each.
The related requires an additional matter beyond subsistence in its locus.
2
Understand the four divisions of attributes of meanings
The passage then states that attributes of meanings are divided into four types based on relation. List each: life (no relation), knowledge and speech (relate to all things), power and will (relate only to possibles), hearing, sight, perception (relate to existents).
Notice the different modes of relation for each: disclosure, indication, creation/annihilation, specification.
3
Reflect on the subtlety of these relations
The passage concludes that knowledge of these relations is not obligatory for every legally responsible person because it is subtle. Consider why this might be.
This is a reminder that not all theological details are required belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنَّ الصِّفات الثبوتية (٤) قسمان: متعلّق ، وغير متعلّق (۱) ، وضابط الأول: ما يقتضي أمرًا زائدا على القيام بمحلها. وغير المتعلق: ما لا يقتضي ذلك. والأمر الثاني: أنَّ صفات المعاني من حيث التَّعلق وعدمه، ومن حيثُ عموم التعلق أو خصوصه تنقسم إلى أربعة أقسام: القسم الأول: ما لا يتعلّق بشيءٍ أصلا وهو صفة الحياة، وقد سبق لنا بيان السرّ في ذلك . والقسم الثاني : ما يتعلّق بجميع الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات، وهما صفتان: العلم والكلام، لكن تعلق العلم بما ذكر تعلّقُ انكشاف، وتعلّق الكلام بما ذكر تعلّق دلالة . والقسم الثالث : ما يتعلق بالممكنات دون الواجبات والمستحيلات وهما صفتان: القدرة والإرادة، لكن تعلق القدرة بالممكنات تعلق إيجاد وإعدام، وتعلق الإرادة بالممكنات تعلق تخصيص. والقسم الرابع : ما يتعلق بالموجودات ، وهو ثلاث صفات ، اثنتان متفق عليهما ، والثالثة مختلف فيها على ما تقدَّم بيانه ، فأمَّا المتّفق عليهما فهما : السمع والبصر، والمختلف فيها: الإدراك. واعلم أنَّ معرفة التعلقات ليست ممَّا يجب على كل مكلف ؛ لأنَّها من غوامض هذا العلم التي تخفى على بعض العقول.
Know that the positive attributes are two divisions: related and unrelated. The rule of the first: it requires an additional matter beyond subsistence in its locus. The unrelated: does not require that. And the second matter: that the attributes of meanings, from the perspective of relation and lack thereof, and from the perspective of generality or specificity of relation, are divided into four divisions: The first division: what does not relate to anything at all, and it is the attribute of life, and we have previously explained the reason for that. The second division: what relates to all obligatory, contingent, and impossible things, and they are two attributes: knowledge and speech, but the relation of knowledge to what was mentioned is a relation of disclosure, and the relation of speech to what was mentioned is a relation of indication. The third division: what relates to possible things without the obligatory and impossible, and they are two attributes: power and will, but the relation of power to possibles is a relation of creation and annihilation, and the relation of will to possibles is a relation of specification. The fourth division: what relates to existing things, and it is three attributes, two agreed upon, and the third disputed as previously explained. As for the two agreed upon, they are hearing and sight, and the disputed is perception. And know that knowledge of the relations is not obligatory upon every legally responsible person because it is among the subtleties of this science that are hidden from some minds.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage classifies the divine attributes into two main categories: related (mutaʿalliq) and unrelated (ghayr mutaʿalliq). It first defines the related as that which requires an additional matter beyond subsistence in its locus (i.e., the essence). Then it further divides the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) into four types based on taʿalluq: (1) Life, which has no relation; (2) Knowledge and Speech, which relate to all things (knowledge by disclosure, speech by indication); (3) Power and Will, which relate only to possibles (power by creation/annihilation, will by specification); (4) Hearing and Sight (agreed) and Perception (disputed), which relate to existing things. Finally, it notes that knowledge of these relations is not a general obligation due to their subtlety.

The Criterion of Talluq and the True Scope of Relational Attributes
Definition of talluq and first matter on its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of talluq, the criterion for which divine attributes have it, and why only the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) possess talluq.

Core Idea
Talluq requires an attribute to entail a secondary object beyond itself.

This lesson lays the foundation for understanding how divine attributes relate to their objects. The author begins by defining what it means for an attribute to 'relate' (talluq) and then clarifies a critical point: only the attributes of meanings, not the mental attributes, have this relation. This distinction prevents absurdities like multiple effective agents on a single effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the definition of the criterion.
2
Understand the criterion
Identify the phrase 'ما يقتضي أمرًا زائدا على القيام بمحلها' — the attribute requires an additional matter beyond its locus.
3
Identify the first matter
The first matter states that only ṣifāt al-maʿānī have talluq. Note the refutation of the alternative view.
4
Reflect on the arguments
Why would attributing talluq to mental attributes lead to absurdity? The passage mentions union of two effective agents and taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil.

Source Text

ضابط المُتعلّق وغير المتعلق من الصفات وغير متعلّق (١) ، وضابط الأول: ما يقتضي أمرًا زائدا على القيام بمحلها (٢) ، (١) في كلام الشارح الله هنا بيان معنى التعلق وفيما قدَّمناه لك مع كل صفة من المباحث ما تتعلق به كل صفة من الصفات التي تتعلق وأنواع تعلقها ، ويقي مما يصح أن نذكره ههنا أمران: أحدهما: أنَّ الذي اعتمده محققو المتكلمين وتبعهم النَّاظم والشارح وجرينا عليه في المباحث السَّابقة أنَّ الصِّفات التي لها تعلّق هي صفات المعاني وحدها ، ومن المتكلمين من ذهب إلى أنَّ الصفات التي تتعلق هي الصفات المعنوية وحدها ، ولم يذهب أحد إلى أن المتعلق صفاتُ المعاني والصفات المعنوية جميعاً ، وذلك مِن قِبل أنَّ القول بذلك يستلزم اجتماع مؤثرين على أثر واحدٍ في القدرة والكون قادراً ، وفي الإرادة والكون مريدا، كما يلزم عليه تحصيل الحاصل في العلم والكون عالماً . والأمر الثاني: أنَّ صفات المعاني من حيث التَّعلق وعدمه، ومن حيثُ عموم التعلق أو خصوصه تنقسم إلى أربعة أقسام: القسم الأول: ما لا يتعلّق بشيءٍ أصلا وهو صفة الحياة، وقد سبق لنا بيان السرّ في ذلك . والقسم الثاني : ما يتعلّق بجميع الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات، وهما صفتان: العلم والكلام، لكن تعلق العلم بما ذكر تعلّقُ انكشاف، وتعلّق الكلام بما ذكر تعلّق دلالة ، وقد بيَّنا مع كل صفة منهما ذلك. والقسم الثالث : ما يتعلق بالممكنات دون الواجبات والمستحيلات وهما صفتان: القدرة والإرادة ، لكن تعلق القدرة بالممكنات تعلق إيجاد وإعدام، وتعلق الإرادة بالممكنات تعلق تخصيص، وقد بيّنا مع كلّ صفة منهما دليل عدم تعلقها بالواجبات والمستحيلات تفصيلا لما ذكره الشارح هنا. واعلم والقسم الرابع : ما يتعلق بالموجودات ، وهو ثلاث صفات ، اثنتان متفق عليهما ، والثالثة مختلف فيها على ما تقدَّم بيانه ، فأمَّا المتّفق عليهما فهما : السمع والبصر، والمختلف فيها: الإدراك. أنَّ معرفة التعلقات ليست ممَّا يجب على كل مكلف ؛ لأنَّها من غوامض هذا العلم التي تخفى على بعض العقول، وإذ قد تقدم ذكر أنواع التَّعلقات مع كل صفة.. فلن نتعرض للكلام على ما ذكره المصنِّفُ والشَّارحُ في هذا الموضع (٢) قوله: (بمحلها) الأليق بمقام الألوهية بموصوفها ، أو نحو ذلك ، ولا يعجبني التعبير بالمحل. الأمير. مخافة التكرار. محي. قسم الإلهيات كالقُدرة ؛ فإنَّها تقتضي مَقْدُورًا يتأتى بها إيجاده وإعدامه، والإرادة ؛ فإنَّها تقتضي مُراد (١) يَتَخَصَّصُ (٢) بها ، والعلم ؛ فإنَّه يقتضي معلوما ينكشف به، لذاته (٣) والكلام ؛ فإنه يقتضي معنى يدلّ عليه، والسَّمع ؛ فإنَّه يقتضي لذاته مسموعاً يُسمَع به، والبصر ؛ فإنَّه يقتضي لذاته مُبْصَرًا يُبْصَر به. وضابط ما لا يتعلّق ما لا يقتضى أمرًا زائدا على قيامها بمحلها، كالحياة (٤) ، فإنَّها صفةٌ مُصححة للإدراك كما يأتي(٥) والمُتعلّق إما أن يتعلَّق بجميع أقسام الحكم العقلي كالعلم والكلام ، أو ببعضها كالقدرة والإرادة بالممكن فقط، والسمع، والبصر، والإدراكِ بالواجب والجائز الموجود (٦) .
The criterion for the related and the unrelated of the attributes, and the unrelated (1). The criterion of the first: that which requires an additional matter beyond subsisting in its locus (2). (1) In the statement of the commentator, may Allah have mercy on him, there is an explanation of the meaning of talluq and what we have presented to you with each attribute of the investigations concerning what each attribute that has talluq relates to and the types of its talluq. Two matters are valid for us to mention here: one of them: that which the verifying scholars of the mutakallimūn relied upon, and the author of the poem and the commentator followed them, and we have proceeded upon it in the previous investigations, is that the attributes which have talluq are exclusively the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī). Among the mutakallimūn is one who held that the attributes which have talluq are exclusively the mental attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya). No one held that the related are both the attributes of meanings and the mental attributes together, because saying that necessitates the union of two effective agents upon a single effect in power and being powerful, and in will and being willing; similarly, it necessitates the acquisition of the already acquired (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) in knowledge and being knowing. The second matter: that the attributes of meanings, with respect to having talluq or not, and with respect to the generality or specificity of talluq, are divided into four sections: The first section: that which does not relate to anything at all, which is the attribute of life; we have previously explained the reason for that. The second section: that which relates to all necessary, possible, and impossible things, and they are two attributes: knowledge and speech. However, the talluq of knowledge to what is mentioned is a talluq of disclosure (inkishāf), and the talluq of speech to what is mentioned is a talluq of indication (dalāla). We have explained that with each attribute. The third section: that which relates to possible things (mumkināt) exclusive of necessary and impossible things, and they are two attributes: power and will. However, the talluq of power to possible things is a talluq of bringing into existence and annihilating (ījād wa iʿdām), and the talluq of will to possible things is a talluq of specification (takhṣīṣ). We have explained with each attribute the evidence for their not relating to necessary and impossible things in detail as per what the commentator mentioned here. And know: The fourth section: that which relates to existent things (mawjūdāt), and they are three attributes, two of them agreed upon, and the third differed about, as has preceded its explanation. As for the two agreed upon, they are hearing and sight, and the differed one is perception (idrāk). Knowledge of the talluqs is not obligatory upon every legally responsible person, because it is among the subtleties of this science that are hidden from some minds. Since the types of talluq have been mentioned with each attribute, we will not engage in discussion of what the author and commentator mentioned in this place. (2) His statement: 'in its locus' — the more appropriate for the station of divinity is 'in its described one' or the like, and I do not like the expression 'locus'. Al-Amir. Out of fear of repetition. Muhyi. Section of Divine Matters: Like power, for it requires an object of power (maqdūr) by which its bringing into existence and annihilating can be effected; and will, for it requires an object of will (murād) that is specified (2) by it; and knowledge, for it requires an object of knowledge (maʿlūm) which is disclosed by it, due to its essence (3); and speech, for it requires a meaning (maʿnā) upon which it indicates; and hearing, for it requires due to its essence a thing heard (masmūʿ) which is heard by it; and sight, for it requires due to its essence a thing seen (mubṣar) which is seen by it. And the criterion of what does not relate is that which does not require an additional matter beyond subsisting in its locus, like life (4), for it is an attribute that renders perception sound (muṣaḥḥiḥa li-l-idrāk) as will come (5). And the related is either that which relates to all divisions of rational judgment (aqsām al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī) like knowledge and speech, or to some of them, like power and will relate only to the possible, and hearing, sight, and perception relate to the necessary and the possible that exists (6).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies divine attributes based on whether they have 'talluq' (relation/connection) to an object beyond themselves. It begins by stating the criterion: an attribute that requires an additional matter (like power requiring an object of power) has talluq; one that does not (like life) does not. The author then clarifies two important points. First, the correct position is that only the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī: life, knowledge, will, power, hearing, sight, speech, and perception) have talluq, not the mental attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyya: being powerful, etc.). He refutes the idea that both categories could have talluq, as that would lead to absurdities like two effective agents on one effect or acquiring the already acquired. Second, he divides the attributes of meanings into four groups based on the scope of their talluq: (1) Life has no talluq at all. (2) Knowledge and speech relate to all necessary, possible, and impossible things—knowledge by disclosure, speech by indication. (3) Power and will relate only to possible things—power by creating and annihilating, will by specification. (4) Hearing, sight, and perception relate only to existent things (necessary and possible that are actual). The passage also notes that knowledge of these talluqs is not obligatory for every legally responsible person due to its subtlety. The author then repeats the criterion with examples and summarizes that the related attributes either relate to all rational judgments (knowledge, speech) or to a subset (power/will to possible only; hearing/sight/perception to existent only). The marginal notes address the expression 'locus' and other clarifications.

Key Terms
تعلق (talluq)
صفات المعاني (ṣifāt al-maʿānī)
صفات معنوية (ṣifāt maʿnawiyya)
تحصيل الحاصل (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that mental attributes (like being powerful) also have talluq.
Correction: Attributing talluq to mental attributes leads to theological absurdities, such as multiple effective agents on a single effect.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would be the problem if both ṣifāt al-maʿānī and ṣifāt maʿnawiyya were said to have talluq?
A) It would lead to the union of two effective agents on a single effect.
B) It would lead to the acquisition of the already acquired.
C) Both A and B.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The essential insight: talluq is confined to ṣifāt al-maʿānī because extending it to mental attributes leads to theological inconsistencies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's power, do you ever fall into thinking of it as a separate entity 'alongside' God? How does the criterion of talluq help you avoid that mistake?
?
Are you willing to subject your beliefs about God's attributes to the same logical scrutiny the mutakallimūn used? If not, what holds you back?
?
This lesson shows that confusing attribute types leads to absurdity. In your own life, where might you be tolerating theological confusion that needs correction?

Divine Attributes
beginner
6 min
The Fourfold Classification of Divine Attributes by Talluq
Classification of ṣifāt al-maʿānī into four sections based on talluq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of the attributes of meanings based on the scope and mode of their talluq.

Core Idea
Attributes of meanings vary in what they relate to and how they relate.

After establishing the criterion, the author now classifies the attributes of meanings into four distinct categories based on whether they have talluq, and if so, to what objects and in what manner. This classification reveals the precise relationships between divine attributes and their objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage that describes the four sections, and the English translation.
2
Identify each section
Note the four sections: life (no talluq), knowledge/speech (all rational judgments), power/will (possible only), hearing/sight/perception (existent only).
3
Note the mode of talluq
For each section, note how the talluq is described: disclosure, indication, creation/annihilation, specification.
4
Understand the note on obligation
The passage ends by noting that knowledge of talluqs is not obligatory for all, as it is subtle.

The criterion for the related and the unrelated of the attributes, and the unrelated (1). The criterion of the first: that which requires an additional matter beyond subsisting in its locus (2). Among the mutakallimūn is one who held that the attributes which have talluq are exclusively the mental attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya). We have explained that with each attribute. We have explained with each attribute the evidence for their not relating to necessary and impossible things in detail as per what the commentator mentioned here. Out of fear of repetition. Muhyi. Section of Divine Matters: Like power, for it requires an object of power (maqdūr) by which its bringing into existence and annihilating can be effected; and will, for it requires an object of will (murād) that is specified (2) by it; and knowledge, for it requires an object of knowledge (maʿlūm) which is disclosed by it, due to its essence (3); and speech, for it requires a meaning (maʿnā) upon which it indicates; and hearing, for it requires due to its essence a thing heard (masmūʿ) which is heard by it; and sight, for it requires due to its essence a thing seen (mubṣar) which is seen by it. Commentary
This passage defines and classifies divine attributes based on whether they have 'talluq' (relation/connection) to an object beyond themselves. (2) Knowledge and speech relate to all necessary, possible, and impossible things—knowledge by disclosure, speech by indication. (3) Power and will relate only to possible things—power by creating and annihilating, will by specification. (4) Hearing, sight, and perception relate only to existent things (necessary and possible that are actual). The passage also notes that knowledge of these talluqs is not obligatory for every legally responsible person due to its subtlety. The author then repeats the criterion with examples and summarizes that the related attributes either relate to all rational judgments (knowledge, speech) or to a subset (power/will to possible only; hearing/sight/perception to existent only). The marginal notes address the expression 'locus' and other clarifications.

Key Terms
انكشاف (inkishāf)

إيجاد وإعدام (ījād wa iʿdām)

إدراك (idrāk)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that life also has talluq, like the other attributes of meanings.
Correction: Life is explicitly stated to have no talluq; it is a condition for perception.

Checkpoint
Which of the following attributes relate to all necessary, possible, and impossible things?
A) Power and will
B) Knowledge and speech
C) Hearing and sight
D) Life and perception

Key Takeaway
The attributes of meanings are precisely divided into four categories based on the range and mode of their relation to objects, providing a clear taxonomy of divine relational properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's life, do you ever imagine it 'relating' to something or depending on something? After learning that life has no ṭalluq at all, how must you adjust your understanding of God's self-sufficiency? What false attachments in your own life are you willing to sever to reflect that attribute?
?
The lesson says God's power relates only to possible things, not to necessary or impossible ones. Does this challenge your view of God's omnipotence? In what area of your life do you need to trust God's specific ability to bring about or remove possibilities, rather than imagining He can do literally anything you conceive?
?
Hearing and sight are limited to existent things. When you feel unseen or unheard by people, does this lesson remind you that God’s hearing and sight are actual and present for all that exists? How can you live today in the awareness that you are fully known and witnessed by the One whose perception is perfect?

Attributes Requiring an Object vs. Those That Do Not
Al-Shanwani enumerates attributes that require an object and states the criterion for those that do not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between divine attributes that require an external object and those that do not, and which attributes fall into each category.

Core Idea
Some divine attributes necessitate an object; life is the exception.

Al-Shanwani begins by clarifying a key classification among the divine attributes: some attributes inherently require an external object (like power requires something powered), while others subsist without such a relation. This passage lists the six attributes that require an object and the one that does not, setting the foundation for understanding how we speak about God’s attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall classification.
Notice the six examples of attributes that require an object.
2
Identify the six attributes with objects
Find power, will, knowledge, speech, hearing, and sight. For each, note what object it necessitates (e.g., power necessitates a thing-powered).
The marginal notes clarify that will's object is eternal but not actualized in eternity.
3
Understand the criterion for attributes without object
Read the definition: attributes that do not necessitate an additional thing beyond subsisting in their locus. Life is the example.
Life only makes perception sound; it does not require a separate object.
4
Reflect on the exception
Ask why life is singled out. The commentary explains that life is unique among the seven attributes of meanings in not requiring an object.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالقُدرة ؛ فإنَّها تقتضي مَقْدُورًا يتأتى بها إيجاده وإعدامه، والإرادة ؛ فإنَّها تقتضي مُراد يَتَخَصَّص بها ، والعلم ؛ فإنَّه يقتضي معلوما ينكشف به، لذاته والكلام ؛ فإنه يقتضي معنى يدلّ عليه، والسَّمع ؛ فإنَّه يقتضي لذاته مسموعاً يُسمَع به، والبصر ؛ فإنَّه يقتضي لذاته مُبْصَرًا يُبْصَر به. وضابط ما لا يتعلّق ما لا يقتضى أمرًا زائدا على قيامها بمحلها، كالحياة ، فإنَّها صفةٌ مُصححة للإدراك كما يأتي

(1) قوله: (تقتضي مرادا) أي: تستلزم مرادا أزلا ، لما أن لها تعلقا تنجيزيا قديما، ولا يلزم من قدمها قدمه ؛ لأنه لا يلزم من إرادته الشيء حصوله بالفعل، إذ هو مستحيل أزلا . الشنواني.

Like power: it necessitates a thing-powereed (maqdoor) through which it brings about existence and nonexistence; and will: it necessitates an object of will (muraad) that becomes specified by it; and knowledge: it necessitates a known thing (maloom) that is disclosed by it, for itself; and speech: it necessitates a meaning that it indicates; and hearing: it necessitates, for itself, a heard thing that is heard by it; and sight: it necessitates, for itself, a seen thing that is seen by it. And the criterion for that which does not [require an object] is that which does not necessitate an additional thing beyond its subsisting in its locus, like life: it is a quality that makes perception sound, as will come.

(1) His words: (necessitates an object of will) means it entails an eternal object of will, because it has an eternal determinate relation, and its eternity does not necessitate its object's eternity; for it does not follow from His willing a thing that it actually occurs, since that is impossible in eternity. (Al-Shanwani)
(2) In a copy: (is specified).
(3) His words: (for itself) means not for another. (Al-Shanwani)
(4) His words: (like life) the kaaf is inclusive; if by the attributes are meant the seven attributes of meanings, since there is no other attribute that does not require an object besides life, or if pre-eternity, everlastingness, and existence are included as meanings, as the commentator will transmit, although the preponderant view is otherwise. (Al-Amir and Al-Shanwani)
(5) His words: (it is a quality that makes perception sound) this is not appropriate here; rather, he should say: it does not seek an additional thing beyond its subsisting in the essence, unless one says: the meaning is that it only makes perception sound and does not necessitate an additional thing. (Al-Amir)
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between attributes that require an external object (tata'allaq) and those that do not. It begins by listing six attributes of meanings that require an object: power necessitates a thing-powereed (existence and non-existence); will necessitates an object of will that becomes specified by it; knowledge necessitates a known thing disclosed; speech necessitates a meaning indicated; hearing necessitates a heard thing; sight necessitates a seen thing. The marginal notes clarify that will requires an eternal object but not its actual occurrence; 'for itself' excludes accidental relations; life is unique among the seven attributes in not requiring an additional object. The passage then states the criterion: what does not require an object is that which does not necessitate an additional thing beyond its subsisting in its locus. Life is given as an example—it only makes perception sound, not requiring a separate object. The notes discuss whether pre-eternity, everlastingness, and existence should be considered attributes with no object, and the commentator al-Amir suggests the wording about perception is not ideal.

Attributes and Their Objects
Classification of divine attributes by object scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine attributes are classified by the scope of their objects (muta'allaq) into those that encompass all rational judgments and those that are restricted to specific categories.

Core Idea
Attributes differ in scope: some relate to all things, others only to possible or existing beings.

Al-Shanwani provides a precise classification of divine attributes according to the types of beings they relate to. This helps clarify why, for example, Allah's power does not apply to necessary or impossible things but only to possibles. The passage also includes a subtle point about perception and non-existent things, showing the careful reasoning in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the terms for the three rational judgments (necessary, impossible, possible) and how they appear in the classification.
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the two categories of attributes
The passage divides attributes into two groups: those that relate to all three rational judgments (knowledge and speech) and those that relate to only some (power/will relate only to possible; hearing/sight/perception relate to necessary and existing contingent). List which attributes fall into each group.
Notice that power and will exclude necessary and impossible objects.
3
Analyze the marginal note on 'existing contingent'
Al-Amir's note clarifies that 'existing' (al-mawjud) refers to the contingent, but can also be read as referring to the necessary, in order to exclude non-existent necessary entities (like the absence of a partner). This shows that hearing, sight, and perception only pertain to existent beings, not pure non-existence.
This nuance prevents misunderstanding that Allah perceives non-entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمُتعلّق إما أن يتعلَّق بجميع أقسام الحكم العقلي كالعلم والكلام ، أو ببعضها كالقدرة والإرادة بالممكن فقط، والسمع، والبصر، والإدراكِ بالواجب والجائز الموجود .

(6) قوله : (الموجود) راجع للجائز، ولك أن ترجعه للواجب أيضا ؛ ليخرج الواجب العدمي، كانتفاء الشريك، فإنّه لا ولا يسمع يبصر ولا يدرك ؛ إذ هو عدم محض، نعم يعلم. الأمير.
And the object [of an attribute] either relates to all divisions of rational judgment (al-hukm al-'aqli), like knowledge and speech, or to some of them, like power and will relate only to the possible (mumkin), and hearing, sight, and perception relate to the necessary (wajib) and the existing contingent (jaa'iz al-mawjud).

(6) His words: (the existing) refers back to the contingent, but you may also refer it back to the necessary, to exclude the non-existent necessary, like the non-existence of a partner, for it is not heard, seen, nor perceived, since it is pure non-existence; yes, it is known. (Al-Amir)
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage classifies the attributes of meanings according to the scope of their objects (al-muta'allaq). It divides them into two types: first, those that relate to all divisions of rational judgment (the necessary, impossible, and possible), such as knowledge and speech; second, those that relate only to part, such as power and will which relate only to the possible (since they do not apply to the necessary or impossible), and hearing, sight, and perception which relate to the necessary and the existing contingent. The marginal note by al-Amir clarifies that 'existing' (al-mawjud) primarily refers to the contingent, but could also include the necessary to exclude non-existent things like the absence of a partner—such non-entities are not perceived but are known. This shows a careful delineation of which attributes perceive which kinds of beings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all divine attributes perceive or affect all types of beings equally, e.g., that hearing and sight apply to non-existent things.
Correction: Hearing, sight, and perception only apply to existing beings (necessary and contingent). Non-existent entities, like the absence of a partner, are not heard or seen, only known.

Checkpoint

A) Power and will
B) Knowledge and speech
C) Hearing and sight
D) Life and power

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes vary in what they relate to: some encompass all things, others are restricted to specific categories like possible or existing beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that Allah's power and will apply only to what is possible—things that can be or not be. Your very existence is a possibility that He actualized. When you face a trial today, will you remember that it is within His power and will, and that He could have made you non-existent? How will this thought change your reaction to difficulty?
?
Hearing, sight, and perception belong only to what exists—necessary or contingent. Now consider that the 'non-existence of a partner' is not perceived, only known. Does your heart truly know that false gods are nothing, or do you give them weight by fearing them or relying on them? Pledge to correct one area where you might be treating non-entities as real.
?
If knowledge encompasses all things—even impossibilities and non-existents—then Allah knows every thought you have ever had, including those you are ashamed of. How does this comprehensive knowledge affect your intention when you perform an act of worship? What will you do to ensure your heart is aligned with His knowledge?

The Attachment of Divine Power to Possibles
Commentator defines 'possible' and explains why divine power excludes necessary and impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power attaches only to possible beings, not to the necessary or impossible, and the logical necessity of this restriction.

Core Idea
Divine power creates effects; only possibles can be effects.

In this passage, the commentator explains a crucial aspect of divine power (qudra): it attaches to every possible being (mumkin) and excludes the necessary (wajib) and the impossible (mustahil). This is not arbitrary but grounded in the nature of power as an effecting attribute. Before reading the text, it helps to grasp the key concepts of possibility, necessity, and impossibility in Islamic theology. The passage builds on these categories to show that only possibles can be the objects of divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the definition of 'possible'
Open the Arabic passage and its translation. Look for the definition of 'possible' (mumkin) as 'that whose existence and non-existence are not necessary, or not impossible by itself'.
Note the phrase 'by itself' (lidhatihi) – this is crucial.
2
Understand the exclusion of necessary and impossible
The commentator explains why the necessary and impossible are excluded from divine power. Find the two arguments: 'obtaining the obtained' (tahsil al-hasil) for the necessary, and 'inversion of reality' (qalb al-haqiqa) for the impossible.
Both arguments rely on the principle that power produces an effect after non-existence.
3
Grasp the concept of 'suitable attachment'
The passage ends by explaining that the attachment of power is 'suitable' (suluhiy), meaning it is eternal in pre-eternity as a suitability, not a temporal act. Reflect on how this ties into the eternal nature of God's attributes.
This avoids the notion that God's power changes or depends on creation.

Attachments of the attributes of meanings and their rulings. And its beginning is an explanation of the attachment of power and its rulings. 33 - So power attached to the possible without limit, by what it attached. And this is what he now began to explain by his saying: (So power) i.e., if you want to know the attachments of the attributes and what they are characterized by of multiplicity and unity, what is obligatory upon you to believe is that eternal power attaches (to a possible) i.e., to every possible being, and it is that whose existence and non-existence are not necessary, or (1) that whose existence and non-existence are not impossible by itself (2). So it includes (3) what cannot be brought into existence from among possibles, but not with consideration to its essence, rather with consideration to something else, such as a possible to which the knowledge of God Almighty is attached concerning its non-occurrence, like the faith of Abu Lahab for example. And the necessary and the impossible are excluded (4); and the necessary and the impossible are excluded (4) because power is an attribute that effects, and from the necessary consequence of the effect is its existence after non-existence. So that which does not admit non-existence at all, like the necessary, cannot be an effect for it, lest obtaining the obtained (1) follow; and that which does not admit existence at all (2), like the impossible, cannot be an effect for it either, lest the inversion of reality follow by the impossible becoming possible, and both are impossible (3). And his saying: (attached) is an agent of 'to a possible', i.e., a suitable attachment (4), which is the eternal attachment, meaning that in pre-eternity it is suitable for bringing into existence and annihilating, in accordance with the attachment of eternal will to them (5) in what does not... (1) His saying: 'Lest obtaining the obtained follow' i.e., if it attached to bringing it into existence, and [lest] inversion of realities [follow] if it annihilated it, because the essence of the necessary does not admit non-existence. And his saying concerning the impossible: 'Lest inversion of realities follow' i.e., if it attached to bringing into existence impossible individuals, and obtaining the obtained if it attached to annihilating it. So in the commentator's speech there is chiasmus, because he affirmed in the first what he omitted in the second, and vice versa. And our teacher, the eminent scholar Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Ahmad al-Adawi, elucidated to us that their statement: 'Inversion of realities is impossible' is objected to by the transmutation of a human into a monkey, for example. And he answered: that their statement: 'Inversion of realities is impossible' means: inversion of the divisions of rational judgment among themselves, such as the necessary becoming impossible and vice versa. So it is impossible for the essence of a human to be identical to the essence of a monkey, because of what would follow from that of one thing being two contradictory things. And transmutation is transfer from one state to another, like forms in prime matter, so it does not apply to us. Let it be contemplated. As for the embodiment of deeds at the weighing as has been said, apparently it is a representation with complete wisdom and justice; otherwise, inversion of entities necessarily requires a common element that remains in both states, like absolute substance between human and monkey, and that is not conceivable in accident and body. And if you wish, believe in such matters in general. Al-Amir, adapted. (2) His saying: (And that which does not admit existence at all) is conjunct to his saying: (So that which does not admit non-existence at all). Al-Shanwani. (3) His saying: (And both are impossible) i.e., obtaining the obtained and inversion of reality. Al-Shanwani. (4) His saying: (Suluhiyyan) with damm on the sad, derived from suluh as a verbal noun on the pattern of 'uqud; as for 'salahiyyan' with alif, it is with fatha on the sad. Al-Amir. (5) His saying: (In pre-eternity) is attached to his saying: 'suitable'. And his saying: (In accordance with the attachment) i.e., that bringing into existence and annihilating occur in accordance with. And his saying: (to them) i.e., to bringing into existence and annihilating. Al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the attachment of the divine attribute of power (qudra) to possible beings. The commentator begins by stating the verse: 'So power attached to the possible without limit, by what it attached.' He then explains the meaning of 'power' in the context of the attributes: one must believe that eternal power attaches to every possible being (mumkin). A possible being is defined as that which is not necessary in existence nor non-existence, or that which is not impossible in existence nor non-existence by itself. The restriction 'by itself' (lidhatihi) is crucial: it includes possibles that are impossible in relation to something else (like the non-occurrence of Abu Lahab's faith) and excludes the necessary and the impossible by essence. The commentator then explains why power cannot attach to the necessary: because power is an effecting attribute, and an effect must come after non-existence; the necessary does not admit non-existence, so attaching power to it would be 'obtaining the obtained' (tahsil al-hasil). Similarly, the impossible does not admit existence, so attaching power to it would be 'inversion of reality' (qalb al-haqiqah). Both are impossible. The commentator then specifies that the attachment of power is a 'suitable attachment' (talluq suluhiy), meaning it is eternal in the sense that in pre-eternity (al-azal) power is suitable for bringing into existence and annihilating, in accordance with the eternal will of God. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the main doctrinal point is clear: divine power relates only to possibles, not to the necessary nor the impossible, and that relation is an eternal suitability, not a temporal act.

Key Terms
قدرة
ممكن
واجب
مستحيل
تعلق
تعلق صلاحي
تحصيل الحاصل
قلب الحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine power can cause the necessary to not exist or the impossible to exist.
Correction: Divine power only attaches to possibles because it requires an effect after non-existence; the necessary cannot cease to exist (would be tahsil al-hasil) and the impossible cannot come to exist (would be qalb al-haqiqa).

Checkpoint
Why does divine power not attach to the impossible?
A) Because the impossible does not exist
B) Because it would require obtaining the obtained
C) Because it would lead to an inversion of reality (قلب الحقيقة)
D) Because power is not eternal

Key Takeaway
Divine power (qudra) attaches only to possible beings because it is an effecting attribute that requires a temporal effect; attaching it to the necessary or the impossible would violate logical principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in an all-powerful God, yet you often doubt whether He can change your situation. Does your doubt stem from a misunderstanding of what divine power really entails? How will you remind yourself daily that God's power is attached to every possible thing, including your circumstances?
?
The passage teaches that divine power cannot attach to the impossible. Are there desires or expectations in your life that are actually impossible (like wanting an outcome that would contradict God's decree)? How can you align your hopes with what is truly possible according to God's will?
?
God's power is eternally suitable (ta'alluq suluhiy), ready to act according to His will. Do you live with the conviction that God's power is ready to help you at any moment, or do you act as if you are left to your own devices? What specific change in your daily reliance on God will you make today?
