The Two Attachments of Divine Power and Their Proof
Al-Bajuri argues that power attaches only to possibles and defines its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power attaches only to possible beings and the distinction between its eternal suitability attachment and its temporal actualizing attachment.

Core Idea
Divine power only attaches to what can possibly exist or not, not to the necessary or impossible.

This lesson examines a foundational argument in Islamic theology about the scope of divine power. The author, al-Bajuri, demonstrates that power cannot affect the Necessary Being or the impossible, so it is confined to possible beings. He then introduces two key types of this attachment: an eternal suitability and a temporal actualization.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument against power attaching to the necessary
The passage first argues that divine power is effective and its effect must come after non-existence. The Necessary Being never lacks existence, so it cannot be an effect. Note the logical reasoning.
This is called 'تحصيل الحاصل' (actualizing the actual).
2
Read the argument against power attaching to the impossible
Similarly, the impossible can never exist, so power cannot bring it about. This would be 'قلب الحقيقة' (inversion of truth).
3
Identify the two types of attachment
After establishing that power attaches only to possibles, al-Bajuri explains the two kinds: the 'talluq suluhi' (eternal suitability) and 'talluq tanjizi' (temporal actualizing). Understand how they differ in time and function.
The suitability attachment is eternal, the actualizing is temporal.
4
Connect to the broader doctrine
Reflect on why this distinction matters for understanding God's power in relation to His will and the creation of the world.
It shows that God's power is not arbitrary but operates within rational boundaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مؤثرةٌ، ومن لازم الأثَرِ وجوده بعد عدم، فما لا يقبل العدم أصلا كالواجب لا يصحُ أنْ يكونَ أثرًا لها ؛ لئلا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل (۱) ، وما لا يقبلُ الوجود أصلا (٢) كالمستحيل لا يصح أن يكونَ أثرًا لها أيضا ؛ لئلا يلزم قلبُ الحقيقة بصَيْرُورَةِ المستحيل جائزاً ، وكلاهما محال (۳) ، وقوله : (تَعَلَّقَتْ) عامل بممكن)) أي: تعلَّقاً صُلُوحِيًّا (٤) ، وهو التَّعلُّق القديم، بمعنى أنَّها في الأزل صالحة للإيجاد والإعدام، على وفق تعلُّق الإرادة الأزليَّة بهما(ه) فيما لا يزال، وتعلقا تَنْجِيزِيَّا، وهو: التعلق الحادث المقارن(۱) لتعلق الإرادة بالحدوث الحالي(٢).
(Divine power is) effective, and from the necessary consequence of the effect is its existence after non-existence. So that which does not accept non-existence at all, like the Necessary Being, cannot be an effect of it, otherwise the actualization of what is already actual would follow (1). And that which does not accept existence at all (2), like the impossible, also cannot be an effect of it, otherwise the inversion of the truth would follow by the impossible becoming possible. Both are impossible (3). And His saying: 'attached' (تَعَلَّقَتْ) is an agent with 'possible' (بممكن) meaning: a relative attachment (تعلقا صلوحيا) (4), which is the eternal attachment, in the sense that it is, in pre-eternity, suitable for bringing into existence and bringing into non-existence, in accordance with the eternal will's attachment to them (ه) in what is perpetual, and a verifying attachment (تعلقا تنجيزيا), which is the temporal attachment concurrent (1) with the will's attachment to temporal origination (2).
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents a key argument concerning divine power (qudra). It begins by stating that power is effective, meaning it produces an effect. The effect must come after non-existence. Therefore, something that never lacks existence (like God Himself, the Necessary Being) cannot be an effect of power; otherwise we would have 'تحصيل الحاصل' (actualizing what is already actual), which is absurd. Similarly, something that can never exist (the impossible) cannot be an effect, because that would require 'قلب الحقيقة' (inverting the truth), making the impossible possible—also absurd. So power only attaches to possible beings, which are neither necessary nor impossible. Next, the commentator explains the phrase 'تعلقت' from the matn (the base text). He says it means power attaches to the possible in two ways: (1) تعلق صلوحي (relative or suitability attachment), which is eternal—power is eternally suitable to create or annihilate, in accordance with God's eternal will, in what never ceases (i.e., perpetually). (2) تعلق تنجيزي (actualizing attachment), which is temporal, arising when the will actually decrees creation at a particular moment.

Key Terms
القدرة
التعلق الصلحي
التعلق التنجيزي
الواجب
المستحيل
الممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power can change the impossible into possible.
Correction: The impossible, by its nature, cannot accept existence; power cannot attach to it because that would invert the truth.

Checkpoint
Why cannot divine power attach to the Necessary Being (الواجب)?
A) Because the Necessary Being does not accept non-existence, so making it exist would be actualizing what is already actual.
B) Because the Necessary Being is eternal and power only attaches to temporal things.
C) Because the Necessary Being is the agent, not the effect.
D) Because power is limited to the possible.

Key Takeaway
Divine power is effective only upon possible beings, through an eternal suitability attachment and a temporal actualizing attachment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that divine power attaches only to possible beings. Have you ever asked Allah for something that contradicts His laws or nature, even implicitly? How will you check your supplications against what is truly possible?
?
The eternal suitability attachment means that God's power was always ready to create you, but only when His will decided. Does that make you more patient with delays in your life, or does it challenge you to trust His timing more deeply?
?
If God cannot actualize the impossible, how does that affect your view of His omnipotence? Does it make Him seem limited, or does it reassure you that His actions are rational and trustworthy? How will you worship a God whose power is so precisely ordered?

The Generality of Divine Power Over All Possibles
Al-Bajuri proves that divine power attaches to all possible beings without limit, citing two verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine power is unlimited and extends to every possible being, as proven by the Quran.

Core Idea
God's power covers every possible thing — nothing is beyond His capability.

This passage from Al-Bajuri's commentary explains why God's power is not limited to a certain number of things. The author begins with a phrase from the matn and then expands with Quranic evidence, showing that power attaches both relatively (as suitability) and actually (as creation).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the phrase 'بلا تناهي ما' which indicates limitlessness.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that divine power is infinite in its attachments — no possible being is excluded. This is the core doctrine.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
Two verses are cited. The first shows relative attachment (power is suitable for everything), the second shows actual attachment (power creates everything). Understand the distinction.
'كل شيء' in both verses refers to all possible beings.
4
Reflect on the implication
This refutes any idea that God's power is limited. Every conceivable possible thing falls within His power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأَشار إلى عُموم تعلُّق القدرة لجميع الممكنات(٣) بقوله: (بِلَا تَناهي مَا) أي: الممكن الذي (به تَعَلَّقَتْ) (٤) بأنْ لا يَخْرُج عنها فرد منه(٥)، يعني أنَّ قدرة الله تعالى غير متناهيةِ المُتَعلَّقات (٦) ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاللَّهُ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرُ ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٨٤] وَخَلَقَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ فَقَدَّرَهُ تَقْدِيرًا ﴾ [الفرقان: ۲](٧) .
And He (the author) indicated the generality of the attachment of power to all possible beings (3) by His saying: 'with no limit of what' (بلا تناهي ما) i.e., the possible being to which it [power] is attached (4), such that no individual of it is excluded from it (5), meaning that the power of God, exalted is He, is infinite in its attachments (6); due to His saying, exalted: 'And Allah is over all things All-Powerful' [al-Baqarah 2:284] and 'And He created everything and determined it with precise determination' [al-Furqan 25:2] (7).
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage establishes the universal scope of God's power. The commentator says that the author (of the original matn) pointed to this generality by the phrase 'بلا تناهي ما' (with no limit of what), meaning that the possible beings to which power attaches are infinite—not a single possible individual is left out. This implies that God's power is unlimited in its attachments. The commentator then cites two Quranic verses: (1) 'Allah is over all things All-Powerful' (al-Baqarah 2:284), which indicates relative (suluhi) attachment—the power is always suitable; (2) 'He created everything and determined it precisely' (al-Furqan 25:2), which indicates actual (tanjizi) attachment—the power actually brings things into existence. The word 'كل شيء' (everything) in the verses refers to all possible beings (in the linguistic sense of 'thing'). This refutes any suggestion that God's power is limited to a certain number of things.

Key Terms
عموم التعلق
بلإ تناهي
القدرة غير متناهية المتعلقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's power is finite or that He cannot create certain things.
Correction: This passage confirms that God's power covers all possibles without limit, as shown by the Quranic verses stating He is powerful over all things and has created everything.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
God's power attaches to every possible being without limit, as affirmed by the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Write it down and then ask yourself: Do I truly believe that nothing is impossible for Him? What would it look like to live today with that conviction?

The Scope of Divine Power: Attached to Every Possible
Author explains the universality of divine power's attachment and cites Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's power (qudra) attaches to every possible thing without exception, and that this universality applies to both potential and actual divine acts.

Core Idea
Allah's power is infinite in scope, reaching every possible thing.

This lesson focuses on a fundamental attribute of Allah: His power (qudra). The author clarifies that divine power is not limited to a subset of possibles; rather, it extends to every possible thing without end. The passage includes a precise explanation of the poetic phrase 'bila tanahi ma' and provides two Quranic proofs that ground this teaching.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation once to get the overall meaning. Notice the key terms: qudra, ta'alluq, mumkin.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim
What is the author asserting about divine power? It attaches to all possible things without exception.
3
Examine the proof verses
Two Quranic verses are cited. One (2:284) indicates universal potential; the other (25:2) indicates actual creation. Distinguish between these two aspects of attachment.
Potential vs. actual attachment is a key nuance.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that if only some possibles are created, power is limited. The passage shows power is unlimited in capacity even if actual creation is specific.

Source Text

وأَشار إلى عُموم تعلُّق القدرة لجميع الممكنات بقوله: (بِلَا تَناهي مَا) أي: الممكن الذي (به تَعَلَّقَتْ) بأنْ لا يَخْرُج عنها فرد منه، يعني أنَّ قدرة الله تعالى غير متناهيةِ المُتَعلَّقات ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاللَّهُ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرُ ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٨٤] وَخَلَقَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ فَقَدَّرَهُ, تَقْدِيرًا ﴾ [الفرقان: ۲].

This means that Allah's power is infinite in its attachments—it can affect every possible thing without limitation. Two Quranic verses are cited as proof: first, 'Allah is over all things All-Powerful' indicates universal potential (t´allaq salihi); second, 'He created everything and determined it with exact determination' indicates actual creation (t´allaq tanziji). The distinction between the two types of attachment is implied: one is the potential to create any possible, the other is the actual creation of specific things in time.

The Unity of Divine Power
The author affirms that divine power is one and explains the reason for its unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power must be one despite the multiplicity of its objects, and how its attachments differ according to states.

Core Idea
One divine power, infinite effects.

This lesson examines a key attribute of Allah: power (qudra). The author, Sanusi, affirms that divine power is single and not multiple, even though it applies to countless objects. He also explains that the specific relations (ta‘alluqāt) of power vary with each object. This teaching guards against the error of multiplicity in the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words establish the claim.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage begins with an imperative 'And unity: obligate for it.' Identify what is being obligated and for whom.
3
Understand the reason
The text gives a reason: 'due to the necessity of fleeing from multiplicity of eternal entities.' Grasp why this reason is critical in Islamic theology.
This goes to the heart of tawhid.
4
Note the qualification
The author adds that 'its attachments must differ.' This nuance shows that unity of power does not mean uniformity in relations.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this argument safeguards monotheism by preventing any multiplicity in the divine essence while explaining the diversity of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَوَحْدَةً أَوْجِبْ لَهَا أي: للقدرة ، يعني أنَّ ممَّا يجب لصفة القدرة مِن غير خلاف عندنا أنَّها واحدة لا تتعدّد، وإن تعدّد مَقدُورها وتَبايَنتْ أحواله ، نعم ؛ يجب لتعلقاتها أن تختلف بحسب اختلاف تلك الأحوال ؛ لوجوب الفرار من تعدُّد القدماء .
And unity: obligate for it — i.e., for power, meaning that among the things necessary for the attribute of power, without disagreement among us, is that it is one, not multiple, even if its objects of power are multiple and their states are diverse. Yes, its attachments must differ according to the difference of those states, due to the necessity of fleeing from multiplicity of eternal entities.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the unity of divine power. It cites the verse from the poem 'And unity: obligate for it' meaning that one must affirm that power is one. The commentary explains that it is a necessary attribute of power, without disagreement among the Asharis, that it is single and not multiple, even though the objects of power (maqdurat) are many and their states (ahwal) vary. The reason for this unity is the need to avoid multiplicity of eternal entities (ta'addud al-qudama'). If power were multiple, that would imply multiple eternal entities, which is impossible. The passage also notes that while power itself is one, its attachments (t´allaqat) must differ according to the differences in the objects, because each object requires a specific relation.

Key Terms
وحدة (wahda)
قدماء (qidam)
تعلق (ta‘alluq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God needs multiple powers to create multiple things.
Correction: One divine power can create all things; multiplicity of effects does not imply multiplicity of power.

Checkpoint

A) Because its objects are one.
B) To avoid multiplicity of eternal entities.
C) Because the poem says so.
D) Because power is an attribute.

Key Takeaway
Divine power is one; its unity is necessary to avoid multiplicity in the eternal essence, yet its attachments vary with each object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you commit to reciting the verse 'Allah is One' (Surah Al-Ikhlas) daily with the full understanding that even His power is one?
?
If you genuinely believed that God's single power is the source of every event in your life—big and small—would you still worry about the future? What is one specific fear you will surrender to God today, trusting that His one power is enough?

Divine Will: Its Unity and Attachments
The author compares divine will to power and explains its attachments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine will is similar to power in its unity and attachments, and why the preferred view holds that will has two primordial attachments rather than a temporal one.

Core Idea
Divine will, like power, is eternal and its attachments are primordial.

In this passage, our author compares divine will to power, extending the same three characteristics—unity, eternity, and attachment. He then clarifies the nature of will's attachments, distinguishing between potential and actual, and explains why a third temporal attachment is unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Notice how the author first establishes the comparison with power.
The comparison is to the 'three matters' mentioned earlier for power.
2
Identify the two attachments
Find the two types of attachment described: primordial potential (salihi) and primordial actual (tanziji). Notice how each is defined.
The word 't´allaq' means attachment or relation.
3
Understand the rejected third attachment
Read about the reported third attachment that is temporal and actual. Understand why the author says the primordial actual suffices.
This reflects the theological principle that God's attributes do not change over time.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does this distinction matter? It safeguards divine immutability while allowing for the specification of possible events.
This connects to the broader understanding of divine predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَمِثْلُ ذِي إِرَادَةٌ) يعني: أنَّ إرادة الله تعالى مثل قدرته في جميع ما تقدم من الأمور الثلاثة المذكورة. واعلم أن الإرادة لها تعلقان صلوحي قديم وهو: صلاحيتها في الأزل لتخصيص كل ممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه وتنجيزي قديم وهو: تخصيصها أزلا الممكن ببعض ما يجوز بالفعل مضافا للوقت الذي علم أنه يوجد فيه. وقيل: إن لها تعلقا ثالثا تنجيزيا حادثا عند تعلق القدرة التعلق التنجيزي، والمُختار أن التعلق التنجيزي القديم كاف عنه .
And similar to that is will. Meaning: that the will of Allah Most High is like His power in all the three matters mentioned earlier. And know that will has two attachments: a primordial potential attachment, which is its suitability in pre-eternity to specify each possible with some of what is permissible for it, and a primordial actual attachment, which is its specification from pre-eternity of a possible with some of what is permissible in actuality, added to the time in which it is known that it will exist. And it is said that it has a third, temporal actual attachment at the time of power's actual attachment, and the chosen view is that the primordial actual attachment suffices regarding it.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage compares divine will (irada) to divine power (qudra). It states that will resembles power in all three mentioned matters—presumably unity, necessity of existence as an attribute, and having attachments. The commentary then explains the types of attachment of will. It identifies two main attachments: a potential (salahi) attachment that is eternal, meaning that will in pre-eternity has the suitability to specify each possible thing with some of its permissible states; and an actual (tanziji) attachment that is also eternal, meaning that will actually specifies a particular possible to exist at a specific time. There is a reported opinion that there is a third attachment, a temporal actual attachment that occurs at the moment when power actualizes the object. However, the preferred view (al-mukhtar) is that the primordial actual attachment is sufficient, implying that no new attachment arises in time. The text ends with the phrase 'suffices regarding it', possibly indicating the conclusion of the discussion.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
تعلق صلوحي (t´allaq salihi)
تعلق تنجيزي (t´allaq tanziji)
قديم (qadim)
حادث (hadith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine will gains new attachments over time, as if God's will changes when He decides to create something in time.
Correction: The correct belief is that will has two primordial attachments that are eternal; a third temporal attachment is unnecessary and would imply change in God.

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, how many attachments does divine will have?
A) One.
B) Two.
C) Three.
D) Four.

Key Takeaway
Divine will has two eternal attachments—potential and actual—and does not acquire a new one in time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will has already specified, from pre-eternity, every detail of your life—including the very moment you read this question—what does that mean for the anxieties you carry about tomorrow? Are you willing to truly believe that His actual attachment is sufficient, or do you secretly think something is missing that you must supply?
?
Consider an area of your life where you feel deeply uncertain: a relationship, a career path, a health concern. According to this lesson, God's will has a primordial actual attachment to that outcome—it is already firmly decided from eternity. Does that knowledge bring you peace, or does it stir discomfort at not being in control? Be honest: what would it take for you to surrender that specific outcome entirely to Him?
?
The passage rejects the idea that God needs a new 'temporal' attachment to make things happen—the eternal decision is enough. Reflect: In your own life, do you act as if God's decrees need your help to come about? Name one concrete habit you have that reveals a lack of trust in the sufficiency of God's eternal will. What would it look like to change that habit starting today?

The Scope and Unity of Divine Knowledge
Commentary explains the comprehensiveness and unity of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's knowledge encompasses all conceivable things—necessary, impossible, and possible—and is one despite the multiplicity of its objects.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge is single yet encompasses all; its unity is proven by revelation and historical consensus.

The commentator explains that Allah's knowledge is not limited; it covers everything from His own essence to impossible possibilities. This passage establishes both the comprehensiveness and the oneness of divine knowledge, using rational and scriptural proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice the structure: scope first, then unity, then proofs.
The passage is a commentary on a poetic line, so it explains and expands.
2
Identify the three categories of knowable things
The passage divides all conceivable things into three: necessary (wājib), impossible (mumtani‘), and possible (mumkin). Find the examples given for each in the text.
Examples: necessary = Allah's essence/attributes; impossible = partner; possible = the world.
3
Understand the claim of unity
Despite the immense multiplicity of objects of knowledge (info: numbers, shapes, all created things), knowledge itself is described as one, without multiplicity or plurality.
This is a key theological point: the attribute is not fragmented.
4
Examine the two proofs
The passage provides two proofs: first, from revelation (Quranic verses about Allah's comprehensive knowledge); second, from historical consensus that only one eternal knowledge has been affirmed (or denied), never multiple.
The second proof is a form of argument from unanimous omission.
5
Reflect on the common misconception
Consider why someone might mistakenly think many objects require many knowledges. The passage clarifies that oneness of the attribute is not contradicted by multiplicity of objects.
This misconception is explicitly addressed in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعَمَّ أَيْضًا وَاجِبًا والمُمْتَنِعْ ومِثْلُ ذَا كلامُهُ فَلْتَنَتَّبِع (وَ) زادَ عليهما بأنَّ (عَمَّ أَيْضًا وَاجِبًا) عقليًّا كذاته تعالى وصفاته (وَ) عمَّ أيضًا (المُمْتَنِع) العقليَّ : كشريكه تعالى ، واتّخاذه ولدًا أو صاحبةً، يعني: يجب شرعًا أنْ يُعتَقَد أَنَّ عِلْمَه تعالى غيرُ مُتَنَاءٍ مِن حيث تعلَّقه ، إِمَّا بمعنى أنَّه لا ينقطع ، وإما بمعنى أنَّه لا يصير بحيث لا يتعلَّق بالمعلوم ؛ فإنَّه يحيط بما هو غير متناه، كالأعداد والأشكال ونعيم الجنان، فهو شامل لجميع المتصوَّرات واجبةً ؛ كذاته وصفاته ، ومستحيلة ؛ كشريك له تعالى ، وممكنة ؛ كالعالم بأسره ، الجزئيَّاتُ مِن ذلك والكليَّاتُ ، ومع هذا فهو واحدٌ لا تعدُّد فيه ولا تَكَثُر ، وإنْ تعدّدت معلوماته وتكثّرت ، أمَّا وجوبُ عموم تعلَّقه سمعاً ؛ فكمثل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاللَّهُ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ﴾ [الحجرات: 16]، ﴿عَلِمُ الْغَيْبِ وَالشَّهَادَةِ﴾ [الأنعام: 73]، وأما وجوب وَحْدَته فلأنَّ النَّاس انحصروا في فريقين؛ أحدهما: أثبتَ العِلْمَ القديم مع وَحْدَتِه ، والآخر: نفاه، ولم يذهب إلى تعدد علوم قديمة أحدٌ.
And it also includes the necessary and the impossible, and like this is his speech, so let us follow. And he added to them that 'it also includes the necessary' rationally, like His essence and attributes, and 'it also includes the impossible' rationally, like His partner and His taking a child or a consort. Meaning: it is legally obligatory to believe that His knowledge, exalted is He, is not finite in terms of its relation, either in the sense that it does not cease, or in the sense that it does not become such that it does not relate to the known; for it encompasses what is infinite, like numbers, shapes, and the bliss of paradise. So it encompasses all conceivable things: necessary, like His essence and attributes; impossible, like a partner to Him; and possible, like the entire world, including particulars and universals. Despite this, it is one, with no multiplicity or plurality, even if its objects of knowledge are multiple and numerous. As for the obligation of the generality of its relation by revelation, it is like His statement: 'And Allah is of all things Knowing' (Al-Hujurat: 16) and 'Knower of the unseen and the witnessed' (Al-An'am: 73). As for the obligation of its unity, it is because people are confined to two groups: one affirmed eternal knowledge with its unity, and the other denied it, and no one went for multiple eternal knowledges.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the poem's verses about the attribute of divine knowledge. It begins by stating that divine knowledge encompasses both the rationally necessary (al-wājib al-'aqli), such as Allah's own essence and attributes, and the rationally impossible (al-mumtani' al-'aqli), such as a partner or child of Allah. The commentator explains that it is legally obligatory to believe that Allah's knowledge is not finite in its relation to its objects—either meaning that it never ceases or that it never becomes such that it fails to relate to something knowable. It encompasses infinite objects like numbers, geometric shapes, and the pleasures of Paradise. The passage then asserts that knowledge covers all conceivable things: necessary (like Allah's essence and attributes), impossible (like a partner to Him), and possible (like the entire world, including particulars and universals). Despite the vast multiplicity of known things, the knowledge itself is one, without multiplicity or plurality. The commentator then provides two proofs: first, from revelation, citing Quranic verses that declare Allah's knowledge of all things and of the unseen and witnessed. Second, from the fact that people throughout history have only affirmed either one eternal knowledge or denied it altogether; no one has ever claimed multiple eternal knowledges, thus proving its unity.

علم
واجب
ممكن
ممتنع
تعلق
وحدة العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because Allah's knowledge encompasses multiple objects, it must be multiple.
Correction: The passage clarifies that knowledge is one despite the multiplicity of its objects.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what two proofs are given for the attributes of divine knowledge discussed?
A) Rational proof from necessity and textual proof from consensus.
B) Scriptural proof from Quranic verses and historical proof from the fact that no one affirmed multiple eternal knowledges.
C) Empirical proof from creation and logical proof from the impossibility of infinite regress.
D) Proof from prophecy and proof from miracles.

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge is one, yet it encompasses all things—necessary, impossible, and possible—proven by revelation and the fact that no one has affirmed multiple eternal knowledges.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Allah’s knowledge covers every detail of your life, including what you do in secret. Is there a hidden habit or thought you would be ashamed to have exposed? What one step can you take today to align that area with His awareness?
?
You may believe in the unity of Allah’s knowledge, but do you live as though His knowledge is compartmentalized—relevant to ‘spiritual’ matters but not to your finances or relationships? Identify one domain where you forget His all-encompassing knowledge, and commit to bringing it before Him in prayer today.
?
The impossibility of a partner for Allah is part of His knowledge. Do you ever implicitly ascribe power or control to created things—people, your own efforts, fate—as if they were independent? When, in the last week, did you act as if something other than Allah had ultimate say? Repent of that and renew your reliance on Him alone.

The Comprehensiveness and Unity of Divine Knowledge
The author defines the scope of divine knowledge and proves its unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine knowledge encompasses all things (necessary, impossible, possible) and why it is one despite multiple objects.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is single and encompasses everything by necessity.

This lesson explores a foundational attribute of Allah: His knowledge. The author explains that divine knowledge covers all conceivable realities—what must be, what cannot be, and what may be—and yet remains perfectly one. We will read the Arabic passage, see its classification, and understand the proofs for comprehensiveness and unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Let the author's classification sink in before analyzing.
Notice the three categories mentioned in the first sentence.
2
Identify the three categories
Look for the terms 'wajib', 'mustahil', 'mumkin' in the Arabic. The author groups all conceived things into these three rational judgments.
These categories are fundamental in kalam.
3
Understand the two proofs
Separate the scriptural proof for comprehensiveness from the historical/rational proof for unity. The passage cites Quranic verses and then makes an argument based on scholarly consensus.
The author uses both revealed and rational evidence.

بيان تعلقات صفة العلم وأحكامها لجميع المتصورات واجبة كذاته وصفاته ومستحيلة كشريك له تعالى وممكنة كالعالم بأسره الجزئيات من ذلك والكليات ومع هذا فهو واحد لا تعدد فيه ولا تكثر وإن تعدّدت معلوماته وتكثّرت أما وجوب عموم تعلقه سمعا فكمثل قوله تعالى ﴿وَاللَّهُ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ﴾ [الحجرات: 16]، ﴿عَلِمُ الْغَيْبِ وَالشَّهَادَةِ﴾ [الأنعام: 73]، وأما وجوب وحدته فلأن الناس انحصروا في فريقين أحدهما أثبت العلم القديم مع وحدته والآخر نفاه ولم يذهب إلى تعدد علوم قديمة أحد.
Explanation of the attachments of the attribute of knowledge and its rulings: It encompasses all conceived things: necessary like His essence and attributes, impossible like a partner to Him exalted, and possible like the entire world—the particulars thereof and the universals. Despite this, it is one, no multiplicity or plurality in it, even if its known objects are multiple and numerous. As for the obligation of its comprehensiveness audibly, it is like His saying: 'And Allah is of all things Knowing' and 'Knower of the unseen and the witnessed.' As for the obligation of its unity, it is because people are divided into two groups: one affirmed eternal knowledge with its unity, and the other negated it—and no one holds that there are multiple eternal knowledges.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof concerning the divine attribute of knowledge. The author first states that knowledge attaches to all conceived things, classifying them into the three categories of rational judgments: wajib (necessary existence, e.g., God's essence and attributes), mustahil (impossible, e.g., a partner to God), and mumkin (possible, e.g., the entire world). It includes both particulars (جزئيات) and universals (كليات). Despite the multiplicity of objects known, the attribute itself is one without multiplicity. The author then provides two proofs: first, a scriptural proof (سمعي) for comprehensiveness, citing Quranic verses showing that God knows everything. Second, a rational proof for unity: people are only two groups—those who affirm eternal knowledge and consider it one, and those who negate it; no one has claimed multiple eternal knowledges.

Correction: The passage states clearly: 'it is one, no multiplicity or plurality in it, even if its known objects are multiple.' The unity of knowledge is established by consensus and rational argument.

Divine knowledge is all-encompassing and singular, proven by revelation and rational consensus.

?

God's Knowledge of the Impossible
Sanusi defines the nature of God's knowledge of impossibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's knowledge of impossible things is knowledge of their impossibility and not knowledge of them as possible or actual.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of the impossible is simply knowing that it cannot happen.

This lesson clarifies a subtle point about divine omniscience. Since God knows all things, but impossible things cannot exist, how does He know them? The answer is that His knowledge of the impossible is knowledge of their necessary non-existence. This passage from Sanusi explains precisely that.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice that the phrase 'knowledge of its impossibility' is central.
2
Understand the meaning
The passage says that God's knowledge of the impossible is not knowledge of it as a possible being, but knowledge of its impossibility. Consider why this distinction matters.
3
Reflect on the implication
If the impossible were to be imagined as occurring, it would lead to corruption. This shows that impossibility is necessary, not contingent.
4
Check your understanding
Test yourself with the quiz to ensure you grasp the key point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعنى تعلق علمه تعالى بالمستحيل علمه تعالى باستحالته وأنه لو تصور وقوعه لزمه من الفساد كذا.
And the meaning of His knowledge, may He be exalted, of the impossible is His knowledge of its impossibility, and that if its occurrence were imagined, such and such corruption would follow.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage clarifies the nature of God's knowledge regarding impossible things (المستحيلات). Since the impossible cannot exist, divine knowledge of it cannot be knowledge of it as an existing thing. Instead, it is knowledge of its impossibility—that it cannot occur. The author adds that if its occurrence were hypothetically imagined, it would lead to corruption (فساد). This explains that God's knowledge encompasses even impossibilities by way of knowing their necessary non-existence and the consequences if they were to occur.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge of the impossible means He knows it as a possible thing that doesn't happen.
Correction: Rather, His knowledge is knowledge of its impossibility—that it cannot occur.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of the impossible is knowledge of its necessary impossibility, not of it as a potential reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Ordering of Divine Power, Will, and Knowledge
Al-Sanusi explains the hierarchy of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical ordering among God's attributes of power, will, and knowledge according to Ahl al-Haqq.

Core Idea
Power follows will, will follows knowledge — the hierarchy of divine attributes.

This passage from al-Sanusi's matn presents a concise but crucial principle: the three primary attributes of divine action are not independent. Power, will, and knowledge are hierarchically linked such that each higher attribute conditions the operation of the lower. Understanding this ordering is essential for grasping the Ash'ari position on qadar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Notice the key terms: قدرة, إرادة, علم.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the dependency chain
Find the three claims of dependency: power depends on will, will depends on knowledge. Ask yourself what this implies about how God acts.
3
Reflect on the phrase 'people of truth'
The author attributes this view to 'Ahl al-Haqq', signalling that this is the orthodox Sunni position. Why might other groups disagree?
Consider the Mu'tazila or philosophers.
4
Apply the rule to concrete example
If God creates a apple, how do power, will, and knowledge relate in that act? This solidifies the abstract concept.
Knowledge of the apple precedes intention, intention precedes creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن تعلقات القدرة والإرادة والعلم مترتبة عند أهل الحق فتعلق القدرة تابع لتعلق الإرادة وتعلق الإرادة تابع لتعلق العلم فلا يوجد تعالى أو يعدم من الممكنات إلا ما أراد إيجاده أو إعدامه منها ولا يريد منها
And know that the attachments of power, will, and knowledge are ordered according to the people of truth: the attachment of power is dependent on the attachment of will, and the attachment of will is dependent on the attachment of knowledge. So He, exalted, does not bring into existence or annihilate from among the possible things except what He willed to bring into existence or annihilate from them, nor does He will from them
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage explains the internal ordering (ترتب) among three divine attributes: power (قدرة), will (إرادة), and knowledge (علم). According to the people of truth (أهل الحق), there is a dependency sequence: power's actual operation (attachment) depends on will's decree, and will's decree depends on knowledge's prior cognizance. Consequently, God only creates or annihilates possible things if He wills that, and His will is based on His knowledge. The passage stops mid-sentence, but the intended conclusion is that God does not will anything in ignorance or without purpose; it all flows from His perfect knowledge.

Key Terms
قدرة
إرادة
علم
ترتب
تابع
إيجاد
إعدام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God’s power operates independently of His will or knowledge, as if He could create arbitrarily without purpose.
Correction: Power’s operation is dependent on will’s command, and will’s command is dependent on knowledge’s wisdom. Every divine act is purposeful and known.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the order among the attachments of power, will, and knowledge?
A) Knowledge depends on will, will depends on power
B) Power depends on will, will depends on knowledge
C) They are all independent of each other
D) Will depends on power, power depends on knowledge

Key Takeaway
God's power only acts according to His will, and His will only acts according to His knowledge — a logical hierarchy that ensures every divine act is wise and purposeful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own life, do you truly believe that every event—both good and bad—is the result of God's power acting through His will, based on His perfect knowledge? Or do you sometimes feel that things happen randomly, as if God were not in control? How does this hierarchy change your perspective on a recent hardship you faced?
?
God's will follows His knowledge, meaning He never wills anything without knowing its full consequences. How does this truth affect your trust in God's decisions for you, especially when you don't understand His wisdom? Can you name one situation where you need to surrender your confusion to His all-encompassing knowledge?
?
This lesson teaches that power alone is not enough; it must be guided by will and knowledge. In your own actions, do you ever use your power (ability) without first aligning it with a righteous will and true knowledge? How can you better imitate this divine attribute of order in your daily decisions?

God's Knowledge of the Impossible
Definition of God's knowledge of the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's knowledge of the impossible is His knowledge of its impossibility and the hypothetical consequences if it were to occur.

Core Idea
God knows impossible things are impossible and would cause corruption if they occurred.

Before we explore how God's power, will, and knowledge relate to each other, the author first clarifies what it means for God to know something that cannot happen. This seemingly abstract point is actually crucial for understanding the limits of divine attributes in Islamic theology. Let us see how the passage defines this special kind of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it defines the attachment of knowledge to the impossible.
The impossible is something that cannot occur by definition.
2
Understand the two aspects
The definition has two parts: (1) God knows the impossibility of the impossible, and (2) He knows that if it were to happen, it would lead to corruption.
The corruption mentioned is a necessary consequence, not an actual event.
3
Contrast with possible things
This definition only applies to the impossible. For possible things, God's knowledge is different – He knows whether they will happen or not.
The passage will later discuss the hierarchy for possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعنى تعلّق علمه تعالى بالمستحيل علمه تعالى باستِحَالتِه ، وأنَّه لو تُصُوِّرَ وقوعه لَزِمَهُ مِن الفساد كذا. واعلم أنَّ تعلقات القدرة والإرادة والعلم مترتبة عند أهل الحقِّ ؛ فتعلق القدرة تابع لتعلَّق الإرادة، وتعلُّق الإرادة تابع لتعلق العلم ؛ فلا يُوجِدُ تعالى أو يُعْدِمُ مِن الممكناتِ إِلَّا ما أراد إيجاده أو إعدامه منها ، ولا يُريد منها إِلَّا ما علم أنَّه يكون ؛ فما علم أنَّه يكون من الممكنات أَرَادَه ، وما علم أنه لا يكون لم يُرد كَونَهُ ؛ فعندنا إيمانُ أبي جهل مأمور به غير مراد له تعالى ؛ لِعِلْمه عَدَمَ وقوعِه ، وكُفْرُهُ منهي عنه ، وهو واقع بإرادته تعالى وقدرته ؛ لعلمه وقوعَهُ .
And the meaning of the attachment of His knowledge, exalted is He, to the impossible is His knowledge of its impossibility, and that if its occurrence were imagined, corruption of such-and-such would necessarily follow from it. And know that the attachments of power, will, and knowledge are ordered according to the people of truth: the attachment of power follows the attachment of will, and the attachment of will follows the attachment of knowledge. So He, exalted, does not bring into existence or annihilate from among possibilities except what He wills to bring into existence or annihilate from them, and He does not will from them except what He knows will be. So what He knows will be from among possibilities He wills, and what He knows will not be He does not will its existence. Thus with us the faith of Abu Jahl is commanded but not willed by Him, exalted, because of His knowledge of its non-occurrence, and his disbelief is prohibited, and it occurs by His will and power, because of His knowledge of its occurrence.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a two-part explanation. First, it defines the meaning of God's knowledge of the impossible: it is His knowledge that the impossible cannot occur and that if it were supposed to occur, corruption would follow. Second, it explains the ordering of the three attributes of power, will, and knowledge according to Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth). The ordering is such that the attachment of power (i.e., what is brought into existence) is dependent on the attachment of will, and the attachment of will is dependent on the attachment of knowledge. Therefore, God only creates or annihilates possibilities that He wills, and He only wills what He knows will happen. This leads to the example of Abu Jahl: his faith is commanded by God (since belief is obligatory) but God does not will it because He knows it will not happen; his disbelief is prohibited but God wills it and brings it about because He knows it will happen. This illustrates the distinction between divine command and divine will, a key point in Sunni theology.

Key Terms
تعلق العلم
المستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God cannot know the impossible because it is not real.
Correction: God's knowledge encompasses both what is real and what is impossible; He knows the impossibility and its hypothetical consequences.

Checkpoint
What does it mean when we say God knows the impossible?
A) He knows it as a possible event.
B) He knows its impossibility and the corruption that would follow if it occurred.
C) He does not know it because it is not real.
D) He knows it as an actual event.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of the impossible is His knowledge that it cannot happen and that it would cause corruption if it did.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself wishing for something impossible—like reversing a past mistake—do you consider the corruption that God knows would follow if it occurred? How does this lesson reshape your desires?
?
God knows the impossibility of things with certainty. Is your heart truly at peace with His knowledge, or do you sometimes struggle to accept that some things are impossible by His decree? What would it take to surrender fully?
?
The lesson says that if the impossible were to occur, corruption would follow. Think of a time you insisted on something that seemed impossible. Did you recognize the corruption it would bring? How can you train yourself to trust God's knowledge over your own wishes?

Ordering of Power, Will, and Knowledge
Hierarchy of divine attributes and example of Abu Jahl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchical relationship among God's power, will, and knowledge, and how it applies to the example of Abu Jahl.

Core Idea
God's power follows His will, and His will follows His knowledge.

Now that we understand how God knows the impossible, the author turns to the relationship between three key attributes: power, will, and knowledge. In Sunni theology, these are not equal partners—knowledge guides will, and will guides power. This ordering solves major theological puzzles, such as why God commands what He does not will, as the example of Abu Jahl will show.

Guided Path
1
Read the hierarchy statement
Read the sentence stating that the attachments are ordered. Notice that power depends on will, and will depends on knowledge.
'Attachment' here refers to what each attribute relates to—for example, power relates to what is brought into existence.
2
Trace the logical flow
Follow the logic: God only creates what He wills, and He only wills what He knows will happen. This means knowledge determines will, and will determines action.
This is a chain: knowledge → will → power (creation).
3
Apply to Abu Jahl
Read the example: Abu Jahl's faith is commanded but not willed by God; his disbelief is prohibited but occurs by God's will and power. Why? Because God knew faith would not happen, so He did not will it; He knew disbelief would happen, so He willed it.
This illustrates the distinction between God's legislative will (command) and His creative will (decree).
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this ordering protects divine wisdom and avoids contradictions: God commands good but allows evil because He knows it will happen and has a purpose in creating it.
This is a core Sunni position against those who claim God wills all He commands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Three Rulings of God's Inner Speech
Sanusi explains three rulings of God's inner speech by analogy to knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rulings of God's inner speech and how it parallels His knowledge in generality, oneness, and infinity.

Core Idea
God's inner speech is one, universal, and infinite — just like His knowledge.

This passage from al-Sanusi explains that the attribute of inner speech shares three key properties with the attribute of knowledge: it applies to all things, it is one, and it has infinite objects of relation. These rulings are derived from rational necessity and scriptural evidence. Understanding these properties protects against misconceptions that God's speech is multiple or limited.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice how the author draws a parallel between knowledge and inner speech.
The passage uses the phrase 'wa mithlu dha kalamuhu' to introduce the analogy.
2
Identify the three rulings
Find and list the three rulings: generality of attachment, oneness, and non-finiteness of objects. Note the brief justifications given for each.
The justifications are: salih li al-jami', imtina' al-takhsis, and thubut bi al-sam'.
3
Reflect on the basis of oneness
Consider why the oneness of inner speech is established by revelation and not by reason. How does this differ from the other two rulings?
This highlights a key methodological point in kalam: some attributes are known through revelation alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانُ تَعلُّقات صفة الكلام النفسي وأحكامها (وَمِثْلُ ذَا كَلَامُهُ) يعني أنَّ كلامَ الله تعالى النفسي القديمَ القائم بذاته مِثلُ العلم في أحكامه الثلاثة: في وجوب عُموم تعلقه بالواجب والممتنع والجائز ، ووجوب وَحْدَتِه ، وعدم تناهي مُتعلّقاته ، فعموم تعلَّقه ؛ لصلوحه للجميع، وعدم تناهي مُتعلقاته ؛ لامتناع التخصيص في صفاته تعالى، ووجوبُ وَحْدَتِه ؛ لثبوت صفة الكلام بالسَّمع دون العقل، ولم يرد السمع
Explanation of the attachments of the attribute of inner speech and its rulings: 'And similar to this is His speech' means that God's inner, eternal speech, subsisting in His essence, is like knowledge in its three rulings: in the necessity of the generality of its attachment to the necessary, the impossible, and the possible; and the necessity of its oneness; and the non-finiteness of its objects. So the generality of its attachment is because of its fitness for all, and the non-finiteness of its objects is because of the impossibility of specification in His attributes, and the necessity of its oneness is because the attribute of speech is established by revelation (sam') and not by reason, and revelation did not come.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation. It first states that God's inner speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) is similar to His knowledge in three rulings (ahkam). The three rulings are: (1) the necessity of its universal attachment to all three categories of existence: necessary (wajib), impossible (mumtani'), and possible (ja'iz); (2) the necessity of its oneness (wahda), meaning it is not divisible into multiple actual speech acts but one eternal attribute; and (3) the infinity of its objects (muta'allaqat), i.e., it relates to an infinite number of things. The passage then provides justifications: universal attachment is because the attribute is fit for all objects (salih li al-jami'); infinity of objects is because no specification (takhsis) can apply to God's attributes; and oneness is because speech is established by revelation (sam') and not by reason, and revelation has not indicated multiplicity (the last part is truncated but understood).

Key Terms
كلام نفسي
العلم
الواجب
الممتنع
الجائز
وحدة
عدم تناهي
السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's speech is multiple or only relates to some things.
Correction: Actually, God's inner speech is one, universal in attachment, and infinite in its objects.

Checkpoint

B) Necessity of oneness
C) Eternity of speech
D) Non-finiteness of objects

Key Takeaway
God's inner speech is one, universal in attachment, and infinite in its objects—paralleling His knowledge.

The Speech Attribute and Its Three Rulings
The commentator explains that God's speech resembles His knowledge in three rulings: universality, unity, and infinity of objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's speech attribute shares three essential rulings with His knowledge: universal attachment, unity, and infinite objects, and that its unity is established by revelation and consensus.

Core Idea
God's speech, like His knowledge, is one, universal, and infinite.

This lesson unpacks a dense passage that draws an analogy between God's speech and His knowledge. The commentator highlights three parallel rulings that clarify the nature of divine speech as an eternal, single attribute. By studying this analogy, you will grasp key theological distinctions about how God's attributes relate to creation and why unity is a necessary feature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the opening analogy and the three enumerated rulings.
The passage uses very precise theological language.
2
Identify the three rulings
List the three rulings that the commentator says are shared between speech and knowledge: universal attachment, unity, and non-finiteness of objects.
Each ruling is briefly explained after being stated.
3
Understand the basis for unity
Notice that the commentator argues for the unity of speech based on revelation (al-sam') and consensus, not reason alone. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
This connects to the broader method of establishing attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَمِثْلُ ذَا كَلَامُهُ) يعني أنَّ كلامَ الله تعالى النفسي القديمَ القائم بذاته مِثلُ العلم في أحكامه الثلاثة: في وجوب عُموم تعلقه بالواجب والممتنع والجائز، ووجوب وَحْدَتِه، وعدم تناهي مُتعلّقاته، فعموم تعلَّقه؛ لصلوحه للجميع، وعدم تناهي مُتعلقاته؛ لامتناع التخصيص في صفاته تعالى، ووجوبُ وَحْدَتِه؛ لثبوت صفة الكلام بالسَّمع دون العقل، ولم يرد السمع بالتَّعدُّد، بل انعَقد الإجماع على نفي كلام ثانٍ قديم. فَلْتَتَّبِعِ القوم فيما التزموه.
("And like this is His speech") meaning that God's eternal self-subsistent speech (al-kalam al-nafsi al-qadim al-qa'im bi-dhatihi) is similar to knowledge (al-'ilm) in its three rulings: in the obligation of its universal attachment to the necessary, the impossible, and the possible; the obligation of its unity; and the non-finiteness of its objects. So its universal attachment is due to its suitability for all, and the non-finiteness of its objects is due to the impossibility of specification in His attributes, and the obligation of its unity is due to the establishment of the attribute of speech by transmission (al-sam') not by reason, and transmission did not come with multiplicity; rather, consensus has been made on the negation of a second eternal speech. So follow the people (al-qawm) in what they have committed to.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of divine speech (kalam nafsi) and its three rulings. The commentator begins by stating that God's speech is like His knowledge in three aspects: 1) its attachment is universal (obligatory to all possible, necessary, impossible objects), 2) it is one (not multiple), 3) its objects are infinite. Then he explains each: universality because the attribute is suitable for all objects; infinitude because God's attributes cannot be subject to specification; unity because the proof for speech is from revelation (sam'), and revelation does not indicate multiplicity, and consensus rejects a second eternal speech. The passage concludes with an instruction to follow the scholars in this position.

Key Terms

العلم
الوجوب
الوحدانية
عدم التناهي
السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's speech is multiple because there are different types (commands, prohibitions, etc.)
Correction: God's speech is one attribute; the diversity is in its relations (e.g., commanding, prohibiting), not in the essence of speech itself.

Checkpoint

A) Power
B) Knowledge
C) Will
D) Hearing

Key Takeaway

The Universal Attachment of Hearing and Sight
Commentary on hearing and sight attaching to all existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine attributes of hearing, sight, and speech attach to every existent without restriction, encompassing all categories of beings.

Core Idea
God's hearing, sight, and speech encompass all existents universally.

After establishing speech as an attribute, the commentator now clarifies that hearing and sight share the same universal scope. This passage emphasizes that these three attributes do not discriminate between physical and abstract, necessary and possible, or any other classification. Every existent falls under their perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage at least twice, then follow the translation. Notice the phrase 'وكل موجود أنط للسمع به' as the starting claim.
The passage is dense with classifications; don't rush.
2
Identify the shared object
The commentator states that hearing, sight, and speech share a single object of attachment (متعَلَّق). Find where he explicitly says this and note what it means.
3
Examine the list of classifications
The passage lists several pairs: necessary/possible, substance/accident, universal/particular, abstract/material, composite/simple. Ask yourself why the author includes so many categories.
This shows the attribute's scope is truly unrestricted.
4
Test your understanding
Try to recall the classifications without looking. Then answer the checkpoint question to confirm your grasp.
The checkpoint is from the passage itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَكُلُّ مَوْجُودٍ أَنِطْ) أي: عَلَّقَ (لِلسَّمْع) الأزلي (به) أي: اعتقد تعلَّقه بكلِّ موجودٍ كَذَا البَصَرْ) الأزليُّ) و(إدْرَاكُهُ) مِثلُ سَمعِه (إِنْ قِيلَ بِه) أي: بثبوته له تعالى كما تقدَّم، يعني: أنَّ هذه الصفات الثَّلاث مُتَحدةُ المُتَعَلَّقِ، فتتعلقُ بالموجودِ واجبًا كان أو ممكنًا عَيْنًا كان أو معنى، كُليًّا كان أو جزئيًّا، مُجرَّداً كان أو ماديا، مُركَّبا كان أو بسيطا.
("And every existent, attach to it the hearing") meaning: He attached the eternal hearing to it, i.e., believe in its attachment to every existent. Likewise the eternal sight, and its perception is like His hearing if it is affirmed for Him, i.e., by its establishment for Him, exalted, as previously. Meaning: that these three attributes have a single object, so they attach to the existent whether necessary or possible, whether substance or accident, whether universal or particular, whether abstract or material, whether composite or simple.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage explains the attributes of hearing (sam') and sight (basar) and their attachment. The commentator interprets the line "وكل موجود أنط للسمع به" as meaning that the eternal hearing is attached to every existent. Similarly, the eternal sight perceives everything. The perception is like hearing if accepted. Then he states that these three attributes (speech, hearing, sight) share a single object of attachment: they attach to every existent, regardless of classification: necessary or possible, substance or accident, universal or particular, abstract or material, composite or simple. This emphasizes the universality of God's attributes of perception.

Key Terms

البصر
المتعلق
الموجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that hearing and sight only perceive physical sounds and sights, thus limited to material substances.
Correction: The passage clarifies that these attributes attach to every existent, including abstract meanings, universals, and simple entities, not just physical objects.

Checkpoint

A) Only physical substances

Key Takeaway
God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech attach to every existent, regardless of its category.

?

The Scope of Hearing and Sight
Al-Shanwani explains the attachment of hearing and sight to all existents and discusses differing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether Allah's attributes of hearing and sight attach to all existents or only to sounds and visible objects.

Core Idea
Allah's hearing and sight encompass all existence, not just what is conventionally heard or seen.

This passage from Jawharat al-Tawhid addresses a key theological question: do Allah's attributes of hearing and sight only perceive what is conventionally heard and seen, or do they perceive all existents? The author presents the view that these attributes attach to every existent, while also noting a differing opinion from later scholars. We will examine the reasoning behind this debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the verse from Jawharat al-Tawhid and the commentator's explanation.
The passage is dense; take time to understand each phrase.
2
Identify the core claim
The commentator states that hearing and sight attach to every existent, regardless of its category (necessary/possible, entity/meaning, etc.). Find the list of categories.
The key phrase is 'كل موجود'.
3
Understand the unity of muta‘allaq
The commentator notes that hearing, sight, and apprehension have the same object of attachment, but this does not make the attributes identical.
This is a subtle point: unity of object ≠ unity of attribute.
4
Examine the scholarly difference
Compare the view of later scholars (attributes attach to all existents) with that of al-Taftazani (attributes attach only to heard/seen things).
This shows that even within the Ash‘ari school, there are nuanced differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانُ تعلقاتِ صِفتَي السَّمع والبصر وأحكامهما
٣٦ - وكلُّ مَوْجُودٍ أَنِطْ لِلسَّمْعِ بهِ كَذَا البَصَرْ إِدْرَاكُهُ إِنْ قِيلَ بِهْ
وَكُلُّ مَوْجُودٍ أَنِطْ ) أي : عَلَّقَ (لِلسَّمْع) الأزلي (به) أي: اعتقد تعلَّقه بكلِّ موجودٍ كَذَا البَصَرْ ) الأزليُّ) و(إدْرَاكُهُ) مِثلُ سَمعِه (إِنْ قِيلَ بِه) أي: بثبوته له تعالى كما تقدَّم، يعني: أنَّ هذه الصفات الثَّلاث مُتَحدةُ المُتَعَلَّقِ، فتتعلقُ بالموجودِ واجبًا كان أو ممكنًا عَيْنًا كان أو معنى ، كُليًّا كان أو جُزئيًّا (٤) ، مُجرَّداً كان أو ماديا (٥) ، مُركَّبا كان أو بسيط (۱) ، ولا يلزمُ مِن اتّحاد المُتَعلَّقِ اتّحادُ الصِّفَة (٢) . وما ذكره المُصنف - رحمه الله تعالى - مبني على ما ذكره بعض المتأخرين (۳) مِن تعلَّق سمعه تعالى بسوى المسموعات عادةً ، وبَصره بسوى المُبْصَرَات كذلك (٤) ، والذي في كلام السعد (ه) وغيرِه أَنَّ السَّمعَ الأزليَّ صفةٌ تتعلق بالمسموعات ، وأنَّ البصر الأزليَّ صفةٌ تتعلق بالمُبصرات ، وهو مُحتمل للعموم والخصوص (٦)
Explanation of the attachments of the two attributes of hearing and sight and their rulings. 36. And every existent — attach hearing to it, likewise sight, its apprehension if it is affirmed. 'And every existent — attach' means: He attached (the eternal hearing) to it, i.e., consider its attachment to every existent. Likewise (the eternal sight). And 'its apprehension' is like His hearing 'if it is affirmed', i.e., by affirming it for Him, may He be exalted, as before. Meaning: these three attributes have a unified object of attachment (muta‘allaq), so they attach to the existent whether it is necessary or possible, whether it is an entity or a meaning, whether universal or particular, whether abstract or material, whether composite or simple. And it does not follow from the unity of the object of attachment that the attribute is unified. And what the author—may Allah have mercy on him—mentioned is based on what some later scholars mentioned: that His eternal hearing attaches to other than what is customarily heard, and His sight attaches to other than what is customarily seen. And what is in the words of al-Sa‘d (al-Taftazani) and others is that eternal hearing is an attribute that attaches to heard things, and eternal sight is an attribute that attaches to seen things, and it is susceptible to generality and specificity.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the object of attachment (muta‘allaq) of the divine attributes of hearing (sam‘) and sight (basar). It begins by quoting the verse from Jawharat al-Tawhid: 'And every existent — attach hearing to it, likewise sight, its apprehension if it is affirmed.' Then the commentator explains each phrase: 'And every existent — attach' means that the eternal hearing is attached to every existent; similarly, the eternal sight. 'Its apprehension' refers to the act of perception (idrak) which is like the act of hearing, and this is affirmed for God (ta‘ala). The commentator then states that these three attributes (hearing, sight, and the faculty of apprehension) have a unified object of attachment: they attach to every existent regardless of its category—whether necessary or possible, entity or meaning, universal or particular, abstract or material, composite or simple. Importantly, the unity of the object does not entail unity of the attribute; each attribute remains distinct. The commentator then notes that this position is based on later scholars (such as al-Sanusi) who argue that God's hearing and sight attach to things beyond what is conventionally heard or seen (i.e., not only sounds and visible objects). However, he contrasts this with the view of al-Sa‘d (al-Taftazani) and others, who restrict these attributes to heard things (masmu‘at) and seen things (mubsirat), and note that the wording is open to both general and specific interpretations. Thus, the passage clarifies a nuanced theological difference among Ash‘ari scholars regarding the scope of these attributes.

Correction: The author argues for a broader attachment to all existents, though al-Taftazani's view restricts them.

The Objects of Divine Hearing and Sight
Commentary on the relation of hearing and sight to their objects and a difference of opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that unity of the object of relation does not imply unity of attribute, and the different scholarly views on the scope of Allah's hearing and sight.

Core Idea
Hearing and sight remain distinct even when their objects overlap.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid clarifies a subtle point about the divine attributes of hearing and sight. The author first establishes a logical principle: two attributes can share the same object without being identical. He then presents two scholarly positions on what these attributes perceive — whether they are restricted to sounds and visible objects or extend to all things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the author's flow.
Notice the concise style of the commentary.
2
Understand the core principle
The passage states that unity of the object (mutā‘allaq) does not force unity of the attribute (ṣifa). This is a key logical rule in discussing divine attributes.
This principle prevents conflating distinct attributes even when they relate to the same thing.
3
Compare the two scholarly opinions
The author mentions a view from some later scholars that hearing and sight relate to things not normally heard or seen. Then he cites al-Sa'd (al-Taftazānī) who restricts them to sounds and visible objects respectively.
These are two different positions on the scope of these attributes.
4
Reflect on the flexibility of interpretation
The passage concludes that al-Sa'd's view is 'susceptible to generality or specificity,' meaning it can be interpreted broadly or narrowly.
This shows that even within a single opinion, there is room for interpretation.

Statement of the relations of the two attributes of hearing and sight and their rulings. [It is] either compound or simple, and unity of the object of relation does not necessitate unity of the attribute. And what the author mentioned—may Allah have mercy on him—is based on what some later scholars mentioned, that His hearing—exalted is He—relates to what is not normally heard, and His sight to what is not normally seen likewise. Commentary
This passage first clarifies that the object of relation can be either simple or compound, and that a single object does not necessitate a single attribute. Then it explains that the author's view is based on some later scholars who hold that hearing and sight can apply to things not normally heard or seen. In contrast, al-Sa'd (al-Taftazānī) and others restrict these attributes to sounds and visible things respectively. The passage concludes that this interpretation is open to either a general or specific reading.

The Distinctness of Knowledge from Other Attributes
Proof of the distinctness of four attributes from knowledge and each other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attributes of hearing, sight, speech, and perception are distinct from knowledge and from each other, based on linguistic denotation and scriptural proof.

Core Idea
Linguistic distinctness implies real distinctness until proven otherwise.

This passage establishes a key principle in the study of divine attributes: the four attributes of speech, hearing, sight, and perception are not identical to knowledge, nor are they identical to each other. The proof comes not from reason but from language and revelation. Sanusi explains that because each attribute has its own unique linguistic meaning, we must treat them as distinct until evidence shows otherwise. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic verse and commentary carefully. Note that the passage begins by stating the distinctness and then provides the proof.
The commentary is part of the source; treat it as the main teaching material.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the sentence that gives the proof: 'because these attributes are only established by revelation, and the linguistic denotation of each is other than the denotation of the other, so it is necessary to carry what has been transmitted on its apparent meaning, until the contrary is established'.
3
Understand the key principle
Notice that unity of the object of relation does not imply unity of the essence. This is a crucial point for avoiding attribute confusion.
This principle applies broadly in Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the application
The commentator mentions that the necessity of relation is derived from the imperative 'Utter' and the unlimited scope from the general term 'existing thing'. This shows how scriptural language informs our understanding of attributes.

Section on Divine Matters. [The mutual distinctness of knowledge from hearing, sight, speech, and perception, and their distinctness from one another.]
37. And other than knowledge are these, as established; then life is that which relates to nothing.
(And 'other than knowledge' these four attributes, which are: speech, hearing, sight, and perception, meaning that they are distinct from knowledge in reality, and likewise some from others, as established among the people by scriptural proofs; because these attributes are only established by revelation, and the linguistic denotation of each is other than the denotation of the other, so it is necessary to carry what has been transmitted on its apparent meaning, until the contrary is established, and unity of the object of relation does not necessitate unity of reality. He remained silent about the oneness of these attributes like life, because it is known from their necessity for their sisters, as there is no difference. As for the necessity of relation, it is derived from the imperative form in His saying: 'Utter', just as the non-finiteness of their objects of relation is derived from the general term entering upon 'existing thing'.)
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the verse stating that the four attributes (speech, hearing, sight, perception) are distinct from knowledge and from each other. The proof is that these attributes are known only through revelation, and in language each has a distinct meaning, so they must be considered distinct unless evidence shows otherwise. Unity of the object does not imply unity of the essence. The commentator notes that the author omitted discussing whether these attributes are one like life, because it is clear they are necessary for one another. Finally, the necessity of their relation to objects is derived from the imperative 'Utter' (in the Qur'anic command to the mountain to utter), and the unlimited scope is derived from the general word 'everything' applied to existing things.

Distinction of Four Divine Attributes from Knowledge and Each Other
Explains distinction of four attributes from knowledge and each other

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why speech, hearing, sight, and perception are considered distinct from knowledge and from each other in Sunni theology, and how unity of object does not imply unity of attribute.

Core Idea
Distinction in reality does not require separation in existence.

In this passage, Sanusi explains that four divine attributes—speech, hearing, sight, and perception—are distinct from the attribute of knowledge and from each other. He bases this on scriptural evidence and linguistic differences, emphasizing that unity of object does not imply unity of attribute. This distinction is essential for understanding the richness of Allah's attributes without compromising His oneness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it begins with a verse line and then provides commentary. Identify the four attributes being discussed.
2
Identify the basis for distinction
The passage states that these attributes are distinct based on scriptural evidence (al-adilla al-samʿiyya) and because their linguistic denotations differ. Understand why revelation is the source for knowing these attributes.
3
Focus on the key principle
Sanusi argues that unity of the object of relation (ittihād al-mutaʿallaq) does not necessitate unity of reality. This means that even if the same thing is heard and seen, hearing and sight remain distinct attributes.
4
Consider the derivations
The commentary explains that the necessity of relation is derived from the imperative form in the verse 'Anit', and the non-finiteness of objects is derived from the universal particle applied to 'mawjud'. Reflect on how these linguistic points support the distinction.
