Alternative Defenses of the Eternality of the Names
Sanusi presents three alternative defenses of the names' eternality and their critiques.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three additional responses to the objection that names are created: by specification (taʿayyun), by referents (madlūl), and by the speech of Allah (kalām Allāh), along with their critiques.

Core Idea
The names are eternal as divine determinations, meanings, or manifestations of eternal speech.

After the initial answers, Sanusi presents further ways to understand the claim that the names are eternal. Some theologians argue that 'naming' refers to Allah's pre-eternal specification of which names would apply to Him. Others say the eternality belongs to the realities denoted by the names—the divine attributes. Still others anchor the names in Allah's eternal speech. Each answer, however, faces its own objections, as the passage shows.

Guided Path
1
Read the third answer: by specification
The passage says 'naming' means the specification (taʿayyun) of the names in pre-eternity. Allah predetermined which names would apply to Him, and that determination is eternal.
This answer moves the eternality to the predetermination, not the names themselves.
2
Examine the objection to this answer
The objection is that everything is predestined in pre-eternity, so this answer does not distinguish the names from anything else.
The objection challenges the uniqueness of this explanation.
3
Consider the fourth answer: by referents
The names are eternal because their referents (madlūl)—like knowledge, power, and will—are eternal attributes of Allah.
This answer is a return to the standard Ash'ari position that the attributes are eternal.
4
Study the fifth answer: by the speech of Allah
The naming is eternal because it is indicated by Allah's speech (kalām), which is eternal.
This answer grounds the names in the eternal attribute of speech.
5
Note the critique of the last answer
The passage notes that this answer is not a good refutation because the eternality of speech is already established earlier, and it does not directly address the original objection.
This highlights the need for the answer to directly counter the objection.

Source Text

Alternative answers are then proposed: (a) 'naming' means the specific determination (taʿayyun) of the names in pre-eternity—Allah predetermined which names would apply to Him, and that determination is eternal; (b) 'names are eternal' refers to the referents (madlūl) like knowledge, power, and will, which are eternal as attributes; (c) 'naming is eternal' because it is indicated by Allah's speech (kalām), which is eternal. The last answer is also critiqued. The passage ends incomplete, but it shows the richness of debates around this issue. The core idea is that there are multiple ways to defend the eternality of names without asserting the words themselves are uncreated.

Key Terms
تعين (taʿayyun)
مدلول (madlūl)
كلام الله (kalām Allāh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all defenses of the names' eternality are equally valid and without problems.
Correction: The passage shows that each answer faces objections, and some are considered insufficient refutations of the Mu'tazila.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the objection to the third answer (by specification)?
A) Specification is a human concept.
B) Everything is predestined in pre-eternity, so this answer does not distinguish the names.
C) Specification implies change in Allah.
D) The Mu'tazila deny predestination.

Key Takeaway
The variety of answers shows the theological effort to preserve the eternality of Allah's names, but each answer has limitations and faces objections from opponents.

When you call upon Allah by one of His names, do you feel that name is just a word you learned, or does it connect you to an eternal truth about His being? What does your heart truly believe?

This lesson reveals that even scholars struggled to articulate how Allah's names are eternal. Does this complexity humble you or trouble you? How does it affect your trust in what you have been taught?
?
Think of a name of Allah you use often, like 'Ar-Rahman'. Have you ever pondered what it means that this name is eternal? How should that change the way you say it and rely upon it in your daily life?

The Eternality of Allah's Attributes: Definition and Proof
Sanusi defines the eternality of Allah's essential attributes and proves it via the impossibility of accidents in His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that Allah's essential attributes are eternal, based on the impossibility of accidents in His essence.

Core Idea
If Allah's attributes were not eternal, His essence would be a locus of accidents—impossible.

Sanusi begins the section on divine matters by stating that the attributes of Allah's essence are eternal. He defines 'eternal' as not being preceded by non-existence and then provides a rational proof: if they were not eternal, they would be originated, leading to three impossible consequences. This passage also addresses the Mu'tazila claim that Allah had no attributes before creation, showing why that view is mistaken.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the definition and the proof that follows. Pay attention to the footnote markers that correspond to the commentary.
The passage begins with 'قسم الإلهيات' and continues through the refutation.
2
Understand the definition of 'eternal'
Sanusi defines 'eternal' (qadīma) as not being preceded by non-existence. This means the attributes have always existed with Allah and were not created or invented by creation.
3
Trace the proof
The proof is a conditional: if the attributes were not eternal, they would be originated. This leads to three impossible consequences regarding Allah's essence. Identify each consequence.
The three consequences are: (1) accidents subsisting in Allah, (2) Allah being devoid of them in pre-eternity, (3) needing a specificator.
4
Note the refutation
After the proof, Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila view by quoting commentators who condemn the idea that Allah had no attributes before creation.
This shows the practical importance of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات صِفَاتُ ذَاتِهِ) (۱) أي: القائمة بذاته تعالى، وهي السبع السابقة، مثل الأسماء عندنا ; فهي (قَدِيمَة ) (۲) أي : يجب لها القدم بمعنى عدم مسبوقيتها بالعدم(۳) أي: فليست من وضع الخَلْق له (٤) ; لأنَّها لو لم تكن قديمة لكانت حادثة(ه) ، فيلزمُ قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى، ويلزم كونه تعالى كان عارياً عنها في الأزل، ويلزمُ افتقارها إلى مُخصص ، وهو (٦) يُنافي وجوبَ || على جميع أقسام الحكم العقلي ، فلا خصوصية للأسماء ، فهذا البحث لم يصفُ ، والأقرب ما ذكره الشارح والتسمية تفسر بالصلاحية أو بالتعيين وقصده بهذا الرد على المعتزلة: إن الله كان أزلا بلا اسم ولا صفة، فلما أوجد الخلق وضعوا له الأسماء والصفات، كما نقله عنهم القرطبي والفاكهاني وغيرهما قال السمين وهذا قول أشد خطأ من قولهم القرآن مخلوق ، لإشعاره باحتياجه إلى الغير الشنواني عن «هداية المريد».
Section on Divine Matters (Ilāhiyyāt). The attributes of His essence (ṣifāt dhātihi) (1), i.e., those subsisting in His essence, may He be exalted, and they are the seven mentioned earlier, like the names according to us; so they are eternal (qadīma) (2), i.e., eternality is necessarily true for them in the sense of not being preceded by non-existence (3), i.e., they are not from the coinage (waḍʿ) of creation for Him (4); because if they were not eternal they would be originated (ḥāditha) (5), then the subsistence of accidental things (ḥawādith) in His essence would follow, and it would follow that He, may He be exalted, was devoid of them in pre-eternity, and it would follow that they would need a specificator (mukhassiṣ), and that (6) contradicts the obligation [of necessity] according to all divisions of rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī). So there is no special status for names; this inquiry is not clear. And the closest is what the commentator mentioned: the naming (al-tasmiya) is interpreted as validity (al-ṣulāḥiyya) or as specification (al-taʿyīn). And his intent by this is a refutation of the Muʿtazila: that Allah was eternally without name or attribute, and when He created creation they placed names and attributes for Him, as transmitted from them by al-Qurṭubī and al-Fākihānī and others. Al-Samīn said: This is a more severe error than their statement that the Qurʾan is created, because it indicates His need for other than Him. From al-Shanwānī via 'Hidāyat al-Murīd'.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the eternality of Allah's essential attributes. It begins by stating that the attributes of His essence—the seven previously mentioned—are like the names in being eternal. The meaning of 'eternal' here is not being preceded by non-existence. Consequently, these attributes are not invented or placed by creation for Allah. The proof is then given as a conditional argument: if the attributes were not eternal, they would be originated (ḥādith). This leads to three impossible consequences: (1) accidental things (ḥawādith) would subsist in Allah's essence; (2) Allah would have been devoid of them in pre-eternity (which would imply change); (3) they would require a specificator (mukhassiṣ) to bring them into existence, contradicting Allah's self-sufficiency. These consequences conflict with the necessary judgments of reason, thus establishing that the attributes are eternal. The passage then notes that this argument applies equally to names and attributes, so there is no special case for names. The commentator al-Shanwānī reports that the best response is to interpret 'naming' as either validity or specification, and that this refutes the Muʿtazilī claim that Allah existed without name or attribute before creation. Al-Samīn condemns this Muʿtazilī view as worse than saying the Qurʾan is created. The core idea is that the proof from the impossibility of accidents in Allah's essence demonstrates the necessity of eternal attributes.

Key Terms
صفات ذاتية (ṣifāt dhātiyya)
قدم (qidam)
عدم المسبوقية (ʿadam al-masbūqiyya)
حوادث (ḥawādith)
مخصص (mukhassiṣ)
أقسام الحكم العقلي (aqsām al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah existed eternally without any attributes or names; humans invented names and attributes for Him after creation.
Correction: Allah's attributes are eternal and not invented by creation; if they were not eternal, they would be originated, leading to absurd consequences.

Checkpoint
According to the proof in this passage, if Allah's attributes were not eternal, which of the following would be an impossible consequence?
A) They would be dependent on creation.
B) They would subsist in Allah's essence.
C) Allah would be devoid of them in pre-eternity.
D) They would be self-subsisting.

Key Takeaway
Allah's essential attributes are eternal, proven by the rational impossibility of accidents in His essence.

Proof That Allah's Essential Attributes Are Eternal
Proof that Allah's essential attributes are eternal via conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's essential attributes must be eternal and how the proof from impossibility of originated accidents in Allah establishes this.

Core Idea
Allah's essential attributes are eternal; if they were created, absurdities follow.

The author begins by identifying the essential attributes of Allah and then proceeds to prove their eternity. The proof is a classic kalam argument: if the attributes were originated, then three impossible consequences follow, all contradicting Allah's absolute self-sufficiency. This refutes the Mu'tazilite position that the attributes are created by creation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and terms
Understand what 'attributes of His essence' (ṣifāt dhātihi) means and what 'eternal' (qadima) implies: not being preceded by non-existence.
The author refers to the seven known attributes.
2
Trace the conditional proof
Follow the logic: if the attributes were not eternal, they would be originated (haditha). Then three impossibilities follow: (1) originated things subsisting in Allah, (2) Allah devoid of them in pre-eternity, (3) their need for a specifier. These contradict absolute self-sufficiency.
Each consequence is separately impossible.
3
Connect to the refutation of the Mu'tazila
The Mu'tazila claimed Allah had no names or attributes before creation; creation invented them. This argument shows that would entail impossible consequences, so their view is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات. صفات ذاته أي: القائمة بذاته تعالى، وهي السبع السابقة، مثل الأسماء عندنا ; فهي قديمة أي: يجب لها القدم بمعنى عدم مسبوقيتها بالعدم أي: فليست من وضع الخلق له ; لأنها لو لم تكن قديمة لكانت حادثة، فيلزم قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى، ويلزم كونه تعالى كان عاريا عنها في الأزل، ويلزم افتقارها إلى مخصص، وهو ينافي وجوب الغنى المطلق. وقصده بهذا الرد على المعتزلة: إن الله كان أزلا بلا اسم ولا صفة، فلما أوجد الخلق وضعوا له الأسماء والصفات.
Section on Divine Matters. The attributes of His essence: meaning the ones subsisting in His essence, may He be exalted, and they are the seven preceding [attributes], like the names in our view. So they are eternal (qadima): meaning eternity (al-qidam) is necessary for them in the sense of not being preceded by non-existence. That is, they are not from the making of the creation for Him; because if they were not eternal, they would be originated (haditha). Then the occurrence of originated things in His essence would be entailed, and His being devoid of them in pre-eternity would be entailed, and their need for a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) would be entailed, and that contradicts absolute self-sufficiency (al-ghinā al-muṭlaq). And by this he aims to refute the Mu'tazila: that Allah was in pre-eternity without name or attribute, then when He created the creation, they put for Him the names and attributes.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage defines and proves that Allah's essential attributes (ṣifāt dhātihi) are eternal (qadima). The author first identifies the attributes as those subsisting in Allah's essence, referring to the seven known attributes (life, knowledge, will, power, hearing, sight, speech). He then explains that 'qadima' means not being preceded by non-existence, not simply having a long duration. He emphasizes that these attributes are not created by the creation as the Mu'tazila claimed. The proof is a conditional syllogism: if the attributes were not eternal, they would be originated (haditha). If they were originated, three unacceptable consequences follow: (1) originated things (ḥawādith) would subsist in Allah's essence, (2) Allah would have been devoid of them in pre-eternity, and (3) they would need a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to bring them into existence. All three contradict the necessary attribute of absolute self-sufficiency (ghinā muṭlaq) which is essential to Allah. Therefore, the attributes must be eternal. He notes that this refutes the Mu'tazilite position that Allah had no names or attributes before creation, and that the creation invented them.

قديمة
القدم
الحدوث
قيام الحوادث
الغنى المطلق
المخصص
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'qadima' means merely beginningless in time, but the author clarifies it means not preceded by non-existence, i.e., eternally inherent and not created.
Correction: Qadima means not having been preceded by non-existence, thus the attributes are inherently eternal, not created.

Checkpoint

A) They would be multiple, temporal, and dependent.
B) They would subsist in Allah, Allah would have been without them in pre-eternity, and they would need a specifier.
C) They would be created by angels, finite, and subject to change.
D) They would be accidental, composite, and divisible.

Key Takeaway
Allah's essential attributes are eternal because if they were originated, it would lead to impossible consequences that contradict His absolute self-sufficiency.

Negative and Active Attributes: Not Eternal
Classification of attributes and etymology of 'dhat'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that negative and active attributes are not eternal and do not subsist in Allah's essence, and you will learn the linguistic origin of the word 'dhat'.

Core Idea
Negative and active attributes are not eternal; they differ from essential attributes.

This lesson clarifies an important classification within Ash'ari theology: the distinction between essential attributes (ṣifāt dhātiyya) and two other categories—negative and active attributes. The author also provides a fascinating linguistic note on the word 'dhat' (essence). By studying this passage, you will grasp a key nuance in divine attribute theology and see how Arabic philology can illuminate theological terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Let the author's own words settle before analysing.
2
Identify the two excluded categories
The passage says that 'negative and active attributes' are excluded when attributes are added to the essence. Find these two terms and note what they are excluded from.
They are excluded from being considered essential attributes.
3
Understand their status
According to the Ash'aris, neither negative nor active attributes are eternal (qadim), nor do they subsist in Allah's essence. Contrast this with essential attributes which are eternal and subsisting.
4
Explore the etymology of 'dhat'
The author explains that the word 'dhat' originates from 'dhawar'. Notice the phonetic changes: dropping the medial waw, changing the final waw to alif, and adding the feminine ta. This shows how Arabic morphology works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخرج بإضافة الصفات إلى الذات: السلبية والفعلية، فليس شيء منهما بقديم عند الأشاعرة، ولا قائم بذاته تعالى. وأصل الذات: ذور; فحذفت العين لكراهة الواوين، ثم قلبت اللام ألفا وألحقت بها التاء المجرورة، والله أعلم.
And by adding the attributes to the essence, the negative (salbiyya) and active (fi'liyya) attributes are excluded. So neither of them is eternal (qadim) according to the Ash'aris, nor do they subsist in His essence, may He be exalted. And the origin of 'dhat' is 'dhawar'; then the medial radical was omitted due to dislike of two waws, then the final radical was turned into an alif, and the genitive ta' was attached to it. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of Allah's attributes. The author states that when attributes are added to the essence (i.e., when we speak of essential attributes), the negative attributes (ṣifāt salbiyya, such as qidam, baqa', etc.) and the active attributes (ṣifāt fi'liyya, such as creation and provision) are excluded. According to the Ash'aris, these two categories are not eternal (qadima) nor do they subsist in Allah's essence. The essential attributes (ṣifāt dhatiyya) are eternal and subsist in His essence. The author then gives the etymology of the word 'dhat' (essence): its origin is 'dhawar' (with two waws), but the waw in the medial position was dropped to avoid two consecutive waws, and then the final waw was changed to an alif, and the feminine suffix ta was added, yielding 'dhat'.

Key Terms
الصفات السلبية
الصفات الفعلية
القديم
قيام بذاته
ذات
أصل الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All attributes of Allah are equally eternal and subsist in His essence.
Correction: The passage teaches that negative and active attributes are not eternal (qadima) nor do they subsist in the essence, according to the Ash'aris.

Checkpoint

A) Essential attributes (sifat dhatiyya)
B) Negative and active attributes (salbiyya and fi'liyya)
C) All attributes without exception
D) The seven attributes of essence

Key Takeaway
Negative and active attributes are distinct from essential attributes: they are not eternal and do not subsist in Allah's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You have learned that 'dhat' etymologically means something with a core being. Yet your own 'self' is fleeting and contingent. The Pre-Eternity of Essential Attributes
Sanusi states the Ash'ari position on pre-eternity of essential attributes and exclusion of negative and active attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari distinction between essential, negative, and active attributes, and why only essential attributes are considered pre-eternal (qadim) in the technical sense.

Core Idea
Only essential attributes are truly pre-eternal; negative and active attributes are not qadim.

In this passage, Sanusi clarifies the Ash'ari position on which divine attributes are considered pre-eternal. He explains that while essential attributes subsist in God's essence eternally, negative and active attributes do not qualify as qadim in the technical sense. However, a nuance emerges: negative attributes are still eternal (azali), just not 'qadim' because that term applies to existent qualities. This lesson unpacks this subtle distinction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification of attributes
Attributes in kalam are divided into essential, negative, and active. Essential attributes are those that are intrinsic to God's essence, while negative attributes are those that deny imperfections, and active attributes are those related to God's actions.
This classification is foundational for the Ash'ari position.
2
Read the passage and note the exclusion
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the statement: 'فليس شيء منهما بقديم' (nothing of them is pre-eternal). Understand that both negative and active attributes are excluded from being qadim.
The passage also says they do not subsist in God's essence.
3
Examine the gloss on negative attributes
The gloss from al-Amir explains that negative attributes are still eternal (azali) but not 'qadim' because the term qadim is reserved for existent qualities. Reflect on why this nuance matters for theological precision.
Al-Nafrawi even suggests mentioning 'negative' here might have been a slip.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان قدم أسمائه تعالى وصفاته الذاتية عند أهل السنة

وخرج بإضافة الصفات إلى الذات: السلبية والفعلية، فليس شيء منهما بقديم عند الأشاعرة، ولا قائم بذاته تعالى.

قوله: (فليس شيء منها بقديم) أما بالنسبة لصفات الأفعال فظاهر، وأما بالنسبة للصفات السلبية فلا يجوز أن يقال فيها أنها ليست بقديمة، قال الأمير: كأنه رأى اختصاص القدم بالوجودي وإلا فالأولى حذف السلبية فإنه تعالى موصوف بها أزلا. ورأيت بخط سيدي أحمد النفراوي أن ذكرها سبق قلم وإلا ففضل الشارح مشهور. الأمير.
Statement on the pre-eternity of His names and essential attributes according to Ahl al-Sunnah.

And by the addition of attributes to the Essence, [the categories of] the negative and the active [attributes] are excluded. So nothing of them is pre-eternal (qadim) according to the Ash'aris, nor subsisting in His Essence.

His statement: '(Nothing of them is pre-eternal)' – as for active attributes, it is apparent. As for negative attributes, it is not permissible to say that they are not pre-eternal. Al-Amir said: It is as if he saw that pre-eternity (qidam) is specific to the existent; otherwise, it would be better to omit the negative [category], for He is described by them eternally (azalan). And I saw in the handwriting of my master Ahmad al-Nafrawi that mentioning them was a slip of the pen; otherwise, the virtue of the commentator is well-known. – Al-Amir.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage begins with a section heading: 'Statement on the pre-eternity of His names and essential attributes according to Ahl al-Sunnah.' It then presents the Ash'ari position: when attributing qualities to God's Essence, negative and active attributes are excluded from the category of essential attributes. Consequently, neither negative nor active attributes are considered pre-eternal (qadim) in the Ash'ari school, nor do they subsist in the Divine Essence. The passage then includes a gloss from al-Amir clarifying the nuance regarding negative attributes: although it is commonly said they are not 'qadim,' this requires careful interpretation. According to one view, the term 'qadim' specifically applies to existent entities, while negative attributes are non-existent (absence of imperfections). Thus, it is more accurate to say that God is described by negative attributes eternally (azali), not that they are 'qadim' in the technical sense. The gloss also references a comment by Ahmad al-Nafrawi suggesting that including 'negative' in the list might have been a slip, but other commentators defend the inclusion.

Key Terms
قديم (qadim, pre-eternal)
أزلي (azali, eternal without beginning)
صفات ذاتية (essential attributes)
صفات سلبية (negative attributes)
صفات فعلية (active attributes)
أشاعرة (Ash'aris)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That negative attributes are not eternal at all.
Correction: Negative attributes are eternal (azali) but not considered 'qadim' because the term qadim is reserved for existent qualities.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'aris, which type of attributes is NOT considered pre-eternal (qadim) in the technical sense?
A) Essential attributes
B) Negative attributes
C) Active attributes
D) Both negative and active attributes

Key Takeaway
Only essential attributes are pre-eternal (qadim) in the Ash'ari tradition; negative and active attributes are not technically qadim, though negative attributes are eternal (azali).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you meditate on God's perfection, which negative attribute (e.g., 'He is not unjust') exposes an area where you are still struggling to submit? What is one concrete step you will take this week to embody that perfection in your dealings?
?
The lesson teaches that only essential attributes are truly pre-eternal. Does your heart feel greater awe for God as the Pre-eternal Essence or as the Eternally Perfect? How will that shift the focus of your worship today?
?
If God's active attributes are not pre-eternal, He is not dependent on His creation. How does that truth challenge your tendency to rely on worldly causes rather than on Him alone? What will you consciously entrust to Him today?

The Principle of Tawqifiyyah in Divine Names and Attributes
Sanusi states the doctrine that God's names and attributes are tawqifiyyah and explains the categories and scholarly views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the doctrine of tawqifiyyah and the scholarly consensus and disagreement regarding the use of names and attributes for Allah.

Core Idea
God's names and attributes require scriptural authorization — they cannot be invented.

This lesson explores a foundational principle of Islamic theology: that the names and attributes we use for Allah are not left to human reason but are tawqifiyyah — established only by divine revelation. Sanusi explains the categories of names/attributes and the positions of major scholars, showing both unity and divergence in this crucial area.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then read the literal translation. This helps you get the rhythm and understand the flow.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the meaning of 'tawqifiyyah'
Focus on the definition: names and attributes are tawqifiyyah, meaning they depend on permission from revelation (Qur'an or Sunnah). No one may use a name or attribute without this permission.
3
Identify the two categories of names/attributes
The passage divides them into those indicating pure perfection and those mixed with anthropomorphism. Note the different rulings for each category.
Mixed ones are unanimously prohibited without permission; pure perfection ones are debated.
4
Understand the areas of agreement and disagreement
There are three agreed-upon issues (permissible with permission, prohibited with prohibition, prohibited if mixed without permission) and one point of difference: pure perfection terms without explicit scriptural permission.
This single point of disagreement is between Ahl al-Sunnah (require permission) and Mu'tazila (allow if no deficiency).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات

بيان توقيف إطلاق أسماء الله وصفاته على إذن من الشارع

٣٩ . واختير أن أسماه توقيفية كذا الصفات فاحفظ السمعية

(واختير) أي: واختار جمهور أهل السنة أن أسماه توقيفية أي: تعليمية يتوقف جواز إطلاقها عليه على تعليم. ومعنى كونها توقيفية: أنه لا يجوز لأحد أن يثبت لله صفة أو يسميه باسم إلا أن يرد نص عن الله أو رسوله يبيح إطلاق هذا الاسم أو الاتصاف بهذه الصفة. ويجب أن تعلم قبل ذلك أن كلًا من الاسم والصفة إما أن يشمل على كمال محض لا يشوبه نقص ولا تشبيه، وإما أن يدل على كمال مع شائبة تشبيه؛ فإن كان كل من الاسم والصفة يدل على كمال مشوب بنوع تشبيه فقد أجمع العلماء على أنه لا يجوز أن يطلق على الله واحد منهما بغير إذن، فإن ورد الإذن أطلقناه عليه ونفينا عنه ما خالطه التشبيه. وإن كان كل من الاسم والصفة يدل على الكمال المحض، فأهل السنة على أنه لا يجوز أن يطلق على الله واحد منهما إلا بإذن خاص. وذهب المعتزلة إلى جواز إثبات ما كان الله متصفًا بمعناه ما دام لا يوهم نقصًا. ومال إليه القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني، وتوقف فيه إمام الحرمين، وفرق الغزالي بين الاسم والصفة فقال: يجوز إطلاق الصفة – وهي ما دل على معنى زائد على الذات – ومنع إطلاق الاسم – وهو ما دل على نفس الذات. والحاصل أن علماء الإسلام اتفقوا على جواز إطلاق الأسماء والصفات على الباري إذا ورد بهما الإذن من الشارع مطلقًا، وعلى امتناع إطلاق واحد منهما عليه إذا ورد المنع من إطلاقه، واتفقوا أيضًا على عدم جواز إطلاق واحد منهما إذا كان يدل على كمال مشوب بما لا يجوز عليه ولم يرد به الإذن. فهذه ثلاث مسائل اتفقوا عليها، ومحل اختلافهم مسألة واحدة وهي: فيما لم يرد عن الشارع نص بالإطلاق أو بالمنع منه، وكان الاسم أو الصفة يدل على كمال محض. ومما ورد الإذن بإطلاقه عليه تعالى وهو موهم بحسب وضع اللغة، ويجب على ما قدمنا.
Section of Divine Matters

Statement on the restriction of applying God's names and attributes to permission from the Lawgiver

39. And it is chosen that His names are tawqifiyyah, likewise the attributes — so preserve the revealed (hearing) [doctrine].

(And it is chosen) meaning: the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah chose that His names are tawqifiyyah, i.e., instructional, such that the permissibility of applying them to Him depends on instruction. The meaning of being tawqifiyyah is that it is not permissible for anyone to affirm for God an attribute or to name Him with a name unless a text from God or His Messenger occurs that permits applying this name or being described with this attribute. And you must know before that that each of the name and the attribute either includes pure perfection that is not tainted by any deficiency or anthropomorphism, or indicates perfection with a touch of anthropomorphism. If each of the name and attribute indicates perfection mixed with a type of anthropomorphism, then the scholars have agreed that it is not permissible to apply either of them to God without permission. If permission comes, we apply it to Him and negate from Him what was mixed with anthropomorphism. If each of the name and attribute indicates pure perfection, then Ahl al-Sunnah hold that it is not permissible to apply either of them to God except with specific permission. The Mu'tazila held the permissibility of affirming what God is described by in terms of its meaning as long as it does not imply deficiency. Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani inclined to this view, Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) suspended judgment, and al-Ghazali distinguished between the name and attribute, saying: it is permissible to apply the attribute – which indicates a meaning additional to the Essence – and he prohibited applying the name – which indicates the Essence itself. The conclusion is that the scholars of Islam agreed on the permissibility of applying names and attributes to the Creator if permission from the Lawgiver has come absolutely, and on the prohibition of applying either of them to Him if prohibition from applying it has come. They also agreed on the impermissibility of applying either of them if it indicates perfection mixed with what is not permissible for Him and no permission has come for it. So these are three issues they agreed upon. Their point of disagreement is one issue: concerning what no textual permission or prohibition has come from the Lawgiver, and the name or attribute indicates pure perfection. And among what permission has come to apply to Him is what is suggestive [of anthropomorphism] according to the linguistic convention, and it is obligatory according to what we have presented.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the doctrine that God's names and attributes are 'tawqifiyyah'—meaning they can only be used for God with explicit permission from revelation (the Qur'an or authentic Hadith). The passage begins with a verse from the poem: 'And it is chosen that His names are tawqifiyyah, likewise the attributes — so preserve the revealed [doctrine].' Then the commentary explains the meaning: the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah hold that it is not lawful to name God with any name or describe Him with any attribute unless a scriptural text permits it. The passage then divides names and attributes into two categories: those that indicate pure perfection (without any deficiency or anthropomorphic implication) and those that indicate perfection mixed with some anthropomorphic suggestion. For names/attributes that have anthropomorphic connotations, scholars unanimously agree they are not allowed without scriptural permission; if permission comes, they are affirmed while negating the anthropomorphic aspect. For names/attributes that denote pure perfection, Ahl al-Sunnah require specific permission from revelation, whereas the Mu'tazila allow them as long as they do not imply deficiency. The passage then notes the positions of al-Baqillani (inclining to the Mu'tazila), al-Juwayni (suspending judgment), and al-Ghazali (distinguishing between names and attributes). Finally, it summarizes the points of agreement (three issues) and the single point of disagreement: names/attributes of pure perfection for which no explicit scriptural permission or prohibition exists. The passage emphasizes that even if a term is misleading according to linguistic convention, if permission has come, it must be used while negating the misleading connotations.

Key Terms
توقيفية (tawqifiyyah, dependent on revelation)
اسم (name)
صفة (attribute)
إذن (permission)
كمال محض (pure perfection)
تشبيه (anthropomorphism)
سمعية (revealed, hearing)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)
باقلاني (al-Baqillani)
إمام الحرمين (al-Juwayni)
غزالي (al-Ghazali)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a name denotes pure perfection, it can be used for God without any scriptural proof.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunnah require a specific scriptural permission even for pure perfection names/attributes; only the Mu'tazila allow them without such permission.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the single point of disagreement among scholars regarding the application of names and attributes to God?
A) Whether names/attributes that indicate pure perfection require scriptural permission
B) Whether names/attributes with anthropomorphic connotations can ever be used
C) Whether al-Ghazali's distinction between name and attribute is valid
D) Whether the Mu'tazila are correct in allowing any name not implying deficiency

Key Takeaway
The principle of tawqifiyyah demands that all divine names and attributes, even those of pure perfection, require explicit scriptural authorization according to Ahl al-Sunnah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a description of Allah in your prayer or speech without checking if it has a basis in the Quran or Sunnah? What does the principle of tawqifiyyah demand you do differently starting today?
?
The passage says even pure perfection names require permission. Which beautiful names of Allah do you use most often? Are they all from the revelation, or have you unknowingly adopted a name from culture or philosophy?
?
If you were to stop using any name or attribute for Allah that isn't explicitly revealed, which ones would you have to give up? Are you ready to surrender even 'good-sounding' descriptions out of reverence for His sole authority to name Himself?

The Tawqeefiyyah of Allah's Names and Attributes
Al-Laqqani defines the principle of tawqeefiyyah and illustrates it with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the doctrine that Allah's names and attributes require scriptural authorization (tawqeefiyyah) and how names like as-Sabur, ash-Shakur, and al-Halim are properly understood.

Core Idea
Allah’s names are not invented; they must come from revelation and be interpreted without deficiency.

This lesson covers a foundational principle in Sunni theology: the names and attributes of Allah are tawqeefiyyah—they cannot be derived from human reason alone. Al-Laqqani explains the consensus on this matter and provides examples of how certain names are interpreted to avoid anthropomorphism. This ensures our understanding of Allah remains free from any imperfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation, to grasp the overall argument before analyzing details.
Notice the logical flow: definition, ruling, analogy, examples.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the statement that Ahl al-Sunnah chose the view that Allah's names are tawqeefiyyah. Understand that this means permissibility depends on scriptural permission.
3
Examine the ruling on names
Look at how the passage distinguishes between names that are permitted (indicating praise) and those that are not (prohibited). Note the analogy with human naming.
4
Study the three examples of interpretation
For each of as-Sabur, ash-Shakur, al-Halim, note the potential lexical deficiency and how it is reinterpreted to befit Allah.
These examples illustrate the principle of ta'wil (interpretation) to avoid anthropomorphism.
5
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider why this doctrine matters: it safeguards tawhid by ensuring no name or attribute is used for Allah without revelation and proper interpretation.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان توقيف إطلاق أسماء الله وصفاته على إذن من الشارع
واختيرَ أنَّ اسْماهُ تَوْقِيفيَّة كذَا الصِّفاتُ فاحفظ السَّمْعِيَّة
(وَاخْتِيرَ) أي: واختار جمهور أهل السُّنَّة أَنَّ أَسْمَاهُ تَوْقِيفِيَّه أي: تعليميَّةٌ يتوقف جواز إطلاقها عليه تعالى على تعليم الشارع وإذْنِه في ذلك، بأن يُسمَع مِن لسانه بطريق صحيح أو حَسَنٍ، أو يأذن في استعماله كذلك؛ فما أَذنَ في إطلاقه واستعماله ممَّا لم يكن إطلاقه مُوهما نقصاً بل كان مُشعرًا بالمدح .. جازَ اتفاقا، وما لا؛ فعلى المنع والتَّحريم؛ إذْ لا يجوز أنْ يُسمَّى النَّبِيُّ الله بما ليس من أسمائه، بل لو سُمِّيَ واحدٌ مِن أفرادِ النَّاس بما لم يُسمِّه به أبواه لما ارتضاه، فالباري تعالى أولى، وليس الكلام في أسمائه الأعلام الموضوعة في اللغات، وإِنَّما الكلام في تأويله بمعنى لا يوهم: الصَّبور، والشَّكور، والحليم، فإنَّ الصَّبور يوهم وصول مشقَّةٍ له تعالى بحسب أصل وضع اللغة، لأنَّ الصَّبر عند أهل اللُّغة: حبسُ النَّفس على المشاق» لكنَّه يُطلق ويفسر بمعنى يليق به تعالى، فالصبور ـ على هذا ـ: هو الذي لا يُعجِّل بعقوبة من عصاه، والشكور يوهم وصول إحسان إليه من خلقه، لأنَّ معنی الشكر لغة: الثَّناء على المُحسن، فيُفسر في حقه سبحانه يليق به، وهو: الذي يُجازي على قليل الطَّاعات بمعني الدرجات، ويُعطي بسبب العمل في الدنيا وهي أيام معدودة نعما في الآخرة غير محدودة، والحليم يوهم وصول أذى إليه تعالى، وهو سبحانه لا يصل إليه أذى، فيفسر الحليم في حقه: بالذي لا يُعجِّل بعقوبة من عصاه، فيرجع لمعنى الصَّبور، وهلم جرا.
Explanation of the restriction of applying Allah's names and attributes to permission from the Lawgiver. And it was chosen that His names are tawqeefiyyah (restricted to revelation) and likewise the attributes, so preserve the revealed [names]. (And it was chosen) i.e., the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah chose that His names are tawqeefiyyah, i.e., instructional, such that the permissibility of applying them to Him, exalted, depends on instruction from the Lawgiver and His permission in that, by being heard from His tongue via an authentic or good chain, or by His permitting its use likewise. So what He permitted in its application and use, where its application does not imply deficiency but rather indicates praise, is permissible by consensus. And what [He did not permit] is subject to prohibition and forbiddance, for it is not permissible that the Prophet name Allah with what is not among His names. Rather, if a single individual among people were named with what his parents did not name him, he would not be pleased with it, so the Creator, exalted, is more worthy. And the discussion is not about His proper names placed in languages; rather, the discussion is about interpreting [these names] in a sense that does not imply [deficiency]: as-Sabur (the Patient), ash-Shakur (the Appreciative), al-Halim (the Forbearing). Indeed, as-Sabur implies the arrival of hardship to Him, exalted, according to the original lexical meaning, because patience according to the people of language is 'restraining the soul from difficulties,' but it is applied and interpreted in a sense befitting Him, exalted. So as-Sabur, according to this, is He who does not hasten to punish those who disobey Him. And ash-Shakur implies the arrival of beneficence to Him from His creation, because the meaning of gratitude (shukr) lexically is 'praising the benefactor,' so it is interpreted in His right, glorified, in a befitting sense, and He is the One who rewards for few acts of obedience with many degrees, and gives due to [one's] work in the world, which is limited days, bliss in the Hereafter without limit. And al-Halim implies the arrival of harm to Him, exalted, and He, glorified, is not reached by harm, so al-Halim is interpreted in His right as: He who does not hasten to punish those who disobey Him, thus returning to the meaning of as-Sabur, and so on.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage defines and argues for the doctrine that Allah's names and attributes are tawqeefiyyah, meaning they can only be applied to Allah based on explicit permission from the Lawgiver (the Qur'an and Sunnah). The passage begins by stating the chosen view of the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah: both names and attributes are tawqeefiyyah. It explains that the permissibility of naming Allah with a name or attributing a quality to Him depends on hearing it from the Lawgiver via a sound or good chain of transmission, or by His explicit permission. It then clarifies the ruling: if the Lawgiver has permitted its use and the name or attribute does not imply any deficiency but rather praise, it is permissible by consensus. If it does not have permission, then it is prohibited. The author then provides an analogy: just as a person would not accept being named by something other than what his parents named him, it is even more unfitting for Allah to be named without His permission. The passage then distinguishes between proper names (a'lam) that are customarily used in languages (which are not the subject of dispute) and descriptive names or attributes that require interpretation (ta'wil) to avoid implying imperfection. It gives three examples: as-Sabur (the Patient), ash-Shakur (the Appreciative), and al-Halim (the Forbearing). For each, it shows how the lexical meaning might imply a deficiency (e.g., patience implying hardship, appreciation implying receiving favor, forbearance implying harm), but then explains that when applied to Allah, these names are interpreted in a way befitting His majesty—meaning He does not hasten punishment, He rewards abundantly, and He does not rush to punish. Thus, the actual meaning avoids any anthropomorphism or deficiency. The passage concludes by noting that other similar names follow the same pattern.

Key Terms
توقيفية (tawqeefiyyah)
الاسم (al-ism)
الصفة (as-sifah)
الإذن (al-idhn)
التأويل (at-ta'wil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think any name conveying perfection can be used for Allah without restriction.
Correction: Even perfect-meaning names require explicit scriptural permission and proper interpretation to avoid implied deficiency.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on applying a name to Allah that the Lawgiver has permitted and that indicates praise?
A) Permissible by consensus
B) Prohibited unless it also implies deficiency
C) Permissible only if it is a proper name in any language
D) Subject to disagreement among scholars

Key Takeaway
Allah's names and attributes are revealed and must be interpreted in a way that avoids any implication of deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Condition of Sam'iyya for Divine Names, Attributes, and Actions
Affirms tawqif condition for attributes and actions, not only names

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why invoking Divine names, attributes, and actions requires explicit Scriptural authorization and how consensus serves as a proof.

Core Idea
To know Allah's names and attributes, only revelation holds the keys.

This lesson examines the theological principle of tawqif (restrictive authorization) in applying names and attributes to Allah. The author argues that the same condition applies not only to names but also to attributes and actions. This principle safeguards against unwarranted speculation about the Divine.

Guided Path
1
Understand the disagreement on derived names
Read the opening lines that introduce the disagreement (khilaf) regarding names derived from attributes (asma' ma'khudha). Note that these names indicate a meaning additional to the essence.
The derived names are like the basic names in requiring Scriptural permission.
2
Grasp the condition of Scriptural permission
Focus on the statement that applying names and attributes depends on Scriptural permission. The author urges you to preserve this condition and not to apply what is not proven by hearing (sam').
The imperative 'fahfaz al-sam'iyya' is a key reminder.
3
Recognize the scope: names, attributes, and actions
Notice that the passage explicitly extends the condition to attributes and actions, not just names. The phrase 'kadha al-sifat min al-sifat wa al-af'al' confirms this.
4
Define sam'iyya and its sources
Identify the definition of sam'iyya: what is conveyed by the Qur'an, an authentic or good Sunnah, or consensus (ijma'). Understand that consensus is never outside these sources.
This definition is crucial for applying the condition.
5
Apply the principle to avoid error
Reflect on the examples given: al-Sabur and al-Shakur (implying deficiency) vs. al-'Alim and al-Qadir (not implying). Both categories require Scriptural authorization.
This guards against the misconception that non-deficient attributes need no authorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات الخلاف في الأسماء المأخوذة : ما دل على معنى زائد على الذات، أي: أنَّها مثل الأسماء وهي: في أنَّ المختارَ أنَّ إطلاقها عليه تعالى بالشَّرط السَّابق يتوقف على الإذنِ الشّرعيّ (فَاحْفَظِ السَّمْعِيَّة ) (٢) أي : إذا عرفتَ أنَّ إطلاق الأسماء والصفاتِ عليه تعالى يتوقف على الإذن الشَّرعيّ فَامتنع من إطلاق ما لم يثبت بسماع إطلاقه عليه تعالى منها(۳) ، ولا تتجاوز السَّمْعيَّة ، سواءٌ أُوهمت: كالصَّبور، والشَّكورِ، (٤) ، أو لم تُوهم كالعالِم ،والقادِر والمراد بالسمعية : ما ورد به كتاب، أو سُنَّةٌ صحيحة أو حسنةٌ، أو إجماع؛ لأنَّه غير خارج عنها(ه)، كذا الصفات من الصفات والأفعال (۱) ، (كَذَا الصِّفَاتُ) من = الاحتياج لإذن، قلنا: إن سلم الإجماع فكفى به دليلا الأمير.
Section on Divinity. The disagreement regarding the derived names: what indicates a meaning additional to the essence, i.e., that they are like the names, and they are: that the chosen view is that applying them to Him, exalted is He, with the previous condition depends on Scriptural permission (so preserve the sam'iyya) (2) i.e., if you know that applying the names and attributes to Him, exalted is He, depends on Scriptural permission, then refrain from applying what has not been proven by hearing its application to Him from them (3), and do not exceed the sam'iyya, whether they imply [deficiency] like al-Sabur and al-Shakur (4), or do not imply like al-'Alim and al-Qadir. And what is meant by al-sam'iyya is what is conveyed by a Book, an authentic or good Sunnah, or consensus; because it is not outside of them (5). Similarly the attributes from attributes and actions (1), (and similarly the attributes) from = need for permission, we said: if consensus is accepted, it suffices as evidence. Al-Amir.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage discusses the disagreement (khilaf) regarding names derived from attributes that indicate a meaning additional to the essence. It affirms that the chosen view is that applying such names to Allah depends on the same condition of Scriptural permission as the basic names. The phrase 'So preserve the sam'iyya' (fahfaz al-sam'iyya) is a summary instruction. The passage explains that one must refrain from applying any name or attribute that is not established by hearing (sam') from revelation, whether the name implies deficiency (like al-Sabur, al-Shakur) or does not (like al-'Alim, al-Qadir). It then defines sam'iyya as that which is conveyed by the Qur'an, an authentic or good hadith, or consensus (ijma'), because consensus is never outside these sources. Finally, it extends the same ruling to attributes and actions: the condition applies to them as well. The commentary adds that if consensus is accepted, it suffices as evidence.

سمعية
أسماء مأخوذة
إذن شرعي
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that attributes derived from actions (like 'al-Muhyi') can be used without Scriptural authorization as long as they do not imply deficiency.
Correction: The passage explicitly extends the condition to attributes and actions, requiring Scriptural authorization for all, regardless of implied deficiency.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling on applying attributes derived from actions (such as 'al-Muhyi') to Allah?
A) They are allowed by consensus.
B) They are allowed only if they do not imply deficiency.
C) They require Scriptural authorization like the names.
D) They are prohibited unless they are mentioned in the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
All Divine names, attributes, and actions require Scriptural authorization; consensus is a valid source for this authorization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says even 'good' attributes like 'al-Shakur' require scriptural authorization. Is there a 'good' quality you have been attributing to Allah without verifying it from revelation? How will you correct that starting today?

The Condition for Naming Allah with Revealed Names
Sanusi explains that naming Allah requires Scriptural permission and defines al-sam'iyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that naming Allah with attributes depends on Scriptural permission, and what constitutes valid Scriptural evidence (sam'iyya).

Core Idea
We cannot name Allah from our own reasoning; only revelation authorizes His names.

Sanusi clarifies an essential methodological principle: how do we know which names and attributes to use for Allah? This passage establishes that even seemingly praiseworthy names must be grounded in revelation, and it defines the sources that count as 'sam'iyya' — the revealed basis for naming.

Guided Path
1
Understand the subject of disagreement
The passage begins by noting the disagreement about names that indicate an attribute added to the essence. The chosen view is that applying these names requires Scriptural permission.
2
Identify the ruling for naming Allah
Sanusi emphasizes that one must only use names established by revelation, whether or not the name might imply imperfection in human language.
Examples: al-Sabur and al-Shakur might imply imperfection, but they are permitted because they are revealed.
3
Define sam'iyya
Learn the definition: sam'iyya includes the Quran, authentic (sahih) and good (hasan) hadith, and consensus.
Consensus is included because it is based on these sources.
4
Distinguish theoretical vs practical matters
The passage ends by noting that weak hadith and analogy are not valid for theoretical (belief) matters, but for practical (speech) matters they have some leeway.
Weak hadith (except extremely weak) and analogy are acceptable in practical usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلاف في الأسماء المأخوذة : ما دل على معنى زائد على الذات، أي: أنَّها مثل الأسماء وهي: في أنَّ المختارَ أنَّ إطلاقها عليه تعالى بالشَّرط السَّابق يتوقف على الإذنِ الشّرعيّ (فَاحْفَظِ السَّمْعِيَّة ) أي : إذا عرفتَ أنَّ إطلاق الأسماء والصفاتِ عليه تعالى يتوقف على الإذن الشَّرعيّ فَامتنع من إطلاق ما لم يثبت بسماع إطلاقه عليه تعالى منها، ولا تتجاوز السَّمْعِيَّة ، سواءٌ أُوهمت: كالصَّبور، والشَّكورِ، أو لم تُوهم كالعالِم ،والقادِر والمراد بالسمعية : ما ورد به كتاب، أو سُنَّةٌ صحيحة أو حسنةٌ، أو إجماع؛ لأنَّه غير خارج عنها، الصفات والأفعال (كَذَا الصِّفَاتُ) من والاحتياج لإذن، قلنا: إن سلم الإجماع فكفى به دليلا. بخلافِ السُّنَّة الضَّعيفة والقياس أيضا، إن قلنا: إنَّ المسألة مِن العِلْميَّات أمَّا إِنْ قلنا: إنَّها مِن العمليَّات فالسُّنَّة الضَّعيفة كالحسنة ، إلَّا الواهية جدًّا والقياسُ كالإجماع.
The disagreement concerning taken names: that which indicates an attribute additional to the essence, i.e., they are like names, in that the chosen view is that applying them to Him, exalted, according to the previous condition, depends on Scriptural permission. So preserve the sam'iyya (revelation-based naming). That is: when you know that applying names and attributes to Him, exalted, depends on Scriptural permission, then refrain from applying what has not been established by hearing its application to Him, and do not exceed the sam'iyya, whether it implies [imperfection], like al-Sabur (the Patient) and al-Shakur (the Appreciative), or does not imply, like al-'Alim (the Knower) and al-Qadir (the Powerful). And by 'sam'iyya' is meant what is conveyed by the Book, or an authentic or good Sunnah, or consensus, because it is not outside them. Attributes and actions (likewise attributes) require permission. We said: if consensus is granted, it suffices as evidence. Contrary to the weak Sunnah and analogy as well, if we say the matter is from theoretical matters; but if we say it is from practical matters, then the weak Sunnah is like the good one, except the extremely weak, and analogy is like consensus.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a classification and ruling on the divine names and attributes. It begins by stating the subject of disagreement: names that indicate an attribute added to the essence. The chosen position is that applying such names to Allah depends on prior condition, namely Scriptural permission. The author then emphasizes 'preserve the sam'iyya,' meaning that one must only use names and attributes that have been established by revelation. The ruling applies to all names, whether they potentially imply imperfection (like al-Sabur and al-Shakur, which in human language imply patience or gratitude, but for Allah they have transcendent meanings) or not (like al-'Alim and al-Qadir). The definition of sam'iyya is then given: it encompasses what is found in the Quran, a sound (sahih) or good (hasan) hadith, or consensus, as consensus must be based on one of these. The author then mentions that weak hadith and analogy are not acceptable in this matter if the issue is theoretical (i.e., belief), but if it is practical (i.e., usage in speech), weak hadith (except extremely weak) and analogy can be used like consensus. The passage guards against using reason alone to invent names for Allah.

السمعية
الإذن الشرعي
الصبور
الشكور
القياس
الإجماع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any name that implies praise can be applied to Allah.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that even seemingly praiseworthy names like al-Sabur and al-Shakur require Scriptural permission to be used.

Checkpoint
What is the 'sam'iyya' in the context of naming Allah?
A) Names derived from rational inference
B) Names that are established by the Quran, sound hadith, or consensus
C) Names that are metaphorical
D) Names that are common in human language

Key Takeaway
Naming Allah requires Scriptural permission from the Quran, authentic hadith, or consensus; weak hadith and analogy are not valid for theoretical beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This lesson teaches that even seemingly positive names like al-Sabur require Scriptural permission. Is there a positive attribute you have attributed to Allah without realizing it might not be established? What concrete step will you take to rectify this in your knowledge and worship?

The Two Meanings of Kalam Allah and Quran
Explains the two meanings of 'Kalam Allah' and 'Quran' with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that both 'Kalam Allah' and 'Quran' have two distinct meanings: the eternal attribute of speech and the created recited words, and that only the attribute is eternal.

Core Idea
The Quran is uncreated as an attribute, but the recited words are created.

This passage establishes a critical distinction in Islamic theology: the divine attribute of speech is eternal and uncreated, while the words we recite and write are created. Before understanding why the Mu'tazila erred, we must grasp this dual usage of the terms 'Kalam Allah' and 'Quran'. The following text from the commentary clarifies this foundation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings for 'Kalam Allah'
Look for the sentence where the commentator says 'Kalam Allah is used in two senses'. Write down the two definitions.
The first meaning is the eternal attribute without sound or letter.
2
Find the evidence for the second meaning
The commentator provides a Quranic verse and a saying of Aisha. Notice how they show that the recited Quran is called 'Kalam Allah'.
3
Apply the same to the term 'Quran'
See how 'Quran' also has two meanings. Conclude: when meaning the attribute, it is qadim; when meaning the recited words, it is makhluq.
This is the foundation for the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات
بيانُ أنَّ كلام الله تعالى قَديمٌ عند أهلِ السُّنَّةَ خِلافًا للمعتزلة
وَنَزِّهِ القُرْآنَ أَي كَلَامَهُ عَنْ الحُدُوْثِ وَاحْذَرِ انتِقَامَهُ
ثُمَّ شَرع في مسألة خَلق القُرآن ، فقالَ: (وَنَزِّهِ القُرْآنَ) أي: يجب عليكَ أن تنزهه عن الحدوث، وقد قدَّمنا في مبحث صفة الكلام أنَّ مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة أنَّ كُلَّا مِن «كلام الله» و«القرآن» يطلق بإطلاقين ؛ فأمَّا كلام الله» فيطلق ويراد به الصفة القديمة القائمة بذاته تعالى التي ليست بصوت ولا حرف، وهو بهذا المعنى ،قديم، ويطلق بإطلاق آخر ويُراد به القرآن الكريم الذي نتلوه بألسنتنا، ويسمعه بعضنا من بعض ونكتبه بأيدينا في مصاحفنا، ووجه إطلاق كلام الله على القرآن المتلو : أنَّه دال على الصفة القديمة القائم بذاته تعالى، أو أنه سبحانه هو الخالق له على هذا النظم ، بهذه الألفاظ ، وهذا الترتيب المُعجز، وليس لأحدٍ فيه شيء ما ، وقد أطلق الله تعالى على القرآن الكريم كلام الله» بهذا المعنى في قوله سبحانه: وَإِنْ أَحَدٌ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ اسْتَجَارَكَ فَأَجِرْهُ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلَمَ اللهِ ﴾ [التوبة: ٦] وأطلقت عائشة الصديقية «كلام الله على المكتوب في المصاحب في قولها: «ما بين دفتي المصحف کلام الله ونُسب هذا إلى كثير من الصحابة، وأما «القرآن» فيطلق أيضا بهاذين الإطلاقين ؛ فتارة يطلق ويراد به الصفة القديمة القائمة بذاته تعالى، وتارة يطلق ویراد به ما نتلوه ونكتبه ويسمعه بعضنا من بعض، لما ذكرنا في إطلاق كلام الله» فإنْ أُطلق بالمعنى الأول ـ وهو الصفة القديمة - فهو قديم ، وإنْ أُطلِق بالمعنى الثاني فهو مخلوق ، ومع أَنَّ كلا «القرآن» من و كلام الله» يطلق تارة على الصفة القديمة وتارة أخرى على المتلو الملفوظ به فإن الأكثر إطلاق لفظ «القرآن» على المتلو الملفوظ به، والأكثر إطلاق لفظ «كلام الله» على الصفة القديمة القائمة بذاته تعالى، ولهذا فسَّرَ الناظمُ القرآنَ بكلام الله في قوله: «ونَزِّه القرآن أي: كلامه حتى يكون أظهر في المُراد ، وقد ذكرنا - مع ذلك - أنَّ أئمة هذه الأمة تَحَرَّجُوا أَنْ يصفُوا القرآن بالحدوث مخافة أن يتبادر إلى أذهان العامة أنَّ الصفة القائمة بذاته حادثةٌ ؛ لأنَّ «القرآن» لفظ مشترك بين هذين المعنيين. وقدمنا أنَّ المعتزلة ذهبوا إلى أنَّ «القرآن» و «كلام الله حادثان ، وأنَّه لا معنى لهما إلا الكلام المُرتب المُسْتمِل على الأصوات والحروف ، ولهذا منعوا أن يتصف الله تعالى بالكلام،
Section on Divine Matters. Clarification that the speech of Allah (kalam Allah) is eternal (qadim) according to Ahl al-Sunna, contrary to the Mu'tazila. [The poet said:] 'And declare the Quran, i.e., His speech, free from temporal origination (huduth), and beware of His vengeance.' Then he began the issue of the creation of the Quran, saying: 'And declare the Quran free from temporal origination.' That is, it is incumbent upon you to declare it free from temporal origination. We have already presented in the discussion of the attribute of speech that the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a is that both 'Kalam Allah' and 'al-Quran' are used in two senses. As for 'Kalam Allah', it is used to mean the eternal attribute subsisting in His essence that is neither sound nor letter, and in this sense it is qadim. And it is used in another sense to mean the noble Quran that we recite with our tongues, hear from one another, and write in our mushafs. The reason for calling the recited Quran 'Kalam Allah' is that it indicates the eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, or that He, exalted, is the creator of it in this composition, with these words and this miraculous arrangement, and no one has any part in it. Allah, exalted, has called the noble Quran 'Kalam Allah' in this sense in His saying: 'And if any of the polytheists seeks your protection, then grant him protection so that he may hear the words (kalam) of Allah' [At-Tawba:6]. And A'isha al-Siddiqa called the written text in the mushafs 'Kalam Allah' in her saying: 'What is between the covers of the mushaf is the Kalam of Allah.' This has been attributed to many companions. As for 'al-Quran', it is also used in these two senses: sometimes it is used to mean the eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, and sometimes it is used to mean what we recite, write, and hear from one another, for the reason we mentioned in the usage of 'Kalam Allah'. If it is used in the first sense – which is the eternal attribute – then it is qadim, and if it is used in the second sense then it is created (makhluq). And although both 'al-Quran' and 'Kalam Allah' are used sometimes for the eternal attribute and other times for the recited and uttered words, the more common usage of the word 'al-Quran' is for the recited uttered words, and the more common usage of the word 'Kalam Allah' is for the eternal attribute subsisting in His essence. For this reason the poet interpreted the Quran as the speech of Allah in his saying: 'And declare the Quran, i.e., His speech, free from temporal origination' so that it is clearer in intended meaning. We have also mentioned that the imams of this nation refrained from describing the Quran as temporally originated (hadith) for fear that the minds of the common people would jump to the conclusion that the attribute subsisting in His essence is temporally originated, because 'al-Quran' is a homonym (lafz mushtarak) between these two meanings. And we have previously stated that the Mu'tazila held that 'al-Quran' and 'Kalam Allah' are both temporally originated, and that they have no meaning except the arranged speech consisting of sounds and letters, and for this reason they denied that Allah, exalted, is described with speech.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on divine matters. The poet's line commands to declare the Quran, meaning God's speech, free from temporal origination. The commentator explains the two senses of both 'Kalam Allah' and 'Quran'. First, he reaffirms that Ahl al-Sunna hold that 'Kalam Allah' can refer to the eternal attribute (ancient, not created) which is without sound or letters, and also to the recited Quran. The recited Quran is called 'Kalam Allah' because it indicates the eternal attribute or because God created it with that miraculous composition. He provides evidence: the Quranic verse where 'Kalam Allah' is used for the recited words (Sura Tawba), and the saying of A'isha that the written mushaf contains 'Kalam Allah'. Similarly, 'Quran' is used both ways. The more common usage of 'Quran' is for the recited words, and of 'Kalam Allah' is for the eternal attribute. Hence the poet clarifies by interpreting the Quran as God's speech. The imams avoided calling the Quran 'hadith' (temporally originated) due to ambiguity. Finally, the Mu'tazila are mentioned as those who deny the eternal attribute and consider the Quran as entirely created, consisting only of sounds and letters. This lesson establishes the Ash'ari position on the uncreatedness of the divine attribute of speech.

كلام الله
القرآن
قديم
حادث
صفة
متلو
مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The entire Quran, including the words we recite, is eternal.
Correction: Only the divine attribute of speech is eternal; the recited words are created, though they are called Kalam Allah metaphorically.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'Kalam Allah' mean when it is said to be qadim?
A) The recited Quran
B) The eternal attribute of speech
C) The written mushaf
D) Both the attribute and the recited words

Key Takeaway
Both 'Kalam Allah' and 'Quran' can refer either to the eternal attribute or to the created recited words; only the attribute is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The passage teaches that 'no one has any part in it' (the Quran). Do you ever subtly feel ownership over your understanding or memorization of the Quran? How can you cultivate a deeper awareness that every letter is solely from Allah, leaving no room for pride?

The Caution of the Imams and the Mu'tazila Error
Explains the caution of imams and the Mu'tazila position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunna imams avoided calling the Quran 'hadith' and how the Mu'tazila deviated by denying the eternal attribute of speech.

Core Idea
The imams guarded against ambiguity, while the Mu'tazila denied divine speech entirely.

After learning the two meanings of 'Quran' and 'Kalam Allah', the commentator addresses a practical concern: why early imams refrained from describing the Quran as 'hadith' (temporally originated). He then contrasts this with the Mu'tazila, who denied the eternal attribute altogether. This lesson reinforces the importance of precise theological language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reason for the imams' caution
Find the phrase 'تَحَرَّجُوا أَنْ يصفُوا القرآن بالحدوث' and see the reason given. It is because 'al-Quran' is a homonym.
2
Understand the homonym problem
The commentator explains that the common people might mistakenly think the attribute is created if the recited Quran is called created.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila position
Read the sentence about the Mu'tazila: they considered the Quran and Kalam Allah as both created, denying the attribute. Why? Because they only know the second meaning.
This is the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات
بيانُ أنَّ كلام الله تعالى قَديمٌ عند أهلِ السُّنَّةَ خِلافًا للمعتزلة
وَنَزِّهِ القُرْآنَ أَي كَلَامَهُ عَنْ الحُدُوْثِ وَاحْذَرِ انتِقَامَهُ
ثُمَّ شَرع في مسألة خَلق القُرآن ، فقالَ: (وَنَزِّهِ القُرْآنَ) أي: يجب عليكَ أن تنزهه عن الحدوث، وقد قدَّمنا في مبحث صفة الكلام أنَّ مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة أنَّ كُلَّا مِن «كلام الله» و«القرآن» يطلق بإطلاقين ؛ فأمَّا كلام الله» فيطلق ويراد به الصفة القديمة القائمة بذاته تعالى التي ليست بصوت ولا حرف، وهو بهذا المعنى ،قديم، ويطلق بإطلاق آخر ويُراد به القرآن الكريم الذي نتلوه بألسنتنا، ويسمعه بعضنا من بعض ونكتبه بأيدينا في مصاحفنا، ووجه إطلاق كلام الله على القرآن المتلو : أنَّه دال على الصفة القديمة القائم بذاته تعالى، أو أنه سبحانه هو الخالق له على هذا النظم ، بهذه الألفاظ ، وهذا الترتيب المُعجز، وليس لأحدٍ فيه شيء ما ، وقد أطلق الله تعالى على القرآن الكريم كلام الله» بهذا المعنى في قوله سبحانه: وَإِنْ أَحَدٌ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ اسْتَجَارَكَ فَأَجِرْهُ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلَمَ اللهِ ﴾ [التوبة: ٦] وأطلقت عائشة الصديقية «كلام الله على المكتوب في المصاحب في قولها: «ما بين دفتي المصحف کلام الله ونُسب هذا إلى كثير من الصحابة، وأما «القرآن» فيطلق أيضا بهاذين الإطلاقين ؛ فتارة يطلق ويراد به الصفة القديمة القائمة بذاته تعالى، وتارة يطلق ویراد به ما نتلوه ونكتبه ويسمعه بعضنا من بعض، لما ذكرنا في إطلاق كلام الله» فإنْ أُطلق بالمعنى الأول ـ وهو الصفة القديمة - فهو قديم ، وإنْ أُطلِق بالمعنى الثاني فهو مخلوق ، ومع أَنَّ كلا «القرآن» من و كلام الله» يطلق تارة على الصفة القديمة وتارة أخرى على المتلو الملفوظ به فإن الأكثر إطلاق لفظ «القرآن» على المتلو الملفوظ به، والأكثر إطلاق لفظ «كلام الله» على الصفة القديمة القائمة بذاته تعالى، ولهذا فسَّرَ الناظمُ القرآنَ بكلام الله في قوله: «ونَزِّه القرآن أي: كلامه حتى يكون أظهر في المُراد ، وقد ذكرنا - مع ذلك - أنَّ أئمة هذه الأمة تَحَرَّجُوا أَنْ يصفُوا القرآن بالحدوث مخافة أن يتبادر إلى أذهان العامة أنَّ الصفة القائمة بذاته حادثةٌ ؛ لأنَّ «القرآن» لفظ مشترك بين هذين المعنيين. وقدمنا أنَّ المعتزلة ذهبوا إلى أنَّ «القرآن» و «كلام الله حادثان ، وأنَّه لا معنى لهما إلا الكلام المُرتب المُسْتمِل على الأصوات والحروف ، ولهذا منعوا أن يتصف الله تعالى بالكلام،
Section on Divine Matters. Clarification that the speech of Allah (kalam Allah) is eternal (qadim) according to Ahl al-Sunna, contrary to the Mu'tazila. [The poet said:] 'And declare the Quran, i.e., His speech, free from temporal origination (huduth), and beware of His vengeance.' Then he began the issue of the creation of the Quran, saying: 'And declare the Quran free from temporal origination.' That is, it is incumbent upon you to declare it free from temporal origination. We have already presented in the discussion of the attribute of speech that the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a is that both 'Kalam Allah' and 'al-Quran' are used in two senses. As for 'Kalam Allah', it is used to mean the eternal attribute subsisting in His essence that is neither sound nor letter, and in this sense it is qadim. And it is used in another sense to mean the noble Quran that we recite with our tongues, hear from one another, and write in our mushafs. The reason for calling the recited Quran 'Kalam Allah' is that it indicates the eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, or that He, exalted, is the creator of it in this composition, with these words and this miraculous arrangement, and no one has any part in it. Allah, exalted, has called the noble Quran 'Kalam Allah' in this sense in His saying: 'And if any of the polytheists seeks your protection, then grant him protection so that he may hear the words (kalam) of Allah' [At-Tawba:6]. And A'isha al-Siddiqa called the written text in the mushafs 'Kalam Allah' in her saying: 'What is between the covers of the mushaf is the Kalam of Allah.' This has been attributed to many companions. As for 'al-Quran', it is also used in these two senses: sometimes it is used to mean the eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, and sometimes it is used to mean what we recite, write, and hear from one another, for the reason we mentioned in the usage of 'Kalam Allah'. If it is used in the first sense – which is the eternal attribute – then it is qadim, and if it is used in the second sense then it is created (makhluq). And although both 'al-Quran' and 'Kalam Allah' are used sometimes for the eternal attribute and other times for the recited and uttered words, the more common usage of the word 'al-Quran' is for the recited uttered words, and the more common usage of the word 'Kalam Allah' is for the eternal attribute subsisting in His essence. For this reason the poet interpreted the Quran as the speech of Allah in his saying: 'And declare the Quran, i.e., His speech, free from temporal origination' so that it is clearer in intended meaning. We have also mentioned that the imams of this nation refrained from describing the Quran as temporally originated (hadith) for fear that the minds of the common people would jump to the conclusion that the attribute subsisting in His essence is temporally originated, because 'al-Quran' is a homonym (lafz mushtarak) between these two meanings. And we have previously stated that the Mu'tazila held that 'al-Quran' and 'Kalam Allah' are both temporally originated, and that they have no meaning except the arranged speech consisting of sounds and letters, and for this reason they denied that Allah, exalted, is described with speech.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on divine matters. The poet's line commands to declare the Quran, meaning God's speech, free from temporal origination. The commentator explains the two senses of both 'Kalam Allah' and 'Quran'. First, he reaffirms that Ahl al-Sunna hold that 'Kalam Allah' can refer to the eternal attribute (ancient, not created) which is without sound or letters, and also to the recited Quran. The recited Quran is called 'Kalam Allah' because it indicates the eternal attribute or because God created it with that miraculous composition. He provides evidence: the Quranic verse where 'Kalam Allah' is used for the recited words (Sura Tawba), and the saying of A'isha that the written mushaf contains 'Kalam Allah'. Similarly, 'Quran' is used both ways. The more common usage of 'Quran' is for the recited words, and of 'Kalam Allah' is for the eternal attribute. Hence the poet clarifies by interpreting the Quran as God's speech. The imams avoided calling the Quran 'hadith' (temporally originated) due to ambiguity. Finally, the Mu'tazila are mentioned as those who deny the eternal attribute and consider the Quran as entirely created, consisting only of sounds and letters. This lesson establishes the Ash'ari position on the uncreatedness of the divine attribute of speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The imams believed the Quran is created because they avoided calling it 'hadith'.
Correction: They avoided calling it 'hadith' to prevent confusion, not because they believed it is created; they affirmed the eternal attribute.

Checkpoint
Why did Ahl al-Sunna imams avoid describing the Quran as 'hadith'?
A) They believed it is eternal
B) Because 'Quran' is a homonym that could be misunderstood
C) They agreed with the Mu'tazila
D) They had no reason

Key Takeaway
The imams avoided ambiguous language to protect the doctrine, while the Mu'tazila denied the eternal attribute entirely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about the Quran, do you ever fall into the same ambiguity as the Mu'tazila by reducing it to mere sounds and letters? How does the imams' caution challenge you to guard your words about Allah's attributes?
?
This passage shows the imams refrained from a term to protect the common believer. In your own life, are you careless with theological terms that might lead others to wrong beliefs? What specific term will you be more careful with starting today?
?
The Mu'tazila denied Allah's attribute of speech because they could only conceive of created speech. Are there any attributes of Allah that you struggle to affirm because they seem incompatible with your limited understanding? What step will you take to submit your intellect to revelation?
